1. BACKGROUND
A. INTRODUCTION
1.1   The Government of China (GOC) has requested Bank assistance in financing the
Wanjiazhai Water Transfer Project. The proposed project would reduce infrastructure
bottlenecks due to extreme water shortages, increase employment and thereby reduce
poverty, decrease human distress due to intermittent and limited water supplies, and
improve water quality in the river systems. Project objectives would be achieved by
diverting water from the Yellow River through tunnels and pipelines to industrial cities in
northern Shanxi.
1.2   Most of China's impressive economic growth since 1978 (9.5 percent per year)
has occurred in the industrial and commercial sectors, which are concentrated in urban
areas. As a result, urban area demand for water increased over 500 percent since 1978,
but available water supplies have been able to meet only 20 percent of that increase. By
1995, 400 of China's 514 largest cities suffered water shortages, with 100 of them,
located in the North China Plain (NCP) characterized as "desperately short" of water.
The economic loss due to these water shortages has been estimated at $12 billion per
year. The shortages also affect the domestic sector. Household consumption in NCP
cities averages between 20 and 50 liters per capita per day (l/c/d). The absolute
minimum for physiological and personal hygiene needs is considered to be 20 l/c/d. The
shortages have also had adverse environment impacts as local surface water has been
completely exhausted, and groundwater seriously overdrawn, leading to land subsidence
in some cities, and salt water intrusion in coastal areas. The reasons for these shortages
are straightforward. The NCP contains 40 percent of China's land and 45 percent of its
population, but only 10 percent of its water resources. The estimated annual water
deficits of 5 billion cubic meters (mi3) in the municipal and industrial sectors, and 35
billion m3 in agriculture amount to more than 70 percent of the average annual flow of the
Yellow River, the largest in North China. GOC has given top priority to alleviating these
water shortages. The longer-term "strategic" plan calls for massive water transfers from
the South, which has 80 percent of the country's water resources, to the North via the
Yellow River. The shorter-term "tactical" strategy includes medium-distance transfers
from the Yellow River to those cities most critically in need of water, including Qingdao
in Shandong Province, Beijing-Tianjin, and the industrial/mining cities of northern
Shanxi Province-the focus of this Project. These transfers will involve water previously
allocated by the State Council, and are consistent with national goals of economic and
social development, and maximum efficient use of Yellow River water.
1.3   Shanxi Province is located in northeastern China, bounded by the Yellow River to
the west and south. The current population is over 30 million, 23 percent of which is



-2-
urban and growing at an annual rate of 3.15 percent. Although agriculture is the principal
source of employment, the northern areas are highly industrialized. Northern Shanxi is
called China's "energy base" due to its vast coal deposits and electricity production,
which in 1993, was 41,700 million kilowatt-hours (kWh), or 7 percent of the national
total (25 percent of which was delivered to the North China grid). In the same year, coal
production reached 310 million tons, or 30 percent of the national total. In addition to
coal and power, Shanxi produces significant amounts of chemicals, metallurgical and
electronics goods. Overall economic growth has kept pace with the national level, with
an average real rate of 13.5 percent since 1990.
1.4   The province is relatively poor in water resources: It has the least water resources
per capita (470 mi3) in the Yellow River Basin, and is the second driest province (after
Ningxia) in terms of surface water resources. These are widely scattered among 15 river
basins, which are separated by mountains, making interbasin transfers difficult. River
flows exhibit a high degree of annual variation, although they are concentrated in the
summer. In most areas, groundwater has been tapped to its limits. Moreover, irrigated
agriculture has suffered most from water shortages: Although irrigation covers about 1 .1
million hectares (ha) or 30 percent of the cultivated area, only 50 percent have adequate
supplies, and some have received no water at all in recent years. To make matters worse,
in the northern industrial region, mining has severely affected water quality.
1.5   The most important economic impacts of the water shortage are felt in the
northern urban industrial areas in the cities of Taiyuan (the capital and largest city),
Datong (the second-largest city and mining center), and Pingshuo (a rapidly growing
mining/industrial region). In the first two, surface water supplies have virtually dried up,
and groundwater has been overextracted to a degree leading to land subsidence of over
3 meters (m) in many places. As a result, all sectors and population groups have suffered.
Farmers have sometimes resorted to irrigating with untreated industrial waste; miners are
often unable to bathe because of a lack of water; people living above the third floor have
no piped supply because pressures are too low; and industries have been forced to
extraordinary lengths and expense to conserve water. By 2000, water demands in these
three areas will outstrip supplies by a factor of 70 percent, with the gap growing much
wider afterwards.
1.6   The project would help alleviate these shortages through the construction of a
water transmission line from the Yellow River at Wanjiazhai Dam to Taiyuan, and later
to Datong and Pingshuo. Taiyuan would be served by a southern tunnel branching off a
general line into the Fen River. The other areas would be served by a northern branch,
passing through the proposed Daliang reservoir, beginning about 2005. The entire project
will deliver up to 320 million m3 per year in 2002, 900 million mi3 by 2010, and 1.2
billion mi3 by 2020. These volumes are within the allocations permitted by the Yellow
River allocation designated by the State Council, and ultimately will result in a 25 percent
increase in provincial water consumption.



-3 -
B. PROJECT ORIGIN AND OBJECTIVES
1.7  In 1964, the Yellow River Conservancy Commission (YRCC) conducted a study
for the Basin and key water control projects, including the Wanjiazhai Dam Project for
diverting water to Shanxi, were identified. In 1986, the Basin plan was updated and the
conclusions of the 1964 study were reconfirmed. The study concluded that Shanxi
Province's main source of water will be from the main branch of the Yellow River. The
provincial Government started its investigation of the Wanjiazhai Water Transfer Project
in 1984 and, with the help of the Ministry of Water Resources (MWR), completed the
studies and preliminary designs in 1992. The State Council approved the plan in 1993.
MWR simultaneously completed the feasibility study of the Wanjiazhai Dam and
preliminary designs in 1993, and construction for the dam was started in late 1994.
1.8  In March 1992, the Bank and YRCC carried out the Yellow River Investment
Planning Study (Report No. 11146-CHA, June 30, 1993), which sought to place
investment options in a consistent, basinwide economic framework. The study evaluated
options with respect to the development goals and physical and economic constraints.
Three interbasin transfers of water (Qingdao City transfer; Wanjiazhai transfer to
Taiyuan, Datong and Shuozhou; and the Hebei-Tianjin transfer) show a 25 percent
increase in net annual regional economic value added (Y 16.5 billion) due to more
rational usage of Yellow River water resources. The study concludes that these transfers
are some of the highest priorities for water use in the Basin.
1.9   The proposed project will therefore: (a) help meet the water needs of the northern
urban areas so as to alleviate structural bottlenecks and permit economic growth to
continue; (b) reduce the environmental impacts of land subsidence due to overextraction
of groundwater; (c) promote commercialization and corporatization of major water
resource project offices; and (d) upgrade the institutional and accounting capabilities of
the Shanxi Water Conservancy Bureau, and support the creation of the Upper Fen River
Basin Corporation for operational and water pricing functions.
C. BANK LENDING TO DEVELOP THE YELLOW RIVER BASIN
1.10  The Bank has helped finance 11 land and water resource development projects in
the Yellow River Basin. These projects focus on reducing poverty by raising agricultural
production through irrigation and drainage systems. Two of the projects involved a dam
on the Yellow River [Xiaolangdi Multipurpose Project (Loan 3727-CHA)] and the
associated resettlement [Xiaolangdi Resettlement Project (Credit 2605-CHA)]. These
projects are unique in that they were a multipurpose effort aimed at improving flood
control for about 103 million people, providing water supplies for 15 cities with water
shortages, and improving water for 2 million ha of poorly served irrigation areas. The
most recent efforts in the Basin include the Loess Plateau Project (Credit 2616-CHA),
which aims to improve watersheds to prevent further soil erosion, and the Shanxi Poverty
Alleviation Project (Credit 2834-CHA), which is designed to directly improve irrigation



- 4 -
areas, and provide water supply and terracing for poor areas in Western Shanxi along the
Yellow River.
D. LESSONS FROM PREVIOUS BANK PROJECTS
1.11  Although the Bank has assisted in 11 water resource and irrigation projects in the
Yellow River basin, the most important lessons for this project were drawn from the
Xiaolangdi Multipurpose Project and hydropower projects in other parts of China such as
the Shuikou Hydroelectric (Loan 2775-CHA) and Ertan Hydroelectric projects (Loan 3387-
CHA), which had large construction components. The lessons include: (a) the project
should be reviewed by a technical panel of international experts, as this improves the
design, economic assessment, and resettlement; (b) civil works procurement packaging and
joint ventures should be arranged between foreign and local contractors to obtain transfer of
technology and best international practices; (c) procurement for major civil works should
begin early so that bid awards will correspond with loan effectiveness and thus avoid delays
and cost overruns; (d) prequalification criteria should be stringent to ensure that all partners
of foreign and local joint ventures can meet their responsibilities; and (e) resettlement plans,
safety nets and budgets should be adequate. An action plan has been incorporated into the
project to ensure proper implementation of the resettlement component.
E. RATIONALE FOR BANK GROUP INVOLVEMENT
1.12  Consistent with the Country Assistance Strategy (CAS) for China presented to the
Board on March 18, 1997, the proposed project supports development efforts focused on:
China's poorer interior provinces; alleviating infrastructural constraints to continued
economic development; enterprise reform by commercializing operations and, inter alia,
introducing rational pricing. and fostering environmental protection for river basins. In
this regard, the project would ease infrastructural constraints in the water supply sector
that are now impeding Shanxi's economic and industrial growth, with consequent effects
on increasing employment and poverty alleviation. It would also relieve periodic
municipal water shortages; and improve the environment by reducing dependency on
groundwater resources and introducing modern water treatment facilities. By supporting
the creation of water resource corporations at various levels and commercializing those
operations by enabling them to trade water and set prices at commercial rates, the project
supports enterprise reform. And, for technology transfer, project tunnels-some of the
longest in the world -would be constructed by double-shielded boring machines, and
use of a master computer-controlled supervisory system would optimize pumping rates to
save energy costs.



-5 -
2. PROJECT AREAS
A. SHANXI PROVINCE
2.1   Shanxi Province is located in northeastern China between 1 10°3O' and 114045'
east longitude and 34°30' and 40025' north latitude.  The western and southern
boundaries are the Yellow River. Neighboring provinces are Nei Mongol (north), Hebei
(east), Henan (south) and Shaanxi (west). The northeastern corner of Shanxi is
approximately 150 km from Beijing.
2.2   The geography of the province is characterized by a series of mountain chains
with elevation ranging between 1,500 and 3,000 m and alluvial plains situated between
600 and 1,100 m. Fifteen river subbasins are scattered throughout: The largest two, Fen
and Qin, cover most of the southern two-thirds of the province and are major tributaries
of the Yellow River. Mean annual precipitation is 524 millimeters (mm). The northwest
is driest (less than 400 mm) while the southwest is relatively wet (600 to 800 mm);
however, 70 percent of precipitation occurs in June through August.
2.3   Population is just over 30 million, with 23 percent classified as urban and 77
percent as rural. It has grown at 1.25 percent annually since 1990, with urban population
2~~~~~~~~~~~~~~
increasing at 3.15 percent. With a surface area of 156,270 square kilometers (km ),
population density in the province is 192 per km2, or 45 percent higher than the national
average. Of the 6,941,000 urban population in 1993, 2,588,000 (37 percent) resided in
the project areas, 1,720,000 in Taiyuan alone. Agriculture, subject to the vagaries of the
weather (given insufficient water for irrigation), has exhibited modest real growth since
1990 (Table 2.1). But the industrial sector grew by an average annual rate of 18.5
percent. This performance is remarkable in that it occurred in an environment that
experiences severe water shortages.
TABLE 2.1: SHANXI INDUSTRIAL AND AGRICULTURAL OUTPUT
(Constant 1990 Yuan)
Year                       Agriculture      Industrial      Total Output
1990                         13,095           61,648           74,743
1991                         11,678           66,211           77,889
1992                         13,258           77,315           90,573
1993                         14,620           99,965          114,585
1994                         15,553          121,620          137,173
Growth rate 1990-94 (%)         4.4             18.5             16.4



-6 -
2.4   Within the nonagricultural sectors, coal production was the most significant,
achieving 26.9 percent of total industrial output value, followed by "other" (commerce,
services and transportation) at 23 percent, metallurgy (16.9 percent), manufacturing (15.3
percent), chemicals (11.7 percent) and electricity (6.7 percent). The last sector would
have had a much larger share had it not been for the existence of price controls, which
limits the value of its output to about 45 percent of it economic value. Clearly, Shanxi's
economy is not only highly industrialized and growing, but its industrial structure is
highly interdependent. Emerging bottlenecks, such as a water constraints that affect one
or more sectors, are beginning to have severe negative impacts throughout the provincial
economy. Since other parts of China rely heavily on Shanxi's products as critical inputs,
production problems would have a wide impact throughout the country.
2.5    Water Resources. Mean annual surface water runoff in the Shanxi Province
from  1956 through 1984 was 10.87 billion m3.  Annual exploitable groundwater
resources total 9.31 billion m3. However, 6.36 billion m3 of the groundwater and surface
water is double counted, implying that the actual average annual total water resource is
13.8 billion m . Water consumption in 1993 was 5.57 billion m3, of which 10 percent
was pumped from nonlocal sources. There are several reasons why this picture is not
nearly so optimistic as may first appear: (a) surface runoff varies greatly, and in one out
of four years, it is 6.35 billion m3 or less; (b) 7.84 billion m3 (71 percent) of surface
runoff occurs in subbasins that lay south or east of the northern demand centers and are
separated from them by long distances and/or mountain ranges; (c) runoff is concentrated
in a few summer months whereas municipal and industrial demand is year-long; and (d)
surface water flows have declined in key parts of the province in recent years. In the
Upper Fen Basin, which supplies the Taiyuan project area, surface water flows averaged
180 million m3/year before 1970, but only 47 million since 1988. This was partly due to
terracing and damming of large areas upstream of the city, and to lowered groundwater
tables (because of overpumping), which have led to greater seepage from streambeds.
Together, these factors imply that surface water resources are virtually exhausted where
and when the water is needed; the same is true of groundwater. Adjusting for double-
counted water, annual exploitable groundwater is estimated to be 2.69 billion m3. In
1992, 3.45 billion mi3 was consumed (given distribution losses, a much greater volume
was actually pumped).
2.6   Irrigation still uses more water than any other sector, with nearly 65 percent of
total consumption, but this percentage has been falling rapidly (see Table 2.2). Indeed,
the negligible growth in irrigation is evidence that water supplies have been virtually
exhausted everywhere in Shanxi as only about 80 percent of irrigable land can be treated,
and most crops receive only one-half the water recommended. If other water were
available, farmers would quickly use it to obtain the high marginal returns from
irrigation. In parts of the project area, farmers have been observed using untreated
wastewater, an unacceptable practice that the proposed project will help eliminate.



- 7 -
TABLE 2.2: WATER CONSUMPTION BY SECTOR, 1993
Sector           Consumption (million mi3)    Share (%)    Growth Rate (%)
Urban                      325                    5.7           6.2
Rural                      302                    5.3           3.7
Industry                 1,382                   24.1           2.9
Irrigation               3,715                   64.9           0.5
Total                  5,724                 100.0            1.5
2.7   Most surprising is that Shanxi industries have been able to maintain rapid growth
in output despite the slow growth in water supplies. In general, they have done so
through a combination of investments in water, increased recycling, and purchasing water
from local farmers, with some paying Y 1 0/m3 or more. This latter practice probably
results in water use being underreported. Such sales are illegal, and the project will seek
to promote free and efficient markets for water, since informal markets are beneficial to
3
both parties: Marginal returns from irrigation are seldom above Y 4/m , but they are
generally higher than Y 20/mr3 in industry. In 1980, Shanxi industries obtained Y 22/m3
of water; by 1990, the figure rose to 48 and exceeded 75 in 1994 (all in constant 1990
yuan). Industrial recycling of water reached 84 percent in 1993, and the province's major
industries already exceeded the 2000 China norms.
2.8   If groundwater use is kept at sustainable levels and nonlocal water resources are
excluded, Shanxi will have only about 4.2 billion m3 of reliable water supplies per year.
However, current consumption exceeds this figure by 30 percent and by 40 percent in the
project areas. Forecasts of water demand show how critical the future water balance may
become: Provincial Water Commission (PWC)-sponsored research concludes that
demand will reach 11.1 billion m3 by 2000, 13.0 by 2010, and 15.1 by 2020, despite rapid
improvements in the efficiency of water use.
B. THE PROJECT AREAS
2.9   The areas to be served by the project are those that have the highest water demand
and the most severe shortages, and the greatest potential for economic growth. While
they contain just 12.5 percent of the population, they produce 29 percent of provincial
industrial total output, over 40 percent of coal, electricity, metallurgical, and chemical
output, and contain only 7 percent of its water resources (see Table 2.3).



-8 -

2.10   Taiyuan. Located in north-central Shanxi along the Fen River and near abundant
raw materials and energy sources, Taiyuan contains one of the largest concentrations of
heavy industry in China. The area to be served includes the city, its suburbs and Gujiao,
the adjacent county. Per capita water resources are only 51 percent of the provincial
average, and 9 percent of the country's. Due to water shortages, 50 percent of its mines,
factories, and enterprises periodically close, which lowers productivity: For example,
Taiyuan Chemical Industry Company, the largest chemical and fertilizer producer in the
province, has a water demand of 3,200 m 3/hour but obtains only 1,080, which results in a
60 percent shortfall in output. For all Taiyuan industries, it has been estimated that 1993
industrial output (Y 18.3 billion) would have been Y21.1 billion (+15 percent) if the
water shortage had been alleviated, and only Y 12.8 billion (-30 percent) if groundwater
use had been held to sustainable levels.
2.11   Datong. Located in northeastern Shanxi near the Sangan River, Datong is the
center of China's largest coal mining area and is an important electricity supplier to the
North China grid and Beijing. The project area includes Datong city and suburbs, and
Datong and Xinrong counties. Per capita water resources are only 230 m3, 43 percent of
the provincial average, and 8.4 percent of China's. Datong Mining Bureau operates the
largest coal mine in China, and requires 163,000 m3/day for its operations and workers'
needs. It receives only 90,500. Since part of the raw coal cannot be washed, revenue
losses per year are nearly Y 800 million. Worker sanitation is a problem as pit drainage
water is often the only available water source for bathing. Miners carry their own potable
supplies to work with them.



- 9 -
2.12  Pingshuo. The Pingshuo project area includes Shuozhou City and suburbs, and
parts of Shanyin, Zuoyun, and Youyu counties. The current situation in Shuozhou City is
not yet as critical as in Taiyuan and Datong for two reasons. First, the Shentou Spring
has been tapped for much of the area's water supplies, and large volumes of water have
been "borrowed" from local irrigators. The borrowed water is to be returned when water
from the proposed project is available. The spring has shown indications of drying up.
From a long-term average of 7.15 m3/second, discharge dropped to 4.44 in 1993-all of
which was consumed.  Planned construction of new industries, mines, and other
enterprises has been postponed pending assurance of additional water supplies.
2.13  The rationale for the Project centers around the existence and impacts of the
shortages and the ability of the project to alleviate them so that economic growth can
continue, coal and electricity can continue to be exported to Beijing and other important
areas, and the ecological degradation can be halted. Water deficits began to appear in
these areas in the late 1980s and have been intensifying. Table 2.4 summarizes water
demand, supply and deficits for 1993. "Demand" here means actual 1993 consumption
increased to account for estimates of shortages in the urban, industrial, and agricultural
sectors.  The first "deficit" column is the shortage based on actual surface and
groundwater usage and amounts to 24 percent of demand. The last column takes into
account the serious overdraft of groundwater in Taiyuan and Datong and the borrowing of
water from irrigators in Pingshuo. If groundwater usage were held to safe (sustainable)
yields, and Pingshuo farmers allowed to retain their supplies, then the true deficit in 1993
was nearly 400 million m3, or almost 40 percent of demand.

2.14  The water shortages are felt by all sectors and consuming groups. Farmers around
the project areas get only about 25 percent of their needs, which explains why they
scavenge for wastewater. Urban households reportedly consume about 40 liters/day on
average (the norm for large Chinese cities is 160). But a recent survey showed that half
of surveyed people in Taiyuan get only 20 liters/day or less.

around the project cities.
