2.17  In 2000, water shortages are 41 percent of demand, 52 percent in 2010, and 61
percent in 2020. The economic impacts of the shortages will fall disproportionately on
the industrial sector since the allocation policies give priority to human consumption.
2.18  Table 2.7 illustrates the gravity of the future situation if the project is not
undertaken. The top block contains forecasts of industrial (nonagricultural) output on the
assumption of sufficient water that can only be met by the project. The second block
projects output if only sustainable supplies of local water are available assuming that
farmers' shares of available water will be kept constant at 1992 levels and all human
consumption demands are met. The bottom blocks show the forgone output if the project
is not undertaken. By 2020, 91 percent of potential output will be lost without the
project, which explains why the drastic steps of physically moving the industrial base out
of Shanxi or of moving Taiyuan to the bank of the Yellow River, have been mentioned in
Government circles as a distinct possibility.



- 12-
D. OPTIONS FOR SOLVING WATER SHORTAGE PROBLEM
2.19  Consideration of a major water supply project such as this must be predicated on
an exhaustive review of alternatives such as imposing higher water prices, conservation,
increased recycling and reuse, reallocations of existing water from other areas and uses,
and even the consequences of accepting drastically lower economic growth.
2.20  Prices. Historically, water prices in Shanxi, as elsewhere in China, have been
low. Farmers have been paying Y 0.1 5/m3 or less, and industries and households, about
Y I/mi3 (see paras. 6.14-6.15). These costs are well below the marginal economic returns
and estimated willingness-to-pay. Therefore, it might be argued that the "shortage" is in
fact artificial, and that a more rational set of prices would clear the water markets and
render the Project unnecessary. Ordinarily, higher prices would have two effects: an
incentive to conserve water, and an incentive to trade water from lower-productivity uses
to higher. As noted below, conservation efforts have probably reached a technical
maximum; measures of water use efficiency have increased sharply over the past decade,
and are now leveling off. If further substantial gains were possible, then one would not
observe idle and underused factories and mines. In the household sector, about half of
Taiyuan's residents are consuming one fourth of the national average, and many, only
one eighth. It is inconceivable that higher prices would uncover significant "waste" of
water. "Grassroots" water markets have appeared in some areas. Transactions have been
observed whereby farmers sell water to industries at prices ranging from Y 5 to Y 1 0/m3.
Because these transactions are voluntary and economically motivated, they undoubtedly
favor both parties. However, agricultural water is seldom available where and when
industries need it, and the scope for expanding these markets is therefore small. In fact, if
all agricultural water in the Project area could be sold to industries and households, the
estimated water deficit in 2001 would decline by only 24 percent.
2.21   The supply of water to the Project areas is virtually inelastic. This is evidenced
by the fact that economic growth has not led to higher consumption and neither
increasing shortages nor price increases have led to decreased consumption, either within
the Project areas, or in the entire Province. Higher prices therefore would serve only to
transfer revenues from consumers to water supply entities. Heretofore, supply costs have
been low, largely associated with costs of groundwater pumping and O&M cost of the
surface water storage and distribution system (see para. 5.11). Costs of measures to
augment the supply of water have not been considered, because such sources effectively
do not exist except for the project. The project now provides an estimate of the marginal
costs of water supply, and these costs are substantially above the costs of supply existing
water and the prices currently paid. If the "new"V water is to be used efficiently, and the
Project is to be financially viable, then it is clear that prices must be increased. The
project thus provides the backdrop and the rationale for instituting a more rational pricing
system. Associated with the project will be a pricing reform policy that raises water
charges to approximately the marginal economic cost of new water, yet ensures that most
water consumers will continue to have the ability to pay for water (see para. 5.18 and
Table 5.1). The new prices will apply to all water consumed in the Project area to ensure



- 13 -
that substitution of "old" water for "new" is not encouraged. Water costs are a small
component of household and enterprise budgets, and will remain within manageable
limits even under the new price structure.
2.22  Conservation. Conservation means reducing waste and curtailing water supplies
to marginal uses. In northern Shanxi, there is virtually no waste. Water utilization ratios
are probably the highest in China. While there are water-using activities that exhibit low
returns (e.g., agriculture), these cannot be termed "marginal" given the necessity to
maintain  subsistence and incomes for millions of poor rural people.   Water
recycling/reuse, which now stands at 85 percent, has produced remarkable gains in the
productivity of water used by industries and mines. One study has shown that most
investment in Shanxi industries over the last decade has gone toward conserving water
and the marginal gains to such investment are approaching zero.
2.23  Reallocation. In 1993, irrigation accounted for 40 million m3 (21.4 percent of
total consumption) in Datong and 83 million m3 (19 percent) in Taiyuan. Irrigation
consumption as a percentage of total consumption has been declining as water has been
diverted to municipal and industrial (M&I) uses or sold by farmers who have water
rights.
2.24  However, not all of the current irrigation supplies could be diverted to M&I uses
in the project areas. Irrigation demands are seasonal, peaking in the spring, whereas M&I
demands are relatively constant throughout the year. The larger irrigated areas are not
located near the cities so that measurable reallocation would require investments in both
surface water storage and conveyance. Those irrigated areas located near the project
cities supply important and valuable produce to those cities. These products would be
difficult and costly to procure elsewhere. However, the most important justification for
not reallocating from irrigation has to do with poverty alleviation. Most of Shanxi's poor
are rural and most rural residents rely on agriculture for the bulk of their incomes.
Without irrigation, agriculture in Shanxi is a precarious undertaking due to low and
unpredictable rainfall. Irrigated yields are double those of rainfed land and could be
much higher were irrigation water available in ample supplies. Agricultural incomes are
quadrupled when crops are irrigated. In 1994, farm wages were only 33 percent of wages
in manufacturing. Thus, any further reallocation of water from farmers would have
serious negative income effects on farmers who are already the poorest population
segment.
2.25  Without the proposed project, slower economic growth is a virtual certainty and in
the project areas, a general economic decline would be highly probable. With current
water shortages in the project areas amounting to 40 percent of estimated demand, and
growing rapidly, and with both ground and surface water supplies declining, failure to
complete the project would engender economic, social, and environmental calamity.
Projections using the Provincial Planning Model (PPM) show that if the project areas'
urban residents are to be given supplies equaling the norms for China and if farmers'
water allocations are to be preserved at current levels, industrial and mining activities



- 14 -
would decline rapidly and virtually cease after the year 2010. The annual loss in
industrial output would be Y 32 billion in 2000 and Y 66 billion in 2010. The impact
would be felt throughout China, given the heavy reliance on northern Shanxi's coal,
heavy industry and chemical output. Hundreds of thousands of skilled workers would
need to be relocated and vast quantities of mineral resources would go unexploited. It is
not surprising that Chinese planners have mentioned the possibility that Taiyuan and
Datong would need to relocated (at incalculable cost) if project water is not forthcoming.
E. ALTERNATIVES TO WANJIAZHAI AS WATER SOURCE
2.26  If economic growth in the project areas is to continue and the urban population is
to receive adequate supplies of water, additional sources of water must be found.
Potential sources include (a) groundwater, (b) local surface water, (c) imported surface
water, and (d) reallocation of existing water from other uses (irrigation).
2.27  Groundwater. In Datong and Taiyuan, there is strong evidence that groundwater
is already overexploited and has been since the late 1980s. The evidence comes from
both a comparison of extraction with estimates of sustainable yields and from
observations of declining water tables and increasing incidence of land subsidence. In
1993, groundwater overdraft in Datong was 40 percent higher than sustainable yield, and
in Taiyuan, 15 percent. Groundwater supplied 86.7 percent of total consumption in
Datong in that year, and 80.4 percent in Taiyuan. Except in the Shuozhou city center
area, groundwater extraction in the Pingshuo project area has not reached crisis levels.
This area has relied heavily on Shentou Spring, the average discharge rate of which had
dropped nearly 40 percent by 1993. The question is not whether increased extraction of
groundwater can be tolerated but whether current levels can be sustained to the year 2000
when the project water is scheduled to become available.
2.28  Local Surface Water. Taiyuan City is situated near the upper end of the Upper
Fen Basin, an enclosed hydrological entity that relies almost entirely on snowmelt and far
upper reach runoff for the river flow. There is negligible offtake above Taiyuan City and
its northern suburbs, but virtually unlimited demand for water in and around Taiyuan and
downstream for irrigation. The utilization rate has been as high as 74 percent in recent
years, which is probably the highest in the Yellow River Basin. This level cannot be
increased, given the seasonality of flows and high sediment content during flood periods.
This level of utilization has resulted in the riverbed often running dry much of the year,
and a dramatic decline in the irrigation supplies to downstream farmers. Furthermore,
there is strong evidence that recorded Fen river flows have been declining at statistically
significant rates. This has been caused by extensive terracing (to conserve water and
lessen erosion) upstream, and by the decline in groundwater tables, which increases
losses from seepage. Datong is located near the middle of the Sanganhe Basin, and
utilizes surface water from the Sanganhe River as well as small volumes from the Yuhe
River which originates in Nei Mongol Province. Sanganhe resources available to Datong
3
are again small relative to projected demands (36 million m at P75 vs. 352 million m
projected 2000 demand), and also are seen to be declining for the same reasons applying



- 15 -
to the Upper Fen. In recent years, the Yuhe supplied about 74 percent of Datong's total
surface water, and the flow of this river is declining sharply as Nei Mongol develops uses
for the water for its own internal economic expansion. Downstream of Datong lies the
Cetian Reservoir which, by agreement, permits 31 million m3/year to be pumped back to
Datong. The remainder of Cetian's storage goes for consumption in Hebei and Beijing
City, which are also experiencing chronic water shortages.
2.29  Imported Surface Water. The alternative sources for importing surface water
are either from adjacent subbasins within Shanxi or from the Yellow River. Shanxi is
divided into 15 subbasins or catchment areas. Conceivably, Taiyuan could receive water
diverted from the Northwest or Hotuohe basins, and Pingshuo and Datong could receive
water from the Honghe and Yanghe basins, respectively. Two factors make these options
virtually impossible. First, water shortages are prevalent throughout northern Shanxi and
are intensifying everywhere. Only in the west are excess surface flows projected, and
these occur mostly in flood season, draining directly into the Yellow River, at points
remote from the project area water demand centers. Second, the division of Shanxi into
subbasins follows the pattern of intervening mountain ranges, which would need to be
crossed if water were to be diverted. These ranges are as high as 3,000 m and would
involve daunting technical and economic challenges.
2.30 The above discussion implies that only importation of water from the Yellow
River by a project such as the proposed Wanjiazhai can meet the projected water
demands. Alternatives cannot supply the volumes required, or would pose even greater
difficulties and higher costs than the proposed project. However, is Wanjiazhai the best
location on the Yellow River for the diversions? It is probably the only location that is
feasible. Above Wanjiazhai the Yellow River turns westward, requiring longer and more
costly diversion routes, and one that encroaches on Nei Mongol. Below Wanjiazhai, the
Loess Plateau begins with its associated runoff with high silt concentrations, which would
require prohibitively high treatment costs.
F. PROJECT PHASING
2.31   The economic and social effect of the water shortage situation is sufficiently
severe in each of the described project areas to warrant the immediate attention to
remedies such as the project. However, a project of the scope and cost of the full project
(para. 1.6) is not feasible given the limited financial means of the Province, and the
capacities of local contractors. Thus, the Project needs to be phased, beginning with the
General Transmission Line and either the North Line (to Datong and Pingshuo) or the
South (to Taiyuan and its environs). The second alternative has been selected for two
reasons. First, Taiyuan has a much larger urban population that would benefit from the
increased household water supplies, and a slightly greater industrial capacity suffering
from shortages than do the northern areas. Second, there is a much larger irrigation area
around and downstream from Taiyuan that could make immediate use of treated
wastewater to increase farm output and agricultural incomes. Thus Phase I of the project
will first serve Taiyuan beginning about 2001, followed by the northern areas in Phase II.



- 16-
3. THE PROJECT
A. PROJECT OBJECTIVES
3.1   The project is part of a phased development program to expand the water supply
and improve the land and water environment in northern Shanxi province. Specific
objectives include:
(a)   Reducing the severe infrastructural bottlenecks in water supply that
impede economic growth;
(b)   Increasing employment and incomes by eliminating the paramount
constraint to development;
(c)   Reducing the distress of the urban population caused by insufficient and
intermittent water supplies, which are barely enough to meet their basic
needs;
(d)   Improving the environment by ending the overextraction of groundwater
and the subsequent drying of surface streams and land subsidence, and by
treating all waste, so as to reduce water pollution downstream;
(e)   Creating a system to rationalize water use, by sector, and reducing water
shortages by managing demand; and
(f)   Promoting integrated management and financial independence for water
institutions.
B. PROJECT DESCRIPTION
3.2   The project would provide the physical works needed to transfer water from the
Yellow River through a system of tunnels, aqueduct pipelines and reservoirs. It would
also support policy and institutional reform for economic water pricing and water
marketing. The project would include the following components:
(a)   Yellow River Diversion Project Corporation (YRDPC)
(i)    Work on the general main tunnel (GMT) would be completed. This
would involve civil works for tunnels no. 1 to 3, 6 to 11, and all
connecting structures such as aqueducts and siphons, power
transmission lines (220 kV, 110 kV, 35 kV) and transformer stations
as well as preparatory work for access roads, adits and water supply
for the south main tunnels (SMT);



- 17 -
(ii)    Civil works for five pumping stations (two underground and three
above ground) and for pressure tunnels no. 4 and 5 for the GMT;
(iii)    Construction of all seven tunnels (totaling 98 km), four aqueducts
and the bifurcation structure for the SMT;
(iv)    Supply and installation of equipment for all pumping stations on the
GMT and SMT. The supply would include all pumps (6.0-6.45
m3/second units), motors (10 MW to 12 MW units), controls for the
motors, communications, valves, gates and steel works;
(v)    Construction of connecting works from the end of the SMT to
Taiyuan that will include a headworks at Fen-1, a 41 km twin
pipeline (2.2-2.4 m diameter each) and a 17.1 km tunnel to the
Huyang treatment plant outside of Taiyuan;
(vi)    Resettlement/compensation  for all people displaced  by  the
aqueducts;
(vii)   Environmental management techniques to offset the negative effects
at the construction sites or from any part of the project;
(viii)    Consultant services to supervise construction, create a board to
review disputes, establish a panel of experts for the design and
construction of underground works and training programs, and
organize an environment/resettlement panel.
(b)   Water Resources Institutional Reform
(i)    Reform of all major water resource institutions will be undertaken
within the Yellow River water delivery area: YRDPC, Fen-1, Fen-2,
Fenhe Irrigation Bureau, Taiyuan Water Company and Taiyuan
Sewerage Bureau. The YRDPC, Fen-1, Fen-2, Fenhe Irrigation
Bureau and the Taiyuan Groundwater Institution and Rural
Groundwater Institution will be integrated into a Upper Fen River
Basin Corporation (UFRBC). This corporation will be responsible
to license all water sold (surface or groundwater) to all users in the
Upper Fen Basin. UFRBC will issue permits for the sale of water
and discharge of water and waste water. To ensure financial
autonomy, water pricing would be liberalized to obtain the best
arrangements within the various sectors, and trading between
sectors. The Taiyuan City, the Fen Bureau Irrigators, Gujao City,
and industries will form the users of water and UFRBC will issue
permits for use for different sources of water. Prices will be based
on the quality of water, time when water is needed, the return flows
and the quality of return flows. The users of the water, Taiyuan City



- 18-
Water Company, Sewerage Company, and Irrigators will also be
reformed so that they will also be financially autonomous.
(c)   Water Pollution Control and Industrial Waste Management
(i)    An environmental master plan for Taiyuan, which would involve
studies on a wastewater permit system, pollution enforcement, and a
phased pollution prevention program;
(ii)    An in-plant wastewater monitoring system and effective pollution
permit system, with charges high enough to deter industries from
discharging waste above the standards;
(iii)    Training and upgrading Environmental Protection Bureau (EPB)
staff skills to prevent pollution, establish and operate pollution
permit systems and a data base for monitoring water and wastewater
quality;
(iv)    A fund to introduce cleaner technology in the seven main polluting
industries (steel, coal, coke, pharmaceuticals, paper, fertilizer and
chemicals).
3.3   Complementary works for Taiyuan would be needed for receiving, treating and
distributing water; they will be carried out by the Taiyuan Municipal Water Company
(TMWC). These works are not under the project but will be implemented in parallel by
TMWC. Also, the city will need to make added investments to ensure that wastewater
generated from the increased water supply will be intercepted, collected and treated. This
will prevent water quality in the Fen River from deteriorating as it passes through the area.
The works that will be financed by a combination of local and Japanese Government
sources (para. 3.41) will involve the following components:
(a)   Water supply for Taiyuan
(i)    A new surface water treatment plant at Huyang with an initial
capacity of 0.9 million m3 per day in 2003, to be increased to 1.4
million m3 per day by 2005 and to 1.76 million m3 per day by 2010;
(ii)    A new network of water transmission lines of 170 km for the City,
with pipe diameters of 300 to 2,000 mm and a special water line of
13 km for the power station;
(iii)    Two booster stations;
(iv)    Consultant services for detailed design and engineering;
(v)    Training for water company staff in finance, operations and
maintenance management.



- 19 -
(b)   Wastewater Collection and Treatment
(i)   Eight new wastewater treatment plants for eight new areas (Hexi,
Xiaodian, Xishan, and Shanglan, Luocheng, Nanxiawen, Jinei and
Power Plant No. 1);
(ii)    Rehabilitating five existing wastewater treatment plants (Yang Jiabu,
Ying Jiabu, Beijiao, Zhaozhuang, and Nanyan);
(iii)   Laying the first phase of 170 km of interceptors and secondary and
tertiary sewers by 2002 (ultimately 660 km);
(iv)    Consultant services for master plan and feasibility studies and first-
phase design;
(v)    Training for municipal commission staff and for institutional
development (to integrate wastewater collection and treatment).
C. PROJECT MONITORING
3.4   Project impact would be monitored with respect to the project objectives (POs),
the key indicators of which are listed below:
(a)   For PO(a), it is planned to monitor the economic output of different
sectors of industry and water deliveries;
(b)   For PO(b), employment growth and income will be monitored;
(c)   For PO(c), it is planned to monitor the water deliveries to households;
(d)   For PO(d), it is planned to monitor the water supplied from groundwater to
all sectors, to observe whether these exceed the safe yield levels of
extraction and to monitor the water quality in the Fen River and tributaries
around and below the city limits;
(e)   For PO(e), it is planned to monitor the industrial and urban water
deliveries to each sector and their economic outputs and employment; and
(f)   For PO(f), it is planned to monitor the progress of pricing reform and the
corporatization of water suppliers in the Upper Fen River Basin and their
integration into the Upper Fen River Basin Corporation.
D. PROJECT FORMULATION
3.5   The Wanjiazhai Dam, identified in the Yellow River Master Plan as the most
economic site, would be the main diversion point from the Yellow River. The dam is not
under the project, but is being constructed by MWR. It is located below the bend



- 20 -
southward (after Hekouzen) where the catchment area is 394,813 km2 (52 percent of the
river basin) and river flows average about 19.7 billion m 3/year. The lowest flow recorded
at this point was 11 billion m3/year in 1928. Since the diversion requires only 1.2 billion
m3/year, there will always be sufficient water for this purpose.
3.6   The dam, of the concrete gravity type, is designed to preserve the long-term
storage of 445 million m3 of water, which will be drawn through two side sluices to the
pumping stations at an elevation of 948 m. The reservoir's normal operating level will be
977 m; its lowest level, 952 m, occurs during silt flushing. The intake sluice sills for the
tunnels skim the water from the reservoir's upper section so that suspended sediment is
minimized and concentration levels are kept to relatively low levels of 1 to 1.3 kg/m3. In
winter, intake sills would be heated to prevent ice from closing the intake. Pumping from
the reservoir would occur only from October to July, because suspended silt
concentrations can exceed 5 kg/m3 in August and September. Safety aspects of the design
were reviewed by consultants appointed by MWR and additional specialists were
appointed to review these aspects during design and construction. MWR has also
installed exterior and interior equipment on the dam to monitor any movements or
deflections in the dam. At negotiations, assurances were obtainedfrom the Borrower that
it will prepare, by December 31, 1998, a satisfactory maintenance program and an
emergency preparedness plan for the dam. Also, the Borrower will employ independent
experts with qualifications, experience and terms of reference acceptable to the Bank to
inspect the dam, associated works and structures regularly starting October 31, 1998,
according to sound engineering practices, to determine if any of the structural conditions,
maintenance or operating methods could endanger safety (see Annex 17). Inspections
would be conducted annually for the first five years of operations and every three years
thereafter.
3.7    MWR, through the Tianjin Design Institute, developed a detailed environmental
impact assessment (EIA) for the dam and program for resettlement. The EIA covered
subjects such as water temperature and quality, reservoir sedimentation, ice regimes and
floods, reservoir regulation, environmental geology, fisheries, impacts of construction
sites, protection measures around resettlement areas and the environmental monitoring
system.   It indicated that dam  construction should not cause any  significant
environmental degradation, provided the dam is operated as planned, which would
include incorporating the Environmental Management Plans specified in the EIA, and
provisions for resettlement. Also, it concluded that resettlement is the project's only
major negative impact, which will be completely offset by the ongoing resettlement plan.
Altogether, 664 residents are affected at the dam site and 6,047 in the reservoir area. All
664 at the dam site have been resettled, the new areas developed and industries opened to
ensure that the income of those resettled is maintained. In fact, surveys indicate that most
incomes have increased by 35 percent. As for the 6,047 residents in the reservoir area,
the investment planned totals Y 510 million, or about Y 84,000/person, which far exceeds
any previous resettlement expenditures in China. Nevertheless, during negotiations,
assurances were obtained that the Borrower will (a) complete the resettlement for the
Wanjiazhai Dam according to a resettlement action plan that has been found acceptable to



- 21 -
the Bank, (b) employ by February 15, 1998 an independent monitoring institution with
qualifications, experience and terms of reference acceptable to the Bank to evaluate
semiannually the socioeconomic progress of those resettled from the dam site; and
(c) provide the Bank with a report of each evaluation along with proposals for meeting the
resettlement plan targets, if they have not been achieved. It is planned that the monitoring
institution will be the Yellow River Conservancy Commission Resettlement Office.
3.8   MWR is in charge of project implementation. Dam construction began in March
1994 and the river was closed in November 1995. The first turbine should be installed in
November 1998 and the dam completed sufficiently to start diverting water from the
Yellow River.
3.9   Alignment and Routing. The issues of tunnel alignment and routing the water
were studied for three decades. The tunnel and water route are shown in Map IBRD
28136, and a schematic is shown in Figure 1, Annex 1. The route was selected based on
the geology that provides the strongest rock relatively free of groundwater, the shortest
distance for the transfer, the lowest cost and pumping requirements. Based on these
criteria, the best alignment for the general main tunnel (GMT) moves eastward from the
Wanjiazhai Dam from an elevation of 957 m, through a mountain range at 1,582 m, and
follows the Pinguan River, which flows west, for about 44 km to Xiatuzhai Village,
where the bifurcation structure is located for the north main tunnel (NMT) and south
main tunnel (SMT). The alignment for the SMT starts from Xiatuzhai and passes through
the Guancen Mountain (2,800 m high) to the Luya Shan Range, for 102 km into the Fen
River Basin. This is the shortest route to the Fen River. From the connection tunnel to
the Fen, the water travels via the upper Fen for 66 km and enters an existing reservoir,
Fen-1. Here the water is transported by way of twin pipelines for 40.2 km and tunnels for
16.8 km. The route was selected because it offered the lowest-cost construction, least
possibility of pollution, lowest loss of water and minimal environmental impact.
3.10  Five pumping stations would move water through these tunnels: three are on the
GMT and two on the SMT. Four have pump lifts of 142 m and one is 80 m. These lifts
were designed to reduce tangential velocities in the impellers so that sand erosion will be
reduced. The station locations were also chosen to maximize pumping efficiency.
3.11  The GMT is in competent mass limestone and dolomite (rock class 1-3) most of
the way (81 percent); only small sections, in the tunnel openings, are in shale, sandstone
and soils. The SMT is also mainly (88 percent) in competent dolomites and limestone
(rock class 1-3) and some small sections are in soils, red clays, sandstone and coal. Most
of the tunnel construction will be by tunnel boring machine (TBM) with double shields.
The tunnel lining will be placed automatically by the TBM. The lining will be precast
reinforced concrete segments made on-site in a segment production factory. Use of
TBMs for construction for long tunnels has been found to be very economical and give
good construction quality. Construction in the less-competent rock would be undertaken
manually, with drill and blast (D/B) techniques. The alignment of both tunnels was



- 22 -
optimized to ensure that geological conditions will be good and construction can be fully
mechanized, thus achieving high quality at low cost.
3.12  Reservoirs. The water transfer system has two main regulating reservoirs. The
Shentongshui Reservoir, with a capacity 150,000 m3, is located between the GMT's
tunnels 5 and 6. It will regulate the inflow of water from the Wanjiazhai Reservoir and
also regulate water flowing out to the rest of the tunnels in the GMT. The reservoir will
be the main dynamic regulator in the entire GMT and SMT tunnel system. Additional
dynamic regulation will be achieved through variable speed motors in the pumping
stations. The only other reservoir will be the Fen-1, located in the Fen Basin about 57 km
from Taiyuan. The Fen Dam was built in the 1960s with a capacity of 700 million m3
3                                      3
and an active storage of 385 million m . The reservoir will provide 120 million m
storage for the period when pumping is not possible from the Wanjiazhai Reservoir
(because of suspended silt loads of over 5 kg/m3). (See Annex 1, Figure 1.1.)
3.13  At negotiations, assurances were obtained from Shanxi Province that it will
continue its program of dam maintenance for Fen-] dam; also that the Province and
independent experts with qualifications, experience and terms of reference acceptable to the
Bank would, not later than February 15, 1998, inspect the dam-associated works and
structures, according to sound engineering practices. This would be done to determine if
any of the structures, maintenance or operating methods are deficient and may compromise
their safety. Shanxi will biannually submit the experts' reports to the Bank starting with
the first report by November 15, 1998.
E. PROJECT WORKS
3.14 General Main Tunnel. The GMT has 11 tunnels, five aqueducts, three pumping
stations (GM1, GM2 and GM3), and one underground regulating reservoir. The tunnels
and aqueducts are designed for a flow capacity of 48 m3/second and to pump 1,200
million m3/year of water to Datong, Pingshuo and Taiyuan. The first phase of the project
will have a capacity to pump 640 million m3/year (25.8 m3/second) to Taiyuan; and,
although the tunnels and aqueducts will be completed at this capacity, the pump stations
will initially have a reduced number of units (because of lower demand in 2002) to pump
320 million m3/year (12.5 m3/second), reaching 640 million m3/year by 2010.
3.15  Tunnels 1 to 5 are pressurized while all others are free-flowing. Tunnels 1 to 8
are circular, with cross-sections of 5.6 m for tunnels 3 to 5, and 5.46 m for tunnels 6 to 8.
Tunnels 9 to 11 have a city-gate shape, 5.0 m wide and 5.36 m high. The 11 tunnels vary
in length: tunnels 8 and 11 are 10 to 12 km, tunnels 6 and 9 are 6 to 7.5 km, and tunnels
1, 2, 3, 4, 5 and 7 vary from 0.21 km to 2.67 km. The five aqueducts connect the tunnels.
3.16  The Shentongshui Reservoir is located after the two pump stations, between
tunnels 5 and 6. Situated at the end of tunnel 5 and at the start of tunnel 6, it is an
underground cavern 12 m wide by 6 m high and 2,900 m long and has storage of
151,000 m3 and control gates to regulate the flows.



- 23 -
3.17  There are two underground pumping stations to pump water from the Wanjiazhai
Reservoir. Pump station 1 is located between tunnels 3 and 4 and pump station 2 is
located between tunnels 4 and 5. Each of the pump stations will be designed to have an
ultimate pumping capacity of 10 units of 6.45 m3/second for each unit. The static lift for
each unit will be 142 m head. Each chamber will be excavated some 130 m long, 18 m
wide and 35 m high. A third pumping station is located between tunnels 10 and 11 and
will be above ground. This pump station will also have 10 pumps of 6.45 m3/second.
3.18  Preparatory work for GMT consists of construction of access roads (105 km),
power supply (220 kV and 110 kV lines), a communications system, and access adits for
all tunnels. Most of this work has been completed. In addition, to get an early start,
YRDPC awarded tunnels 6, 7, and 8 to an international contractor in 1994. Similarly,
contracts for tunnels 1, 2, 9, 10, and 11 were already awarded to national contractors that
were selected through national competitive procedures. Bank missions have reviewed
these works, which are being funded by YRDPC, and the quality of design and
construction is satisfactory. Bank funding for the GMT will focus on tunnels 3, 4, and 5
and the pumping stations.
3.19 South Main Line. The SMT consists of seven free-flow tunnels (98 kin), two
buried pipes, two aqueducts, three culverts and two pumping stations. The discharge
capacity of these facilities is 25.8 m3/second. Tunnels 1 to 3 will have a horseshoe-
shaped cross-section, with a top diameter of 4.24 m, an invert width also of 4.24 m, and
water depth will be 3.08 m. As tunnels 4 to 7 will be constructed with boring machines
(TBM), the tunnels' shape will be circular with a diameter of 4.2 m. Tunnel lengths, as
indicated in Table 3.1, particularly for tunnels 5 (26.4 km) and 7 (42.6 km), are among
the longest in the world.
TABLE3.1: TUNNELLENGTHS
(km)
TunnelNo.       1      2       3       4       5      6       7     Total
Length        0.84    1.87    4.16    6.93   26.44   14.63   42.63   98.00
3.20  The SMT will have two pumping stations at ground level, 81.3 m long, 15.4 m
wide and 27.7 m high. Pumping station 1 is located between tunnels 1 and 2 and
pumping station 2 is located between tunnels 2 and 3. Each will have six units of pumps
with a unit capacity of 6.45 m3/second and lift capacity of 142 m.
3.21  Connecting Works. The connecting works link Taiyuan's main water treatment
plant to the Fen-1 reservoir, a distance of about 57 km. The works consist of an intake
tower in the reservoir and a pipeline that follows the Fen riverbed most of the way to the
city. The dam was constructed in 1961 for flood control, irrigation and water supply; it



- 24 -
has been upgraded several times with new outlet tunnels to sluice silt through and
stabilize the active storage. Dam safety inspections have also been carried out by the
Shanxi Water Conservancy Bureau.
3.22  The pipelines are six twin lengths, for a total of 40.2 km, and seven tunnel
sections of 16.7 km. The pipe-lengths vary from 10.8 km to 0.11 km and the diameters
are 2.2 m to 2.5 m. The seven tunnels vary from 0.1 km to 13.122 km and the diameters
vary from 2.2 m to 2.5 m. Prefabricated reinforced concrete pipes will be used, as they
can withstand a pressure of 10 bars at the joints. The pipeline will have 7 valve
chambers, 76 inspection wells, 134 ventilation air valves, 6 pressure reduction valves, 2
energy dissipation pools, 17 regulator valves and 2 butterfly valves. The tunnels will be
constructed using drill and blast techniques. (See Annex 1, Figures 1.2 and 1.3.)
3.23  Plant and Equipment. The main equipment to be procured will be pumps,
motors, controls and communications items. The required flow between 2001 to 2010 is
12.9 m3/second and after 2010, it will be 25.9 m3/second. For the first stage (in 2001),
there will be 15 units of vertical centrifugal pumps (three pumps per station). Each
pump's unit capacity will be 6.45 m3/second, with a static lift capacity of 142 m. Pump
power of 10.8 MW will be used for the first two pumping stations on the GMT and two
stations on the SMT (a total of 12 units). For the third pumping station, unit capacity will
be 6.45 m3/second, but the static lift capacity will be 80 m and the pump power will be
6.3 MW. The pump inlet, guide vanes and outlet areas will be coated with special
hardened materials and impellers will be made of hardened steel to resist sand erosion.
Fifteen synchronous vertical shaft electric motors will be procured: three units with a
capacity of 6.75 MW and 12 units with a rated capacity of 12.5 MW. Except for the
GMT's, the first two stations, which will use fixed-speed motors (six units), all other
motors will be variable-speed drives (nine units). The starting facilities for the fixed-
speed motor units will be variable-frequency starters (6 units) and for the variable-speed
motors will be variable-speed converters (9 units).
3.24  Control Systems. One central dispatching and control station and 13 local
control stations will be built to coordinate and integrate the operations of the entire
diversion system and to enhance reliability and safety. Control and automation systems
will be procured for flow control, economic flow dispatching, normal starting and
stopping, automatic operation and coordination for emergency events, and operation
monitoring. Of the three levels of controls, the first level is at the local pump unit and the
control consists of starting and stopping a pump unit, automatic actions in the event of an
emergency and data collection for levels two and three. The second level is at the pump
station for managing pump units, gate and valve control. At this level, data will be
collected to be transmitted to level three and information will be displayed at local
centers; there will also be some remote control functions. A third level will have a
supervisory control and data acquisition system (SCADA). It will manage by remote
control the whole hydraulic network and equipment, and emergency shutdown of pumps.
There will also be global monitoring of all equipment and storage of all historical



- 25 -
information. In addition, there will be programs to control operations of all pumps and
motors to maximize the system's efficiency.
3.25  Water Sector Institutional Reform.  This component affects several water
resource institutions in the area where water from the Yellow River will be transferred
under one corporation, the Upper Fen River Basin Corporation (UFRBC) for the supply
of water. The users of water will be the cities, industries, irrigators, etc. The users will
be issued permits to use water by UFRBC.
3.26 These institutions include YRDPC, the Fen River Dam I Bureau, Fen River Dam
II Bureau, and Fen River Irrigation Bureau. UFRBC will be responsible for all water
permits (surface or groundwater). Also, water user charges are so low that the institutions
receive subsidies, and are not financially autonomous.
3.27  The reform will have two phases. In the first (from 1997 to 2000) all suppliers of
water, YRDPC, Fen-1, Fen-2, Groundwater Institutions, Fen Irrigation Bureau, will be
corporatized and made financially independent from the Government. The irrigation
districts at the village level will be turned over to farmers who will run the systems
themselves. The water resources management institutions at the town, county and district
levels will be corporatized, converted to financially autonomous units, and would derive
all their revenues from user charges. In addition, water prices (see para. 5.18 and Table
5.1) will be at levels to reflect the economic value of water. Also, the allocation of water
resources will ensure that industrial and urban sectors are provided with enough water,
although there will be a basic allocation of water for agriculture and human consumption.
Last, water trading within and outside of sectors will be allowed to achieve more rational
use.
3.28  In the second phase, all six water agencies will be placed under one corporation or
holding company to better manage the resource. A more detailed description is given in
Annex 1 1.
3.29  The regulatory body, the Wanjiazhai Water Management Committee, will
coordinate management activities of the corporatized entities mentioned above. This
body will regulate water allocation and future price adjustments and thus ensure
consistency of water prices across sectors and sources, monitor surface and groundwater,
and coordinate and approve water trading.
3.30  The reform will be carried out by three institutions: the Shanxi Commission for
Structural Reform (SCSR), the Water Conservancy Department (SWCD) and Bureau for
Price Administration (SBPA).
3.31  Technical assistance of $10.1 million (including contingencies) will be provided
under the project to help carry out the work and will be applied as follows: $2.5 million
for consultants including $200,000 for promoting community participation, $0.7 million
for training, $2.6 million to establish a water resources monitoring system, and $4.3
million for equipment and vehicles (see Annex 2, page 99).



- 26 -
3.32  Water Pollution Control and Industrial Waste Management. The project also
provides for investments in water pollution control and industrial waste management.
The Taiyuan and Shanxi Province EPBs will be strengthened, conduct studies and
implement programs to improve pollution control and maintain water quality in the Fen
River and other receiving waters at acceptable levels. These initiatives will include:
(a)   An environmental master plan for Taiyuan, which involves a wastewater
management master plan to further develop the discharge pernit system
and related pollution prevention programs;
(b)   In-plant wastewater monitoring and enforcement systems, with effective
charges and penalties;
(c)   Training for EPB staff; and
(d)   A subloan facility of $8.0 million to implement cleaner technologies in the
main polluting industries.
3.33  The purpose of the subloan facility is for financing industrial enterprises in
Taiyuan City to adopt Clean Technologies to reduce and control their pollution to protect
and improve the Fen River water quality and the environment of Taiyuan. The local
counterpart fund will come mainly from the special environment protection investment
from Shanxi Provincial and Taiyuan City Govermments as well as from enterprises' self-
raised fund. To effectively manage the subloan facility, Taiyuan will establish a special
body, the Subloan Administration Technical Committee (SATC), and an executive
agency, the Subloan Management Office (SMO), which are patterned after the experience
of Tianjin, which also has a major industrial center with problems of waste management
similar to these at Taiyuan. SATC is in charge of subloan management, which comprises
representatives of concerned government agencies including TEPB, Taiyuan Planning
Commission, and Taiyuan Finance Bureau with TEPB at leading position. The major
duties for SATC include: (a) preparing directives and regulations for subloan use;
(b) setting up subloan approval conditions; (c) setting up the SMO; (d) approving the
SMO annual work plan; (e) approving subloan projects; and (f) reviewing the SMO
annual report.
3.34  Subloan Operation Procedure. Initial proposals are to be made by candidate
enterprises and submitted to responsible government agencies for review and permission.
Then, formal proposals are submitted to the SMO for appraisal with the criteria discussed
below.
3.35  Procedures for Processing Subloans. TEPB with the use of local consultants
will be responsible for processing the subloans. The enterprises will prepare an initial
feasibility that will be reviewed in detail by TEPB using its own consultants. TEPB will
appraise the proposals and submit the appraisal to the SMO for final approval. Prior
Bank review will only be needed for the first three subprojects and subsequent
subprojects for which subloans of over $1.0 million or more are proposed.



- 27 -
3.36  Subborrower Eligibility Criteria. The subborrower must be in the following
types of industries: steel, chemicals including fertilizer, paper mills or pharmaceuticals.
Under the circumstances, a loan will be made available under a first come/first serve basis
to industry who could meet the following criteria and subproject eligibility criteria: (a) is
a limited liability (shareholding) company or otherwise established as a legal entity;
(b) has a completed EIA approved by the TEPB and has a mitigation plan satisfactory to
the Bank; (c) has adopted the new accounting standards and its financial statements have
been audited by independent auditors; and (d) the company has been appraised and found
creditworthy (see Annex 15).
3.37  Terms of the Subloan. The subloan terms will be as follows:
* Interest Rate and Fees. Subborrowers will pay a commitment fee of 0.75 percent
on the undisbursed amount of the subloan. The minimum interest margin will be
2 percent over the Bank interest rate but could vary depending on the cost of
undertaking the required appraisal and loan processing, administrative overheads
for the SMO and repayment risk.
*  Maturity. The maturity of individual subloans will be no more than three years'
grace and eight years' repayment period.
*  Rate of Return. Each subproject financed will have a financial rate and an
economic rate of return of no less than  1I percent.
3.38  The maximum size of a loan cannot exceed $1.5 million. The proceeds of the
subloan are mainly for pollution control equipment and services and must not cover the
costs of civil works, taxes, or land.
3.39  During negotiations, assurances were obtainedfrom Shanxi that it will establish
through Taiyuan an SMO with terms of reference, staffing and other resources
acceptable to the Bank by December 31, 1997 to appraise subprojects, provide subloans
and supervise, monitor, and report on subprojects in accordance with an operations
manual's procedures and on terms and conditions satisfactory to the Bank. Appraisal
procedures will focus on (a) an analysis of the viability of the enterprise undertaking the
project; (b) an evaluation of the subproject's EIA's mitigation plan; (c) an evaluation of
procurement arrangements; and (d) an assessment of the enterprise's ability to pay back
the loan.
F. COMPLEMENTARY WORKS
3.40  Complementary investments and activities described in detail in para. 3.3 will be
needed to efficiently and economically use some 640 million m 3/year of additional raw
water from the Yellow River to the Fen River (for the use of Taiyuan and surrounding
areas). These complementary works will be planned, financed and implemented by
Taiyuan and the Province over the next 25 years. The investments will double Taiyuan's
available water resources by 2001, triple them by 2020, and support sustained economic



- 28 -
growth and higher living standards over the whole region.  The relevant sector
institutions responsible for implementing them and, operating and maintaining services
are being reformed and expanded to accomplish the tasks.
3.41  Costs and Financing of Complementary Works. The estimated cost of the
water supply component is about Y 3.2 billion and of the wastewater component, Y 3.0
billion. These works will be financed from self-generated funds, including increased
water and wastewater charges, other provincial sources (Y 0.9 billion), and from bilateral
loans. Bilateral and multilateral sources have tentatively earmarked a loan to cover up to
70 percent (Y 1.8 billion) of the cost of the water supply component. It is also expected
to finance about Y 750 million of the wastewater component.  Taiyuan industrial
enterprises are expected to contribute about Y 765 million.
3.42  Implementation Schedule. Most of these works are scheduled to be completed
concurrently with the additional project water coming onstream. The Huyan Treatment
Plant, with a capacity of 1.0 million m3/day, and the replacement of about 170 km of
undersized and aging transmission lines and the installation of new lines will be
completed by 2002.  Rehabilitation of the five sewage treatment works and the
completion of the first phase of the sewer program (interceptors, secondary and tertiary
sewers) to provide about 0.6 to 0.7 million m3/day of wastewater collection and treatment
capacity, is also scheduled for completion by 2003. During negotiations, assurances
were obtained from Shanxi that all proposed complementary works for Taiyuan water
supply and wastewater collection and treatment and associated resettlement activities
would be implemented as planned in a program acceptable to the Bank so as to be
completed by June 30, 2002. Shanxi will provide the Bank on a semiannual basis (by
each April 15 and October 15) details of the designs, financing and construction progress
for Taiyuan water supply and sewerage plans implemented in the preceding semester and
plans for construction for the 12 months ahead.
G. ENVIRONMENTAL IMPACT ASSESSMENT
3.43  A detailed EIA was carried out by the China Institute of Radiation Protection
(CIRP) and the Shanxi University Environmental Engineering Department, with help
from foreign consultants. It was reviewed and endorsed by the National Environmental
Protection Agency of China (NEPA). Based on the Bank review and recommendations
during project preparation (November 1995 and May 1996), the reports were improved
and the EIA was submitted to the Bank in July 1996. CIRP prepared an English
summary of the report, which will be circulated to the Board on October 12, 1996.
3.44  The EIA reviewed the need for water in the Taiyuan area; the availability of water
for diversion (Annex 16); impacts along the delivery system (resettlement, assurance of
continued delivery, maintenance of water quality, disposal of tunnel excavations and
siltation problems); use of imported water and water pollution. It also specified needs for
overall environmental monitoring, an environmental management plan (EMP) and
training needs (see Annex 5).



- 29 -
3.45  The studies indicated the project had overwhelming positive affects because it
would address the scarcity of water.  At present, average urban household gross
consumption is 45 liters/capita/day, which is 41 percent of China's average, and 29
percent of the world average. Industrial water use is limited to 60 percent of demand and
recycling has reached the limits of technology. The present daily shortfall is about
400,000 m3/day, of which about 200,000 m /day are supplied by overextracting
groundwater. This has caused the groundwater to drop to over 100 m in many places and
very severe land subsidence in the city of up to 3 m. The groundwater depletion has also
caused several local streams in the Fen River Basin to dry up.
3.46  Hence, any measures to introduce water from the Yellow River will help to reduce
the human distress and improve living conditions in the city. In addition, reducing the
use of groundwater will halt land subsidence and improve the flows in small streams.
3.47  The EIA identified several potential impacts, of which only two have significant
potential negative impacts if not properly managed: (a) resettlement of a small number of
people and (b) the increased wastewater generated from the introduction of large volumes
of water to the city. However, with the proposed control measures, the net impacts for
both situations will be positive.
3.48  In addition, although most of the project works will be underground, they will
affect some surface areas due to land acquisition for tunnel crossings in river valleys and
for earth spoil disposal (from tunnel excavation) and health hazards to workers on
construction sites. The health hazards to workers and disposal of tunnel spoil can be
offset by straightforward control measures available in China and these will be applied in
the project. Because 1,204 individuals will be affected by land acquisition, a resettlement
plan was prepared in accordance to OD 4.3 (see paras. 3.50-3.57), to offset negative
impacts.
3.49  To ameliorate the wastewater problem, a detailed wastewater treatment and
management plan was developed and funding sources identified; as a result, by 2002 (one
year after water arrives in Taiyuan), 89 percent (42 percent in STW's and 37 percent in
lagoons) of the wastewater will be treated and by 2010, 100 percent (74 percent in STW's
and 26 percent in lagoons). In addition, a Water Pollution Control and Industrial Waste
Management Program was incorporated into the project [see paras. 3.2(c) and 3.3]; by
2002, the water quality of the Fen River in Taiyuan will be improved from class V to
class IV, and by 2010, to class IV. During negotiations, assurances were obtained that
YRDPC will employ by September 15, 1997 an independent environment/resettlement
panel of experts to review and make recommendations with respect to the EMP. Further,
it shall maintain an Environmental Management Office with competent staff in adequate
numbers and with responsibilities and functions acceptable to the Bank to manage,
coordinate and monitor the EMP. Also, it shall ensure that all activities under the
project will comply with the EMP.



- 30 -
H. LAND ACQUISITION AND RESETTLEMENT
3.50  Project Impacts. The project entails acquisition of land and the resettlement of
those affected. YRDPC conducted a detailed census, with the help of counties, towns and
villages; based on the results, acquisitions will involve 7,393 mu (440 ha) of permanently
held agricultural land, 2,495 mu (166 ha) of temporarily leased agricultural land, and 23
small enterprises. It will also involve the resettlement of 54 individuals in 16 households.
About 1,204 persons will lose land, the 16 households have lost their dwellings, and 273
employees will be affected due to the relocation of their enterprises. The resettlement
impacts are described in Annex 6.
3.51   Resettlement Action Plan (RAP). To mitigate these adverse impacts, YRDPC
prepared a comprehensive strategy for implementation and submitted it to the Bank. The
RAP includes: (a) a detailed census of all people and assets affected; (b) the legal
framework for land acquisition; (c) a framework for implementation (objectives for land
acquisition and resettlement, compensation policies and rates, preferential policies, etc.);
(d) institutional arrangements; (e) costs and budgets; (f) an implementation schedule; (g)
participation and consultations; (h) grievance procedures; and (i) monitoring and
evaluation. The RAP covers all works including the resettlement and compensation of
the people affected by the project. The RAP, which the Bank found satisfactory, is
summarized in Annex 6.
3.52  Resettlement Strategy. The strategy was designed to ensure the standard of
living of all those affected will be improved or, at the least, restored. To achieve this
goal, the following principles were incorporated into the RAP.
(a)   Land acquisition and resettlement should be minimized as much as
possible;
(b)   Compensation for structures should be at replacement cost without
depreciation;
(c)   Compensation should be paid to the collectives before land acquisition and
used for productive purposes;
(d)   All infrastructure affected will be replaced;
(e)   Priority in job opportunities with the project will be given to those affected
by the project;
(f)    Land will be redistributed to farmers who lose land due to the project;
(g)   All affected enterprises will be helped to relocate and workers will be
given an allowance during relocation;



- 31 -
(h)   Those affected will be invited to participate in preparing  and
implementing the RAP; and
(i)   Those affected will be able to redress their grievances.
3.53  Resettlement Costs. These costs, which were included in the project, total an
estimated Y 51.33 million (which involve physical and price contingencies).
3.54 Implementation Arrangements. YRDPC is responsible for implementing the
entire resettlement component. It has a resettlement office to carry out the planning,
designing, budgeting, and coordination with the county resettlement offices (CROs). The
YRDPC resettlement office (YRDPCRO) has three field offices with 3 to 5 staff to
coordinate land acquisition, contract with local government, supervise resettlement
progress and support the CROs and township resettlement offices (TROs). The CROs
and TROs each have 5 to 10 staff and are responsible for implementing the land
acquisition, livelihood development programs, and compensation to those being resettled.
TMWC will be responsible for all resettlement and compensation of people affected by
the complementary works.
3.55  The RAP will be carried out in a highly participatory manner, and those affected
will be consulted at each stage in the planning and implementation; avenues for
redressing grievances were established at all levels. At present, all 54 individuals have
been resettled; several others who lost land received new land and many have started
agricultural production and sideline industries. YRDPC's monitoring indicates the
incomes of those affected have increased substantially (Table 3.2).  A detailed
implementation program is presented in Annex 6.
TABLE 3.2: INCOMES BEFORE AND AFTER RESETTLEMENT
(Constant 1992 Yuan)
County                 Per Capita         Per Capita       Annual Income
Farmland (ha)       Grain (kg)        (Yuan/capita)
Before, in 1992           0.12               465              1,025
After, in 1995            0.14               573              1,347
3.56  Monitoring and Evaluation. Monitoring of resettlement will occur at three
levels. First, YRDPC will conduct bimonthly internal reviews of land acquisition, civil
works, land development and redistribution, off-farm activities, restoration of public
facilities, training of CRO and TRO staff and the welfare of all project-affected people
(PAP). Second, an independent monitoring institution will conduct external reviews.
Yellow River Conservancy Commission Resettlement Office (YRCCRO), which was
selected as the independent monitor, will review the physical progress, PAP livelihood
restoration, PAP participation, grievances and effectiveness of grievance procedures, and



- 32 -
proposed measures for improvement. YRCCRO will submit semiannual monitoring
reports to YRDPC and Bank. Third, a seven-member international/local panel will meet
twice a year to review the resettlement progress and propose measures for improvement.
It will submit reports of its findings to YRDPC and the Bank.
3.57  During negotiations, assurances were obtained from Shanxi that resettlement
agencies at the provincial, county and township levels will carry out the resettlement
under the project according to the RAP. YRDPC will (a) also carry out resettlement
according to the RAP; (b) employ an independent monitoring institution by September
15, 1997, acceptable to the Bank, to evaluate the socioeconomic progress of those
affected by resettlement, (c) employ an independent panel of environment/ resettlement
experts by September 15, 1997, satisfactory to the Bank, to review the resettlement
semiannually (see para. 3.49); (d) promptly furnish to the Bank, for comment, reports of
the findings and recommendations of both groups; and (e) implement all
recommendations for follow-up actions, taking account of the Bank's comments.
I. STATUS OF PROJECT PREPARATION
3.58  Detailed designs have been completed for tunnels in GMT and SMT, underground
pumphouses, aqueducts, culverts and other structures linking tunnels. Tunnels 6 to 11 on
the GMT are already under construction (see para. 3.18). These designs, which form
about 80 percent of all civil works, were reviewed by the Bank and found to be
satisfactory. Bank missions also reviewed preliminary designs for the connecting works
and the complementary works for water supply for Taiyuan. Feasibility studies for
sewerage works and treatment were reviewed and found satisfactory. During appraisal,
the mission reviewed the detailed design for these components. All land acquisition for
construction for the GMT was completed; land acquisition for the SMT has started and
will be completed in 1998.
3.59  Designs for electrical and mechanical works pumps, motors and controls were
also completed, reviewed by the Bank and found satisfactory. The design for the
SCADA system will be completed by December 1997.
3.60 Prequalification documents for international competitive bidding (ICB) works for
the two underground pump stations and all tunnels on the SMT were completed, and were
reviewed and approved by the Bank. Bids for the tunnels and pump caverns have been
received and are being evaluated (see para. 3.69).  YRDPC has advertised in
Development Business and China Daily for prequalification of mechanical contractors,
which will be completed in June 1997. The Bank is reviewing the bidding documents for
electrical/mechanical works and these will be completed by June 1997.
3.61  Detailed proposals were also received for the technical assistance (TA) for Water
Sector Institutional Reform and Water Pollution Control and Industrial Management.
These proposals were found to be satisfactory.



- 33 -
J. CONSTRUCTION SCHEDULE
3.62  Construction of preparatory work for SMT started in 1994 and should be
completed in early 1997. At Pingguan, located close to tunnel 8 of GMT, YRDPC has
constructed a large field office for design and construction supervision. In addition, there
are several site offices already constructed along the SMT for receiving contractors.
Contract award for all major civil works contracts should be completed after Board
approval, expected in July 1997.
3.63   The main civil works construction will start in September 1997 and will be
completed in July 2002, after about five years (see Annex 10 for a detailed schedule). A
six-month commissioning period will be required before water can actually be supplied to
the city. Taiyuan will complete all its works by the end of 2002 to receive water. The
proposed schedule is considered reasonable and attainable. A detailed program of project
supervision is presented in Annex 13. The monitoring of all key construction activities is
presented in Annex 10.
K. PROJECT COST ESTIMATES
3.64  The total costs of this phase of the project are estimated to be $1,351.6 million
equivalent, of which $492.9 million (36 percent) represents the foreign exchange
component. Annex 2 contains, in detail, the estimated costs of the project, which are
summarized in Table 3.3. The project base costs are in February 1997 prices. These
costs include interest during construction and commitment charges of $63.8 million. The
tunnel and other underground works costs are based on ongoing works where
international contractors have bid. The costs of electrical and mechanical equipment,
transformers and gates are based on recent quotations from manufacturers and suppliers'
prices for similar equipment and materials. The costs of the remaining items are based on
the consulting engineers' data for similar works. Physical contingencies are assumed as
follows:  10 percent for the aboveground works; 15 percent for the tunnels and
underground works; 10 percent for the remaining preparatory works, contract
administration, technical assistance, environmental management, studies and training;
and 10 percent for the electromechanical equipment. These percentages are considered to
be reasonable in view of the site features, the stage of design, and the contracts ongoing at
the site. The price escalation for costs expressed in foreign exchange (US dollars) has
been calculated in accordance with the anticipated international price escalation of 1.7
percent for 1997, 2.2 percent for 1998, and 2.5 percent for 1999 and thereafter. The price
escalation for costs expressed in local currency is calculated according to projected local
inflation rates of 6.2, 5.8, and 5.5 percent for 1997, 1998 and 1999 and thereafter,
respectively.

L. FINANCING PLAN
3.65      The financing proposed for the project is shown in Table 3.4.  To enable timely
and effective implementation of the project, it is necessary to ensure an adequate flow of
local funds, as shown  in  Annex  2.  YRDPC  will derive  all its counterpart funds from
surcharges on coal, electricity and water supplied. The surcharge collection was established
in 1993 and about $240 million had been collected up to 1995. Collection in 1995 was
$115 million and it is expected a slightly greater amount will be collected per year in 1996-
2001. The surcharge collections are fairly well-established and should not pose any major
issues  for counterpart fumding.   During  negotiations,  assurances  were  obtained  that
YRDPC would submit to the Bank by December 15 of each year the financing plan and the
proposed implementation program for the next year as approved by the Government, and
thereafter implement this program, taking into account the Bank's views on it.

- 35 -
3.66  China is eligible for single currency loans under the Bank's expanded program.
GOC and Shanxi Province have selected LIBOR-based US dollar single currency loan
terms for the project in order to facilitate management of the foreign exchange risk of
their borrowing by more closely matching the currency of their liabilities with their net
trade flows, about 80 percent of which are US dollar denominated. They selected the
LIBOR-based product in order to preserve the full maturity of this loan, compared to the
fixed-rate option that would have resulted in a shorter maturity. The Borrower judges
that it can manage any interest risk. The loan of $400 million would be for 20 years,
including a 5-year grace period at the standard rate for LIBOR-based US dollar single
currency loans. The proceeds of the loan would be onlent from the GOC through the
Shanxi Government to YRDPC with the same terms as the Bank loan. At negotiations,
an assurance was obtained from Shanxi that it would onlend the proceeds of the Bank
loan (other than for the Institutional Support Program for Shanxi Government) to
YRDPC, under a subsidiary loan agreement with a 20-year term, including 5 years of
grace at the same interest rate and commitment fee as those paid by the Borrower. The
execution of a subsidiary loan agreement between Shanxi and YRDPC, satisfactory to the
Bank, would be a condition of effectiveness of the loan.
3.67  A small portion of the financing is from official bilateral sources for pipe-making
equipment, consultants and for special steel for the pipes.  Since the funds are not
immediately required, it is not proposed to have a cross-effectiveness clause in the loan
agreement. However, we propose to have a cross-default clause; in the event the funds do
not become available by July 1, 1998 or there is a suspension or cancellation of the
cofinancing, the Borrower is obligated to cover the shortfall in funds.
M. PROCUREMENT
3.68  The procurement arrangements for the project have followed the Bank's
"Guidelines for Procurement Under IBRD Loans and IDA Credits" published in January
1995 and revised in January and August 1996, as well as experience gained from
previous Bank infrastructure projects in China. The Standard Bidding Documents
(SBDs), which have been agreed between the Ministry of Finance (MOF) and the Bank in
August 1996, would be adopted for all international competitive bidding (ICB) and
national competitive bidding (NCB) for civil works and goods. For the appointment of
consultants, the Bank's Model Contract Agreement for Consultants would be used. The
main items to be procured are: (a) the main civil works; (b) electrical and mechanical
equipment; and (c) engineering and consulting services. The procurement arrangements
for the proposed projects are summarized in Table 3.5.

3.69  Civil Works. All contracts for civil works valued at $10 million equivalent or
above would use ICB procedures. There will be eight lots of civil works contracts (see
Annex 3). The procurement of the main civil works, through ICB procedures, has been
divided into three lots, with a total value of $328 million, one for the underground pump
stations and two sets of tunnels for the SMTs and aqueducts and culverts. These contracts
would be mainly financed by the proposed Bank loan. All civil works contracts will require
prequalification. A Disputes Review Board will be included in contracts valued at $50
million or more. The prequalification documents have been reviewed by the Bank and the
prequalifications have been implemented by YRDPC. A total of 10 prequalified contractors
have tendered for ICB bids and the bids are being evaluated. Bidding documents for these
main civil works have been reviewed in the Bank. All other civil works (16 contracts),
valued at $69.6 million, will be financed under NCB procedures because they are
distributed and small in nature and not of interest to foreign contractors.
3.70  Goods. All contracts for goods valued at $1 million equivalent or more would be
awarded under ICB procedures. Concrete pipes, pumps, electric motors, mechanical works
and control systems valued at $130.4 million (three lots) will also be procured using ICB
procedures. Qualified domestic manufacturers would be eligible for a margin of preference
of 15 percent of CIF prices or the actual customs duties, whichever is lower, in the bid
evaluation. Items estimated to cost less than $1 million equivalent will use NCB
procedures up to an aggregate total of $59.7 million. The proposed Bank loan would also
finance environmental and hydrologic forecasting equipment for $4.3 million, which would
be procured through limited international bidding (LIB) procedures following the Bank's
Guidelines. LIB procedures are being used because of the specialized nature of the
equipment. Items or groups of items estimated to cost less than the equivalent of $300,000
per contract, up to an aggregate amount of $3.1 million, may be purchased on the basis of at
least three quotations from qualified suppliers eligible under the Bank's procurement
guidelines for national shopping.
3.71  Procurement Review. All contract packages for works and goods financed by the
Bank and estimated to cost $10.0 million equivalent or more for works, $1.0 million
equivalent or more for goods and consultant contracts valued at $100,000 equivalent or
more for firms and $50,000 equivalent or more for individuals would be subject to the
Bank's prior review (about 95 percent of the loan). In addition, the first three NCB
contracts less than $10.0 million in value for works and $1.0 million in value for goods will
be subject to the Bank's prior review.
3.72  Consultants.  Consultants are needed for construction supervision, detailed
design, reviews, engineering and environment panels, institutional reform and training. A
detailed list is provided in Table 3.6. All consultants would be selected in accordance
with principles and procedures satisfactory to the Bank on the basis of the "Guidelines
Selection and Employment of Consultants by World Bank Borrowers," January 1997.
YRDPC will retain the present international consultants for detail design (210 person-
months) who have prepared the feasibility study, the detailed designs and bidding
documents, on grounds of intimate knowledge of the site, river hydrology, local



- 38 -
construction conditions, and the overall project in the interests of project safety,
economy, efficiency and expediency. The consultants have also been involved in
evaluation for prequalifying of contractors and will be involved in bid evaluation with
YRDPC. The consultant services for construction supervision for tunnels and pump
caverns (479 person-months), connecting works pipelines (200 person-months) and for
the environmental master plan (75 person-months) for Taiyuan City would be procured
using competitive procedures (QCBS) following the Guidelines. The $0.8 million of
consultancy for institutional support is a quality-based service, which is highly
specialized and needs intimate knowledge of Chinese and local practices. Another $2.2
million of consultants for engineering design panels, environmental/resettlement panels,
groundwater monitoring, etc. will be individual consultants.

N. DISBURSEMENT
3.73   The proposed Bank loan of $400 million would be disbursed over a period of five

technology pollution fund subloan reimbursements would be made on the basis of
Statements of Expenditure (SOEs). Documentation supporting the SOEs would be
retained by YRDPC and made available for review by Bank supervision missions. To
facilitate disbursements, a Special Account in US dollars, with an authorized allocation of
$15 million (less than four months of average eligible expenditures), will be established

50 percent of the authorized allocation, whichever occurs sooner. Retroactive financing
in an aggregate amount of $28 million would be provided under the loan to cover
payments made for expenditures on civil works, urgently needed goods, consultant
services, and training incurred before signing the Loan Agreement, but after October 20,
1996.
