3.74   The disbursement schedule for the proposed Bank loan is given in Annex 4,
Table 1. The disbursement period covers the first phase of the construction period. The
disbursements profile for this project varies slightly from the agriculture sector profiles.
There is a slow startup because of the time taken by large civil works contractors to
mobilize. However, once mobilization is completed, the disbursement proceeds rapidly,
and it conforms with the disbursement profile of other agricultural projects (see Annex4).
The disbursement schedule is based on the detailed project implementation program. The
loan is expected to be fully disbursed by June 30, 2003.



- 40 -
0. PROJECT MONITORING, REPORTING AND SUPERVISION
3.75  Supervision. The project would be supervised through Bank missions whose
frequency would be guided by the progress of the works. However, on the average, a
mission at about nine-month intervals would be mounted; a Supervision Mission Plan is
presented in Annex 13. In addition to field missions, staff input would be provided at
Headquarters for project-related activities such as completion of supervision reports,
procurement documentation and contracts, staff training and equipment proposals and
follow-up and review of the studies on water sector institutional reforms, industrial waste
management and antipollution fund utilization. Moreover, the Bank would monitor
progress of implementation of all project components through quarterly progress reports
to be submitted to the Bank. It is estimated that over the implementation period of the
project the total time expended on supervision would be in the order of 100 person-
weeks.
3.76  Monitoring.   During implementation, project performance, including the
achievement of physical targets and attainment of other project objectives will be
monitored by YRDPC through the use of performance indicators, reports on the
environment and land acquisition and resettlement, progress reports and auditing of
project accounts. In addition, upon completion, the project will be reviewed in an
Implementation Completion Report.  Annex 12 presents the proposed monitoring
indicators for the project. Each of the project components' indices will track annual
progress in attaining targets during implementation and impact objectives after the project
is completed. Assurances were obtained at negotiations that YRDPC shall, by April 30
of each year and for three years after completion of the project, furnish to the Bank an
annual monitoring report, in a format acceptable to the Bank, covering all components of
the project, in conformance with the agreed monitoring indicators. In addition, YRDPC
will provide semiannual progress reports by April 15 and October 15 of the project
implementation in the previous semester including any revisions to the project or its RAP
or EMP.
3.77  Reporting. Based on the monitoring activities described in Annex 13, YRDPC
will submit to the Bank an annual monitoring report. This report will be based on the
following reports, which will be submitted to the Bank during the course of a given year:
(a)   Annual environmental monitoring reports during the construction phase.
(b)   A  series of internal and external (independent) monitoring reports
assessing the impact of land acquisition and resettlement on various
segments of the local population, describing the income restoration
strategies followed and the number of people compensated (para. 3.56 and
Annex 6).
(c)   Annual audit reports for the project accounts and financial statements
(para. 3.79).



- 41 -
(d)   Semiannual progress reports for all project activities and up-to-date
performance indicators.
(e)   A Project Implementation Completion Report would be prepared and
submitted to the Bank not later than six months after the loan closing date.
P. ACCOUNTS AND AUDITS
3.78  Accounting. Up to now, YRDPC has maintained two sets of accounts, one set for
capital construction and the other for operation. Since these two sets of accounts are not
consolidated, the balance sheet for operations does not include construction work-in-
progress and related financing. However, under the newly revised general principles set
forth by the Ministry of Finance, enterprises will have to consolidate the capital
construction accounts with the operations accounts. YRDPC will consolidate its accounts
and special technical assistance using foreign consultants has been included as part of the
project to help do this.
3.79  Auditing. Audits of Bank-financed projects have been conducted by the Shanxi
Provincial Audit Bureau (SPAB) under the guidance of the State Audit Administration
(SAA). This arrangement is satisfactory. Assurances were obtained from YRDPC and
Shanxi (for the Institutional Components for Water Sector Institutional Reform and Water
Pollution Control and Industrial Waste Management) that they would maintain separate
accounts for the project and that the audited project accounts of Shanxi and the audited
financial statements of YRDPC and Shanxi Government would be furnished to the Bank
within six months after the end of each fiscal year. The Borrower would arrange for audits
of the Special Account and the details of withdrawals from the loan account made on the
basis of SOEs and the auditor's opinion as to whether such withdrawals were made against
expenditures eligible for disbursement by the Bank. Audited financial statements would
also be required to be furnished to Shanxi (for forwarding to the Bank on request) by
enterprises borrowing subloans under the clean technology pollution fund. YRDPC has
developed an internal auditing section (staffed with chartered accountants), which
periodically will examine the accounts of each operating unit. The objective of these
examinations is primarily to test for accuracy and compliance with Ministry of Finance
regulations. The section will serve as liaison between external auditors and the project
entity. The Bank will review the internal auditing function during project implementation
with a view toward increasing its efficiency and usefulness to YRDPC.



- 42 -
4. PROJECT MANAGEMENT AND IMPLEMENTATION
A. PROJECT ORGANIZATION
4.1    General Structure. The Government of Shanxi Province (GSP) will take overall
responsibility for implementing the project. Recognizing the project's importance and
complexity, and based on successful experiences elsewhere in the country, the GSP
created the Wanjiazhai Water Transfer Project Headquarters (WWTPH) to guide the
project management office(s) and coordinate the construction and resettlement efforts of
related organizations and authorities. The general organization of the project is shown in
Annex 9.
4.2   The main role of WWTPH, which is headed by the Vice Governor of Shanxi
Province and made up of representatives from various key departments of the province, is
to oversee the Wanjiazhai Yellow River Diversion Project Corporation (YRDPC) in
policy and fiscal matters, as well as project construction and resettlement, and to
coordinate issues and problem resolution at the provincial level.
4.3   Project Management Office (PMO). YRDPC, whose function is to build,
operate and maintain the project, has established a PMO for the project. Originally
known as the Yellow River Diversion Project Bureau (YRDPB), YRDPC was initially set
up as an administrative organization of the provincial government, in order to have easy
access to other government organizations and authorities. In 1994, it was approved by
the provincial government and registered as a state-owned enterprise.  Its general
manager, the former director of the Planning Commission of Shanxi Province, reports
directly to WWTPH and the provincial government. At present, it has 734 staff and more
(up to 1,500) will be added during the construction and operational phases. Although
YRDPC was formed recently, its key personnel have been involved in the construction
and/or operation of similar projects, or involved in the planning and preparation of this
project; thus, it is capable of managing the project's construction and operations. The
PMO will cooperate with agencies in charge of resettlement, in order to coordinate the
project's resettlement component. Further, it has worked out a plan for progressively
reforming the corporation from an administrative organization to an economic enterprise
during the life of the project. During negotiations, assurances were obtained that
YRDPC will maintain its project management office with competent staff in adequate
numbers and it will have responsibility andfunctions satisfactory to the Bank.
B. PROJECT EXECUTION AND SUPERVISION OF CONSTRUCTION
4.4   YRDPC will execute the project, acting as the employer. It will be responsible for
preparing and executing all bids for civil works, goods and services; also, for managing
construction, cost and quality control of all civil works and the installation of plant and



- 43 -
equipment. It will be assisted by the Wanjiazhai Project Supervision Joint Venture
(WPSJV), comprised of six organizations, which will supervise construction; by the
Tianjin Investigation and Design Institute (TIDI) and Shanxi Investigation and Design
Institute (SIDI), which will oversee design and design changes; by foreign and local
consultants, which will assist in managing the contract, as well as cost and quality
control. Also, a panel of experts (POE, para. 4.8) will review design changes and any
special construction problems semiannually. In addition, a dispute review board (DRB,
para. 4.10) will be appointed to resolve any disputes that arise with the contractors (see
Annex 9).
4.5   Construction Supervision. WPSJV will act as Engineer ' for the Employer and
be responsible for supervising daily and detailed fieldwork for quality control, certifying
payments to contractors, maintaining a field laboratory for testing materials delivered to
the site, and revising design documents/drawings according to field conditions. Its six
supervision organizations are licensed and have experience with the construction and
supervision of similar projects. With support from YRDPC and assistance from the
consultants, WPSJV will have the capability required to mange the construction of such a
complex and large project. The organization of WPSJV is shown in Annex 9. At
negotiations, assurances were obtained that YRDPC will continue to employ an Engineer
with qualifications, experience and terms of reference acceptable to the Bank in order to
undertake the construction supervision.
4.6   Engineering Design and Technical Assistance.  Having been retained by
YRDPC, TIDI is assisting with the design of civil and electromechanical works from the
inlet to the end of tunnels to Daliang Reservoir on the north main line and the end of the
south main line; SIDI is assisting with the design of civil and electromechanical works
from Daliang Reservoir, including the reservoir, to the end of the north main line and
connection works, starting from Fen-] Reservoir. Both are experienced institutes and
have recently designed some large- and mid-scaled hydro projects.
4.7   Nevertheless, YRDPC recognizes the very complex and delicate nature of the
construction and will therefore continue to use international consultants during
implementation. International engineering consultants have also been actively involved
in reviewing the project design and resolving specific technical matters related to the
pump station, TBM technology, tunneling and electromechanical equipment/plant. A
number of technical delegations and prominent tunnel and electromechanical experts
have visited the project site, offered valuable suggestions and shared their experiences
with their Chinese counterparts in building large and complex projects.
4.8   Panel of Experts.  YRDPC will continue to employ a POE, comprised of
international and domestic specialists, to help resolve particular technical problems
"Engineer" as defined in the Bank's Standard Bidding Document for Procurement of Works, January
1996.



- 44 -
related to the project and advise on major design and construction issues. The POE,
which has effectively reviewed the project's major technical issues, has been funded by
YRDPC. Under the proposed project, the POE would meet regularly. At negotiations, an
assurance was obtained from YRDPC that it will employ a POE by September 30, 1997
that is independent from the project's designers, that the POE will conduct, at least twice
annually or more frequently as needed, reviews of designs and technical specifications
and advise on unforeseen problems relating to project implementation, and that YRDPC
will report the POE's findings to the Bank and Borrower on a semiannual basis.
4.9   Foreign Consultants. Due to the project's magnitude and complexity, and the
use of international civil contractors and equipment suppliers, YRDPC will appoint
experienced and qualified international consultants who will play a key role in assisting
YRDPC and WPSJV to manage project construction. Their scope of work will include
contract management, cost control, scheduling, and construction site administration
during the entire implementation period. The total input of engineering services and
technical assistance is estimated to be about 679 person-months of foreign consultants'
services, which is considered reasonable for a project of this sort (see Annex 14).
4.10 Dispute Review Board (DRB). Based on the experience with other projects
involving international contractors in China, YRDPC recognizes the need to have a DRB
to help it resolve the conflicts that emerge during construction between the employer and
contractors. Therefore, YRDPC and the contractors will jointly employ a DRB to resolve
such disputes.
4.11  The procedures for inspecting, maintaining and monitoring the safety of
underground/civil works in China generally follow international practices and are
considered satisfactory for the proposed project.
4.12 Environmental Management Plan (EMP). For the project works, the EMP will
be implemented by YRDPC's Environmental Management Office (EMO), whose
functions would be to ensure that all contractors adhere to the relevant environmental
specifications in their contracts.  The EMO will also monitor the environmental
parameters for air, water and land in the construction and resettlement sites.
C. OVERALL COORDINATION AND IMPLEMENTATION OF OTHER COMPONENTS
4.13  Resettlement Coordination. Various agencies and jurisdictions, at different
levels, are responsible for resettlement; thus, coordination will be essential and will be
carried out by two agencies. As the owner of the project, YRDPC will supervise and
coordinate resettlement activities through WWTPH. YRDPC will also be responsible for
channeling resettlement funds to the local governments' resettlement agencies. For this
reason, it has set up a Resettlement Office to maintain close liaison between the
Provincial Resettlement Bureau and local governments at various levels, as well as to
supervise and coordinate the program. And, since the GSP has ultimate authority for
resettlement and bears the responsibility for the welfare of its citizens, its Provincial



- 45 -
Resettlement Bureau will also be involved in coordinating the various local government
agencies and provincial departments.
D. OPERATIONS AND MANAGEMENT
4.14  Institutional Components.  The Water Sector Institutional Reform will be
carried out by SCSR, SWCD and SBPC (see para. 3.30). A large coordination working
group convened by SWCD will be involved in working out details of this component.
The larger group will consist of Taiyuan City municipal agencies such as TMWC,
Municipal Commission, Water Saving Office, etc.
4.15  The Water Pollution Control and Industrial Waste Component would be carried
out by the Taiyuan EPB under the guidance of the Provincial EPB.
4.16  YRDPC's organizational arrangement for project operations and maintenance
(O&M) will be quite different than for construction: Its operational departments will be
created about two years before the first phase of the project is completed. YRDPC's
organization will be transformed gradually to have the capability to operate and maintain
the entire system. During negotiations, assurances were obtained that YRDPC will
prepare under guidelines acceptable to the Bank and furnish to the Bank a program by
June 30, 1999 for smooth transformation of YRDPC from a construction entity to an
operating entity and thereafter will implement this plan, taking into account the Bank's
comments on this matter. The plan will have details of the new organizational structure,
staffing numbers and qualifications, and training programs for staff to make the
transition.



- 46 -
5. FINANCIAL MANAGEMENT
A. WATER RESOURCES AND SUPPLY ENTITIES
5.1   YRDPC is responsible for construction of the proposed project, including the
general main tunnel, pumping stations, south main line, and connection works [see para
3.2 (a)]. YRDPC was registered as a corporation in December 1995, and will become the
executing agency to operate and manage the project upon completion of construction.
YRDPC is also responsible for providing investment capital for the Shanxi Government's
share in the Wanjiazhai Dam.
5.2   To receive diverted water from YRDPC, TMWC will need to expand its treatment
capacity by constructing the Huyan water treatment plant and installing additional
transmission/distribution pipelines in Taiyuan city by year 2002. Since the project is
designed to supply water to industrial and domestic users in Taiyuan through TMWC and
since YRDPC will be liable for repayment of the Bank loan, determination of end-user
water tariffs becomes critical as it will directly impact TMWC's ability to pay for
purchased water and in turn YRDPC's ability to repay the Bank loan. Therefore,
financial forecasts along with projected water tariffs are prepared for both YRDPC and
TMWC, and both entities shall be covered by financial covenants.
5.3   Shanxi Water Conservancy Department (SWCD) governs Fen Reservoir 1
Management Bureau (Fen-1), Fen Water Conservancy Management Bureau (FWCMB),
and Zhang Zhe Reservoir Administrative Bureau (ZZRAB). As diverted Yellow River
water will flow through Fen Reservoir 1 and Fen-1 is responsible for management and
operation of the reservoir, YRDPC will need to have an agreement (in the interim until
Fen-l is incorporated within the Fen Basin Authority) with Fen-I detailing (1)
management fee to be collected by Fen-1, and (2) entitlement of water volume by Fen-1
and YRDPC at discharge point after the proposed project is commissioning.
5.4   Fen Reservoir 2, downstream of Fen Reservoir 1, will be used to adjust Fen River
water volume during different seasons. The Fen-2 Reservoir Water Enterprise Company
Ltd. (Fen-2) will construct, own, and manage Fen Reservoir 2.
5.5   Taiyuan Groundwater Resource Management Co. (TGRM) and the Self-Supply
Tubewell Commission (SSTC) are both responsible for monitoring, licensing, and
management of groundwater resource.
B. PROJECT FINANCING
5.6   Total project cost before interest during construction (IDC) is estimated at
Y 11.52 billion for all project works. Based on such cost estimate, plus required



- 47 -
investment in Wanjiazhai Dam of Y 2.23 billion but excluding about Y 0.2 billion for
institutional reform and water pollution control components that are not under YRDPC's
responsibility, YRDPC's costs amount to Y 13.55 billion prior to IDC, or Y 14.23 billion
after IDC and the foreign exchange gain/loss is taken into account (see Annex 8,
Summary Table, page 216).
5.7   YRDPC's capital requirement during 1993 to 2001 is estimated to be Y 14.07
billion, which is Y 165 million less than the above estimated total cost of Y 14.23 as the
foreign exchange loss during construction is a noncash item caused by revaluation of
long-term loans.
5.8   The proposed project, along with other nonproject components, will be financed
mainly by GSP surcharges, the World Bank loan, a small cofinancing loan, and
investment income from Wanjiazhai Dam's net income from electricity sales. GSP
surcharges, to be collected and about Y 7.6 billion used for the project before the end of
year 2001, consist of surcharges on coal sales, provincial electricity consumption,
outbound electricity sales, and water consumption (see Annex 8). The Bank loan of
Y 3.8 billion, $400 million equivalent, and a cofinancing loan of Y 273 million, $30
million equivalent, will be borrowed by YRDPC. Because Wanjiazhai Dam is to be
completed by end- 1998 and will start to have power revenue by 1999, YRDPC will then
be entitled to investment income. Such investment income is estimated at Y 911 million
during 1999 to 2001.
5.9   Altogether, a financing gap during 1996-2001 may still exist. Such a shortage
could be financed by (a) allocation of additional water revenue derived from the water
tariff adjustment starting in 1997; (b) an adjusted water resource fee; and (c) the Shanxi
Investment and Trust Company in case of a short-term financing need.  During
negotiations, assurances were obtainedfrom Shanxi that each year incremental revenues
from tariff adjustments and increased water resources fees between 1997 and 2001 will
be allocated to YRDPC by December 31 of that year as a capital contribution to YRDPC.
5.10  Huyan water treatment plant and associated pipelines in Taiyuan city will cost
Y 2.55 billion, of which Y 900 million will be financed by budgets of provincial and
municipal governments as well as by TMWC's internal cash generation; the remaining
will be funded by Japanese OECF loans.
C. PAST FINANCIAL PERFORMANCE
5.11  YRDPC's past financial statements for 1992-95 are presented in Annex 8. As a
construction organization, YRDPC follows "financial accounting systems for
construction units" and prepares only the capital balance statement. As of end-1995,
YRDPC had invested Y 1,134 million in project preparation and construction and Y 424
million in Wanjiazhai Dam; both are financed by GSP surcharges. Details on GSP
surcharges and YRDPC's existing financial reporting systems are described in Annex 8.



- 48 -
5.12 TMWC's past financial statements for 1992-95 are presented in Annex S. In
1995, the average water tariff for industry and domestic users in Taiyuan city was Y 1.1
and Y 0.7/mr  [value-added tax (VAT) inclusive] respectively, or Y 0.86/m3 on average
(excluding VAT). Despite increases in net income since 1994 due to water tariff
adjustment, TMWC has not kept much as retained earnings for future investment because
most net income was used for the GSP water surcharge paid to the Government. Most
capital investments such as installation of transmission pipelines are still funded by the
municipal budget rather than self-financing or borrowing. As a result, TMWC maintains
a healthy financial structure with a low outstanding liability for the past three years.
Adjustment of water tariffs is subject to government approval. Therefore, even though
TMWC has adopted an enterprise accounting system and prepares commercial financial
statements, it is not yet a financially autonomous company.
5.13 As quasi profit-making organizations, Fen-1, FWCAB, and ZZRAB are not
included in the governmental budget. For financial reporting purposes, all three bureaus
submit their own balance sheet and income statement to WSCD for aggregation. Fen- 1's
earnings are mainly from water resource fees paid by FWCAB (which is downstream on
the Fen River) and partly from electricity sales, while FWCAB's revenue is primarily
from water sales to irrigation and industries. Fen-1 charged FWCAB water resource fees
for an allocated quota for irrigation and industrial consumption at 1 fen and 10.5 fen per
m3 respectively in 1995 (the latter was increased to 15 fen in 1996). FWCAB charged
3                                   3
irrigation water consumption at 5.5 fen per m and industry consumption at 48 fen per m3.
In view of the charge limit set by the Price Bureau, both Fen-I and FWCAB have to
underprovide depreciation and incur less-than-required costs in order to ensure a small
amount of surplus for employee benefits. The imputed cost-recovery water resource fee
levels for Fen-1's water supplies to irrigation and industry are 9 fen and 18 fen per m3
and imputed FRCAB cost-recovery water prices for irrigation and industry are estimated
at 7 fen and 68 fen per m3 if necessary costs are taken into account. SWCD's past three
years' financial statements are presented in Annex 8.
D. WATER PRICING
5.14 Water pricing for YRDPC and TMWC is determined by the estimated total capital
investment cost which belongs to SMT, the estimated cost for the Huyan water treatment
plant, suggested financing plans for both YRDPC and TMWC, forecast water
transfer/supply volume, operational and financial assumptions, along with agreed
financial requirement indicators (see below and Annex 8).
5.15 The GMT cost is a common cost for SMT and NMT and thus should be shared
based on the estimated eventual water transfer volume, 56.5 percent and 43.5 percent,
respectively. Investment in Wanjiazhai Dam, also part of the capital requirement during
phase I (1993-2001), is a long-term investment, and thus is not subject to cost sharing.
5.16  The cost for SMT consists of the shared GMT cost, the cost to complete SMT,
and the cost of connection works, which in total equal 81.72 percent of total phase I



- 49 -
investment cost including capitalized interest and foreign exchange gain/loss. That is,
only 81.72 percent of total phase I cost will be considered in determination of water
tariffs for TMWC starting from year 2002; the remaining 18.28 percent of cost will
belong to NMT and will not be subject to depreciation until completion of NMT.
5.17  Phase I capital requirements will be financed through GSP surcharges as well as
borrowing. Sources of funds and their uses are assumed to be as follows:
(a)   The World Bank loan of $400 million and the cofinancing loan of $30
million will be used to finance foreign currency requirements for the
proposed project;
(b)   Investment income from Wanjiazhai Dam, assumed to be Y 911 million
during 1999-2001, can be used in project construction;
(c)    GSP surcharges can be used to finance Wanjiazhai Dam and project
activities if a shortage of funds still exists after application of the previous
two financing sources; and
(d)   If a shortage still exists, particularly during 1997-1998, additional
surcharges or incremental water revenue as a result of water tariff
adjustment will be needed.
5.18  At present, the provincial price bureau has authority to determine and adjust water
tariffs within the province. Shanxi Province is aware of the necessity of adjusting water
tariffs to recover the capital investments of the project for repayment of the loans. To
smooth out the impact on users of the impending tariff adjustment, the provincial price
bureau has decided to increase water tariffs gradually from 1997 for all users. The goal of
the proposed tariff adjustment is to price water based on operating costs and debt service
in the year 2002 when the project is put into operation (see Table 5.1). These tariffs are
essentially equal to the long-run marginal cost of water in the year 2000. It is hoped that
such a policy could further stimulate water savings and rationalize the water consumption
structure among different sectors. It should be noted the average tariff ratio for irrigation
water to industrial water is presently 1:10 and with the new tariff structure it will reduce
to 1:4 in the year 2000. These tariffs apply to TMWC-supplied water, which covers the
Yellow River-diverted water and groundwater supplied by TMWC. However, there is
also self-supplied groundwater by industries and enterprises, which is now priced at much
lower tariffs and controlled by the Water Savings Office of Taiyuan Municipality.
During negotiation, assurances were obtainedfrom Shanxi that it would prepare, on the
basis of guidelines acceptable to the Bank. (a) a program by February 15, 1998 to
ensure that, by June 30, 2002, self-supplied groundwater would have the same tariffs as
for all other water supplied in Shanxi; and (b) a program by June 15, 1998 to ensure that
groundwater extraction would also be limited to safe yield levels. Shanxi would
thereafter implement these programs, taking into account the Bank's comments on them.



- 50 -
5.19  Water sales were 144 million m3 in 1995 and therefore the additional water
revenue caused by the above pricing reform action plan would amount to more than Y 1
billion between 1997 and 2000. The provincial price bureau intends to use this additional
revenue to finance either YRDPC or water supply projects within Taiyuan city, or deposit
it partially as a debt repayment fund. Because water tariffs should cover the debt
repayment component after project commissioning, and because financing plans for
YRDPC and TMWC indicate both organizations are short of local counterpart funds for
their investments, it would be more justified if this additional water revenue were used
for both YRDPC and TMWC's project investment instead of debt repayment.

5.20  With a view to obtaining economic efficiency and promoting prudent financial
management, at negotiations assurances were obtained that financial performance
indices for YRDPC and TMWCs' water supply operations will meet the following
requirements:
(a)   YRDPC would take all measures, including but not limited to tariff
adjustments, to ensure a debt service coverage ratio of no less than 1.2
throughout the whole debt repayment period (2003-16), and a rate of
return on two-year average notionally revalued net fixed assets in service
of no less than 2 percentfor year 2011-16.
(b)   Shanxi Province would cause TMWC to take all measures, including but
not limited to tariff adjustments, to ensure that (1) debt/equity ratio is
better than 70/30 between 1997 and 2001, (2) a debt service coverage
ratio of no less than 1.2 is achieved between 2002 and 2016 when YRDPC
is paying off its subsidiary loan; and (3) a rate of return on two-year
notionally revalued net fixed assets in service of no less than 2 percent is
achievedfor the same period of 2002 and thereafter.
E. FUTURE FINANCES
5.21  Table 5.2 illustrates the YRDPC's financial projection summary of a simulated

simulation indicates the projected water tariffs to be charged by YRDPC to TMWC.

projected net losses in such years. Despite forecast net loss, the minimum debt service
coverage ratio of 1.2 can still be met during these years because internal cash generations
(mainly from depreciation) are sufficient to cover principal repayments.

the above financial performance targets. In Table 5.4, the average tariff before year 2002
is the tariff that should be maintained by TMWC for its investment needs, not the higher
tariffs suggested by the tariff reform action plan.

5.23   Sensitivity analysis (see Annex 8) shows that projected water tariffs for YRDPC
and TMWC will be more sensitive to the accuracy of estimated water transfer volume. It
emphasizes the importance of timely completion of the proposed project as well as Huyan
water treatment plant-both phase I and phase II. The system's ability to transfer water
as scheduled and to have an estimated matching capacity to receive transferred water is
critical to realization of the estimated water transfer volume by YRDPC and water supply
volume by TMWC. Reduction in investment income from Wanjiazhai Dam would also
make the projected tariffs higher, because additional water tariff adjustment would be
needed should YRDPC have weaker capability to meet the financial covenants. Cost
overrun of Huyan water treatment plant's phase I cost would impact tariffs by TMWC



- 53 -
since in the current projection model it is assumed that any shortage of funds after
availing of financing from the provincial and municipal governments and TMWC's
internal cash generation will be financed by borrowing.
5.24  The sensitivity analysis shows that the proposed tariffs for YRDPC and TMWC
are less sensitive to cost overrun of the proposed project as it is assumed that a shortage
of capital (after drawing down the World Bank loan, cofinancing loan, investment
income, and GSP surcharges) will be financed by additional surcharges or funds from the
provincial government free of charge. With regard to the proposed reused water, if the
tariff is set at Y 1.5/m3 instead of Y 0.2 as currently assumed, the projected tariffs for
YRDPC and TMWC will drop by about Y 0.5. Therefore, water reuse mechanisms,
including construction and rehabilitation of wastewater treatment plants in Taiyuan and
water allocation and pricing schemes, need to be put in place as early as possible.
F. FUTURE REFORM
5.25  The existing water resource and supply institutions have different priorities in
water allocation, different levels of financial autonomy, and various reporting systems.
These differences have resulted in problems such as insufficient maintenance, lower-than-
market-price tariffs, rigid pricing mechanisms, and questionable water allocations. Water
has to be managed in all its forms from rainfall in the catchment to reaching the sea, with
proper controls through a licensing system. Licenses for surface water or groundwater
abstraction as well as licenses for the return of water to the river basin are necessary to
address allocation problems. Water reuse, time of use, and quality of water should all be
reflected in water pricing. Establishment of an Upper Fen River Basin Corporation
(UFRBC) is thus envisioned under the project, with responsibility for regulatory
functions such as management and administration of a unified licensing and charging
system in Shanxi Province for the Fen River.
5.26  Reforms in financial aspects other than the pricing system are also required. All
water resource and supply institutions and enterprises, to different degrees, should
strengthen their financial viability by gaining better skills in financial, operational, and
capital investment planning, having power to borrow and invest, having rights to propose
tariff adjustment, retaining any revenue surplus for future use, and having the power to
hire and fire.
5.27  Along with institutional and financial reforms, transition from  the current
reporting systems to a unified reporting system in line with international accounting
practices is needed not only for project monitoring but also for better analysis of financial
and operating performance.  All water sector institutions and enterprises, whether
involved in construction or operation, should be able to provide easily understandable
financial reports or consolidated accounts.
5.28  Terms of reference for the proposed institutional study and financial strengthening
are presented in Annex 8.



- 54 -
6. BENEFITS, JUSTIFICATIONS AND RISK
A. GENERAL
6.1   The primary motivation for Phase I of the proposed project is to alleviate severe
water shortages in Shanxi's Province's capital and main industrial center Taiyuan, and its
surrounding areas.
6.2   In 1993, water demands were estimated to be 33.5 percent greater than water
available from sustainable local supplies. By 2000, the deficit will be 50 percent and
continue to grow thereafter. Current water deficits in Taiyuan constrain economic growth
and employment, and limit Shanxi's ability to export coal, industrial products, and
electricity. Since much of the existing industrial capacity will be idled without sufficient
water supplies by the year 2000, relaxing this constraint for industry alone generates
sufficient economic benefits to justify the project.
6.3    Water shortages also  inflict hardships on  municipal consumers, create
environmental damage as the groundwater is depleted, and hamper the ability of farmers
to meet agricultural demands. Municipal consumers suffer from periodic interruptions of
service and perpetually low water pressures. Many people living above the third floor are
continually without water. Groundwater has been overextracted by 15 percent or more
every year since 1990. This exploitation has led to rapidly falling water tables (reaching
145 m in some places), a growing cone of depression under Taiyuan City and increasing
incidences of land subsidence. The proposed project water would halt overextraction of
groundwater and permit a gradual natural replenishment of the aquifers. No attempt was
made to measure these environmental benefits. Between 1987 and 1993, farmers in the
Taiyuan project area saw their irrigation supplies decline from 207 million m3 to 68
million. In part, this was due to water tables dropping below their capacity to pump, but
mostly the water was transferred to industrial uses, either voluntarily through sales or
involuntarily. The Project would preserve farmers' rights to their local water supplies,
and provide them with increased supplies of treated waste and sewage water for use in
irrigating crops.
B. PROJECT BENEFITS
6.4   Water Demand Projections. The benefits from increased water supplies depend
in large part on the estimates of future water demand. Recent statistical evidence
suggests that the water demand projections presented in Chapter 2 may be too
conservative.2 Those projections assumed an urban population growth rate of 1.2 percent
2 Those projections were made by local Project staff and the Tianjin Design Institute.



- 55 -
between 1993 and 2000, whereas the growth in 1993-95 was nearly 3 percent per year.
They also projected the growth in industrial capacity at 8 percent to 2000, whereas recent
growth has been about twice that. The appraisal mission in 1996 produced a revised set
of water demand projections for household and municipal (H&M) demand, as well as
industrial demands, based on more recent trends, and careful forecasts worked out by the
Taiyuan Water Company. The revised projections include a breakdown into three cases:
"low" (which produces results similar to the earlier projections), "medium" (the most
likely case), and "high". In the medium case, urban population grows at 2.1 percent from
1995 to 2000, then slows to 1.4 percent. Industrial capacity grows at 10 percent from
1995 to 2000, then slows to 7.5 percent and 5 percent in the following decades. The
results of these new projections are summarized in Table 6.1.
TABLE 6.1: WATER DEMAND PROJECTIONS, SUPPLIES, AND SHORTAGES
(million m3)
M&I Demand           M&I Demand less GW           Shortage %
Year    Low    Medium    High    Low    Medium   High    Low    Medium    High

6.5   Increased Human Consumption.3 Per capita urban consumption in Taiyuan was
91 liters/capita/day (l/c/d) in 1993 compared to the average for large Chinese cities of
210 l/c/d. A recent survey found that in half of households, per capita consumption was
less than 40 l/c/d. The project will permit urban consumption to reach China-wide norms
of 200 l/c/d by the year 2000. Water supplies will be available thereafter to parallel the
increase in population. It is estimated that urban consumption will increase by nearly 50
percent between 1993 and 2000.
6.6    Typically, willingness-to-pay (WTP) is used to measure the economic benefits to
increased urban consumption. WTP for urban water is observed to be about Y 1.8/m3 in
some coastal cities of China and is projected to be Y 4.3/mi3 in Taiyuan by 2002.
However, WTP is only valid for small increments in the water supply not for the large
changes that the proposed project will engender. Instead, a more conservative measure of
benefits, consumers' surplus (CS) is used. CS has been estimated from consumer
3 Includes consumption by commerce also.



- 56 -
demand functions to be Y 2.5/m3 for Taiyuan in 2000, rising to 4.5 in 2020. These
economic values are lower than the amortized cost of project water, but the increased
supplies generate unmeasured social benefits.
6.7    Benefits from Increased Industrial Consumption. Shanxi industries have made
heroic efforts to conserve water. Shanxi industries surpassed China-wide norms for the
year 2000 for recycling in 1991 and achieved an 84.3 percent recycling rate in 1993.
Production function analysis shows that most investment in Shanxi industries in recent
years went toward increasing the productivity of water. Yet water remains the overriding
constraint on industry. It is estimated that 1993 industrial output could have been Y 6.5
billion greater (22 percent) had there been no water shortage. Factories have been
observed to shut down periodically when water is not available.
6.8    Given an alleviation of the water shortage, Shanxi industrial capacity will
continue to grow based on the natural resource endowment and location of the province.
With additional water, annual industrial output is projected to increase by 75 percent
between 1993 and 2000. Without additional water, output is likely to stagnate near
current levels. Table 2.7 showed projected industrial output with and without the Project.
6.9   The benefits from the water delivered to mines and industries by the proposed
project are based on the increase in industrial economic value added4 due to the water
delivered. A simple calculation based on average value added per unit of water shows
that the return to water might be as high as Y 67/m3.  But the calculation is not
straightforward, since the benefits vary by industrial sector, by project area (because the
sectoral mix is different), by future year, and by degree of underutilized existing
industrial capacity. The calculations are further complicated by the fact that Shanxi
industries are highly interdependent: the power sector buys a great deal of coal, without
which additional water would have little impact on power production and hence
economic value added. By using a linear programming model derived from a Shanxi
input-output model, the measures of additional value added per unit of water shown in
Table 6.2 were obtained.
6.10   These values represent benefits applying to unutilized (because of water shortage)
industrial capacity beginning in 2000. After 2000, benefits will slowly decline to the
perceived long-run cost of water, which we assume to be about 5.8-the announced
future price of water to industries in Taiyuan. By 2029, all plant in existence in 2000 will
have been replaced with modern technology based on the 5.8 price, and benefits from
project water will have fallen to that level. It should be noted that this technology will be
far more conserving of water than that currently in operation, largely based on the
assumption of free water.
4 Economic value added is the sum of payments to factors of production (labor, capital, etc.) adjusted
where necessary for distortions due to taxes, subsidies, and/or price controls.

6.12    Benefits to Farmers.  Irrigated farming can be quite profitable near the large
cities if high-valued fruits and vegetables are grown. But average returns to irrigation are
quite low  at about Y 2/m3, which explains why farmers sometimes sell their water to
industry. The proposed project water will be far too expensive to use for irrigation
directly. Nevertheless, farmers in the project areas will stand to benefit indirectly. First,
their shares of both surface and groundwater will be preserved at current levels. Second,
the subsidence of water tables due to overextraction will cease. Currently, water tables



- 58 -
are dropping toward levels at which farm pumps may cease to operate. Over time, water
tables should again rise to stable depths, making it easier for farmers to access
groundwater. Third, there will be vastly greater supplies of treated sewage and industrial
waste water available for irrigation. The planned schedule of water treatment allows the
volumes shown in column 4 of Table 6.3 to become available to farmers in the project
area and those downstream in the Fen River Basin.
6.13   Economic benefits of irrigation water supplies are estimated to be Y 4.0/mi3 within
the Taiyuan Project Area and Y 1.2/mi3 downstream. The former area has higher returns
because the water transport distances are shorter (lower losses) and it has a higher
percentage of high-valued fruits and vegetables in its cropping pattern. The projected
supplies of irrigation water shown in Table 6.2 receive primary and secondary treatment,
or oxidation ponding. Priority will likely be given to farmers in the Taiyuan Project area,
up to the limit of their demands of 108 million m3/year. The remaining water will be
released to the Fen River for use by irrigators below Taiyuan. It has been verified that
downstream shortages in irrigation water are sufficiently large to absorb these volumes.
The use of the treated water in this manner will ultimately double the incomes of farm
families receiving it.
C. PRICES, TARIFFS, AND COST RECOVERY
6.14 The investment costs, including physical contingencies, and operation and
maintenance costs have been taken into account in estimating the economic costs of the
project. Economic O&M costs include electricity (valued at Y 0.35/kWh),5 management,
maintenance, overhaul and repair, and replacement costs of pumps and other equipment, all
expressed as economic values. Locally supplied components were evaluated at an exchange
rate of Y 8.3 to $1. Agricultural labor has been valued at Y 10 per day, the prevailing
market rate in the region. World Bank commodity price projections (1996) are used to
estimate farm-gate economic prices in constant 1995 terms for traded outputs and inputs.
6.15   The economic capital costs of water transfer from the project are Y 4.5/m3. The
financial costs are Y 4.86/m3. These levels, however, are far above present water tariffs
ranging from Y 1.0/mi3 for households to Y 1.2/m3 for industries.  As described in
Annex 7, the economic and social returns from Project water are sufficient to justify
tariffs that will result in full cost recovery. Accordingly, the Shanxi Province Price
Bureau has taken steps to increase water tariffs in Taiyuan in a stepwise fashion, so that
residential consumers will be paying Y 3.66/m3 by 2000, and industries, Y 5.78/m3,
averaging Y 4.94/mi3 (see Table 5.1). These levels have been justified through WTP
analysis (on the part of households) and financial profit analysis (for industries). They
will not only ensure full cost recovery, but provide incentives for more efficient water
use. Farmers who use treated wastewater from the project will be required to pay
Y 0.5/m3 to recover treatment and distribution costs.
5 The shadow price used in the Bank's power projects.



- 59 -
D. ECONOMIC EVALUATION
6.16  Project investment (main works, water treatment and water supply networks for
Taiyuan City), incremental operation and management cost streams, and benefits streams
are presented in Annex 7. Based on the assumptions contained in these cost and benefit
streams and a 50-year project life, the economic internal rate of return (EIRR) of the
project, excluding water treatment, is estimated to be 22.0 percent. The net present value
discounted at 12 percent (to 1995) is Y 10,316 million. Increased urban consumption
generates 7 percent of these benefits, and industrial consumption 85 percent and irrigation
10 percent. These returns exclude any difficult-to-measure environmental benefits from
the elimination of groundwater mining, and from the removal of health risks associated
with inadequate urban water supply.
E. ENVIRONMENTAL BENEFITS
6.17  The project would improve the economic potential and living conditions in the
most economically productive region of Shanxi Province.  This region is already
critically short of water for virtually all human development needs including (a) domestic
(b) industrial, and (c) agricultural needs. The project produced water will be used first for
residential purposes to meet consumer demands for drinking water, second for industries
to maintain and increase output and employment, and third for agriculture to maintain
and improve productivity. It is also planned to reduce the use of freshwater resources for
agriculture by substituting use of treated sewage effluents. In addition, the project-
produced water will reduce pressures on groundwater resources, already very serious and
causing serious economic/socioeconomic losses to all affected community sectors. The
reduction in groundwater use will also have important effects on small streams in
maintaining year-round flow and restoring the aquatic ecology. The project will also
make major strides to reduce pollution in the Fen River and thereby make more water
available for municipal purposes downstream.
6.18  The argument may be made that the project favors the socioeconomics of the
urbanized target areas, which are less poor than the rural areas. This is true, but it is these
target areas that are of prime importance to the macro economy and socioeconomic
standards of the entire province. The huge increase in industrial employment anticipated
with the project (Table 6.4) will obviously represent the main opportunity for masses of
farm youth to escape poverty. Also, where reservoirs are to be built as part of the project,
it is planned that the resettled rural farmers will be benefited, that is, their housing and
income levels will be improved as a result of the resettlement.
F. EMPLOYMENT AND INCOMES
6.19  Industries in the Taiyuan Project Area generated about 744,600 jobs in 1994, with
a total wage bill of Y 3,253 million. Table 6.4 shows the projected path of employment



- 60 -
and incomes with and without the project.6 In addition, farmers in the project area and
downstream will generate approximately Y 1,250 million of agricultural output from the
use of project wastewater, with associated gains of 35,000 person-years of employment
and Y 400 million of farm incomes.

G. PROJECT RISKS
6.20   The proposed project faces a variety of risks which could affect its performance.
These fall under the general headings: physical, finance, implementation and operational
risks, institutional/policy risks, environmental/long-term sustainability and economic.
Each type of risk is discussed in turn. Where possible, the economic cash flow model is
used to estimate the impact on the project's performance of a failure to avert the risk, and
the results summarized in Table 6.5 below.

- 61 -
6.21   The major physical risks involve construction mishaps and delays and seismic
events, which could destroy the tunnels and connecting works. Construction risks are
deemed to be within manageable limits given close supervision by foreign and Chinese
experts. Nevertheless, a 10 percent cost increase has been included in the base case cash
flow model. The probability of an earthquake of a sufficient magnitude (7.0 Richter
scale) to damage the works is estimated to be less than 0.01 percent over the construction
period. As shown in Table 6.5, a 50 percent increase in construction costs (after 1996)
would still yield a rate of return of 16.3 percent, and a two-year delay in obtaining any
benefits would still produce a 16.3 percent return.
6.22   An additional concern is that future flows in the Yellow River may be insufficient
to supply the Project's diversions. A simulation over the 56 years of available hydrologic
data showed that sufficient water will be available in all months and all years if upstream
diversions are held to currently agreed provincial allocation limits.
6.23   Financing risks arise from the finance scheme, which relies on general revenues
for only 10 percent of cofinancing, with the remainder from special taxation of water,
electricity and coal. The taxation scheme is already functioning and generating the
revenues projected for it, and there has been no reported resistance to it on the part of the
major coal and power producers. The impacts of a shortfall in any of the finance
components would be equivalent to a physical risk resulting in a one-year delay in project
benefits, and is deemed manageable.
6.24  Implementation and operational risks involve failures to deliver the needed
volumes of water in a timely fashion. To prepare for the complexities of implementing
and operating the project, YRDPB was established at an early stage, and staffed with
carefully selected local experts. The staff has been supplemented by foreign consultants
who will continue to assist and oversee the operation. A failure in implementation and
operation resulting in a one-year delay in achieving benefits would reduce the IRR from
22 percent to 18.8 percent. A continuing operational problem that would result in 20
percent lower water deliveries throughout the life of the project would reduce the IRR to
15.6 percent (Table 6.5).
6.25  Institutional/policy risks involve resistance to the proposed water tariff structure
or other factors that would result in low or uneven water fee collections. Such failures
would adversely affect the locally-financed component (see above).  If the water
authorities fail to charge the same price for all water of the same quality (project water
and existing ground and surface water), then the latter sources will continue to be
overexploited, and the demand for project water will be less than projected. This type of
risk is being addressed through strict water pricing covenants covering all water sources.
6.26   Environmental/sustainability risks would arise from a failure to adequately treat
and reuse incremental industrial waste and sewage water facilitated by the project. Such
a failure would result in environmental damage downstream of Taiyuan, and to the entire
lower reach of the Yellow River. Covenants to be negotiated would ensure that releases
to the Fen and Yellow rivers will be within safe levels according to Chinese



- 62 -
environmental standards. There is also a significant economic and income-distributional
benefit to treatment and reuse. Without this component the IRR would fall from 22.0
percent to 20.6 percent.
6.27  The major economic risks associated with the project are related to the projections
of industrial output and water demand by firms in the project areas. A lengthy economic
depression in China could cause the demand for Shanxi exports to fall and thereby reduce
the need for WWTP water. However, even under the "Low" assumptions about
economic growth (8 percent until 2000, then 5 percent), the rate of return is still above 18
percent. The "Low" growth rate is about half of Shanxi's recent experience.
6.28  Several other critical assumptions in the EIRR analysis were tested for sensitivity.
These tests are summarized in Table 6.5. In each case, the economic performance of the
project remains acceptable. The project would remain viable even if a combination of
unforeseen cost increases and delays should occur. If all post-1996 construction costs
should increase by 50 percent, operating costs increase by 50 percent, and all benefits
were delayed one year, the EIRR would still be 12.3 percent and the cost-benefit ratio
would be above 1.
H. PROJECT AFFORDABILITY
6.29  The proposed project water will be significantly more expensive than any water
source used in the project area to date. In 1993, industrial consumers paid an average of
Y l.1/m3 and urban residents, Y 0.7/mi3. Local authorities have already begun to increase
water prices, with the objective by the year of 2000 of Y 5.8/m3 for industrial users,
Y 3.66 for urban residents, and Y 0.6 to Y 1.5 for irrigators. The price structure was
designed to recover project costs as well as take into consideration the ability-to-pay of
consumers.
6.30  With the eventual prices in place, will the consumers be able to afford project
water? In the case of industrial users, the answer is unequivocally yes. Input-output
analysis has shown that marginal financial returns to all industries combined was about
3                                                            3
Y 20/mi in 1990, and rising rapidly. By 2000, it will be in the range of Y 50/mr.
6.31   Farm budget analysis has shown that the marginal returns to irrigation are in the
range of Y 1.1 to Y 5/m3 or more and have been rising recently as agricultural commodity
prices have increased. Even with the higher prices projected, irrigation charges will still
amount to only 6 percent of farm budgets in 2000 for grain production, and 20 percent for
vegetables.
6.32  Urban residents may be the most likely group to suffer from the increased water
charges since their costs are scheduled to increase fivefold over 1993 levels. However,
household budget information reveals that only 0.4 percent of household income went for
water in 1993, and this will rise to only 0.75 percent in 2000 with the higher water tariffs
and projected income increases. Under the unlikely possibility of no increase in urban
incomes, water would still comprise only about 2 percent of household budgets.



- 63 -
7. AGREEMENTS REACHED AND RECOMMENDATION
7.1   During negotiations, assurances were obtained from:
(a)   the Borrower that it will prepare, by December 31, 1998, a satisfactory
maintenance program and an emergency preparedness plan for the dam.
Also, the Borrower will employ independent experts with qualifications,
experience and terms of reference acceptable to the Bank to inspect the dam,
associated works and structures regularly starting October 31, 1998,
according to sound engineering practices, to determine if any of the
structural conditions, maintenance or operating methods could endanger
safety. Inspections would be conducted annually for the first five years of
operations and every three years thereafter (para. 3.6);
(b)   the Borrower will (a) complete the resettlement for the Wanjiazhai Dam
according to a resettlement action plan that has been found acceptable to the
Bank; (b) employ by February 15, 1998 an independent monitoring
institution with qualifications, experience and terms of reference acceptable
to the Bank to evaluate semiannually the socioeconomic progress of those
resettled from the dam site; and (c) provide the Bank with a report of each
evaluation along with proposals for meeting the resettlement plan targets, if
they have not been achieved. (para. 3.7);
(c)   Shanxi Province that it will continue its program of dam maintenance for
Fen- 1 dam; also that the Province and independent experts with
qualifications, experience and terms of reference acceptable to the Bank
would, not later than February 15, 1998, inspect the dam-associated works
and structures, according to sound engineering practices. This would be
done to determine if any of the structures, maintenance or operating methods
are deficient and may compromise their safety. Shanxi will biannually
submit the experts' reports to the Bank, starting with the first report by
November 15, 1998 (para. 3.13);
(d)   Shanxi that it will establish through Taiyuan an SMO with terms of
reference, staffing and other resources acceptable to the Bank by
December 31, 1997 to appraise subprojects, provide subloans and
supervise, monitor, and report on subprojects in accordance with an
operations manual's procedures and on terms and conditions satisfactory
to the Bank (para. 3.33);
(e)   Shanxi that all proposed complementary works for Taiyuan water supply
and wastewater collection and treatment and associated resettlement



- 64 -
activities would be implemented as planned in a program acceptable to the
Bank so as to be completed by June 30, 2002. Shanxi will provide the
Bank on a semiannual basis (by each April 15 and October 15) details of
the designs, financing and construction progress for Taiyuan water supply
and sewerage plans implemented in the preceding semester and plans for
construction for the 12 months ahead (para. 3.42);
(f)    YRDPC  will employ  by  September 15, 1997  an  independent
environment/resettlement panel of experts to review and make
recommendations with respect to the EMP. Further, it shall maintain an
Environmental Management Office with competent staff in adequate
numbers and with responsibilities and functions acceptable to the Bank to
manage, coordinate and monitor the EMP. Also, it shall ensure that all
activities under the project will comply with the EMP (para. 3.49);
(g)   Shanxi that resettlement agencies at the provincial, county and township
levels will carry out the resettlement under the project according to the
RAP. YRDPC will (a) also carry out resettlement according to the RAP;
(b) employ an independent monitoring institution by September 15, 1997,
acceptable to the Bank, to evaluate the socioeconomic progress of those
affected by resettlement; (c) employ an independent panel of environment/
resettlement experts by September 15, 1997, satisfactory to the Bank, to
review the resettlement semiannually (see para. 3.49); (d) promptly furnish
to the Bank, for comment, reports of the findings and recommendations of
both groups; and (e) implement all recommendations for follow-up
actions, taking account of the Bank's comments (para. 3.57);
(h)   YRDPC would submit to the Bank by December 15 of each year the
financing plan and the proposed implementation program for the next year
as approved by the Government, and thereafter implement this program,
taking into account the Bank's views on it (para. 3.65);
(i)   Shanxi that it would onlend the proceeds of the Bank loan (other than for
the Institutional Support Program for Shanxi Government) to YRDPC,
under a subsidiary loan agreement with a 20-year term, including 5 years
of grace at the same interest rate and commitment fee as those paid by the
Borrower. The execution of a subsidiary loan agreement between Shanxi
and YRDPC, satisfactory to the Bank, would be a condition of
effectiveness of the loan (para. 3.66);
(j)    YRDPC shall, by April 30 of each year and for three years after
completion of the project, furnish to the Bank an annual monitoring report,
in a format acceptable to the Bank, covering all components of the project,
in conformance with the agreed monitoring indicators.  In addition,
YRDPC will provide semiannual progress reports by April 15 and October

15 of the project implementation in the previous semester including any

(1)    YRDPC will maintain its project management office with competent staff

(o)   YRDPC will prepare under guidelines acceptable to the Bank and furnish

make the transition (para. 4.16);
(p)   Shanxi that each year incremental revenues from tariff adjustments and
increased water resources fees between 1997 and 2001 will be allocated to
YRDPC by December 31 of that year as a capital contribution to YRDPC
(para. 5.9);
(q)   Shanxi that it would prepare, on the basis of guidelines acceptable to the
Bank: (a) a program by February 15, 1998 to ensure that, by June 30,
2002, self-supplied groundwater would have the same tariffs as for all
other water supplied in Shanxi; and (b) a program by June 15, 1998 to

levels. Shanxi would thereafter implement these programs, taking into

(r)    YRDPC and Shanxi Province that financial performance indices for

(i)    YRDPC would take all measures, including but not limited to tariff
adjustments, to ensure a debt service coverage ratio of no less than
1.2 throughout the whole debt repayment period (2003-16), and a

(ii)   Shanxi Province would cause TMWC to take all measures,

debt/equity ratio is better than 70/30 between 1997 and 2001; (2) a

(3) a rate of return on two-year notionally revalued net fixed assets

of 2002 and thereafter (para. 5.20).

single currency Bank loan of $400 million to the People's Republic of China. The loan
would be for a term of 20 years, including a grace period of 5 years, at the Bank's
standard interest rate for LIBOR-based US dollar denominated single currency loans.

D. CONTROLS AND AUTOMATION
It is understood that the major tasks of the control and automation systems are:
* Flow Control
* Economic Flow Dispatching
* Systemwide Normal Starting and Stopping
* Automation Operation Schemes and Coordination for Emergency Events
* Operation Monitoring
* Water Pricing
Control Levels
A three-level control and automation structure is a good approach for the project.
This concept is supported by the World Bank Team. The major functionality of the three
control levels is summarized as follows:
Level One (local or Pump Unit Level) Control
- Signal unit starting and stopping
* Automatic actions in case of a local emergency
3 Data collection and transmission to Level Two and Three
Level Two (Control/Pumping Station Level) Control
* Management of pump units: operating, maintenance, standby, if the control
station is local at pump station.
* Gate and valve control
* Display important information at local control centers
* Data collection and transmission to Level Three
* Logs and reports printing for local usage
* Allowing operators to perform some "remote control" functions.
Level Three (Diversion or Central Dispatching Level ) Control
* Management of the whole hydraulic network
* Remote control of key equipment
* Major emergency response schemes
* Global status information of all important equipment
* Operation planning and dispatching strategy
* Systemwide key information display
* Data treatment, storage, analysis and statistics
* Logs and reports printing for diversion usage
