- 74 -                      ANNEX l
PART 2: COMPLEMENTARY WORKS: TAIYUAN CITY
1.    Taiyuan Municipality (area 7,000 kM2, population 2.6 millions in 1991) comprises
three urban districts (the City proper), two suburban districts, three counties and the Gujiao
City. Most of the population (about 1.8 million), is concentrated in Taiyuan City (170 km2)
and in the suburban districts. [These are also an estimated 300,000 "temporary" residents,
i.e., people without official residence permits living and working in the main urban areas.]
The area is heavily industrialized (coal mining, power generation, steel, heavy machinery
and chemicals). About 85 percent of industry is concentrated in Taiyuan City, about 10
percent is located in the northern outskirts and 4 percent in Gujiao. In 1993 there was about
23,600 ha of irrigated agriculture in the Municipal area. This annex reviews the current
situation for both water supply and wastewater in Taiyuan and goes on to identify the works
that will be required and are being planned to efficiently utilize the transferred water. The
concomitant institutional and operational improvements necessary and the associated
financing plans are also discussed.
A. WATER SUPPLY
2.    Water Agencies. The Municipality has in place a number of agencies responsible
for water supply, including:
(a)   the Taiyuan Municipal Water Company (TMWC), which provides water to
some 1.45 million people and industries in the main urban areas covering
about  O0 km;
(b)   the Municipal Water Resources Management Commission, which provides
water to the suburban districts and the counties, as well as the power and
steel industries, and for agriculture;
(c)   the Gujiao Water Supply Company; and
(d)   industries that operate their own sources (around 558 wells) serving both
industrial and domestic needs.
The Tianjin Investigation and Design Institute (TIDI), consultants to The Yellow River
Development Project (YRDP), has carried out comprehensive studies of the water resources
available to the Municipality as a whole, and has prepared water demand forecasts. The
Southwest Municipal Engineering Design and Research Institute of China (SMEDRIC) has
prepared prefeasibility studies for the water supply works required to utilize project water,
and has also prepared forecasts of demand and resources available for the city area.
3.    Present Water Resources. Both surface water and groundwater are used within
the Municipality, although the latter dominates (over 80 percent) for municipal/domestic
and industrial uses. Groundwater abstractions are estimated by the Municipal Water
Resources Commission to be 960,000 m3/day (350 million m3/year) compared to a safe
yield (estimated by TIDI) of 840,000 m3/day (307 million m3/year).  Most of the



- 75 -                     ANNEX I
overabstraction is in the urban area, where current (1994) abstraction rates are about
650,000 m3/day, compared with an estimated safe yield of around 410,000 m3/day
(SMEDRIC). Groundwater levels have declined by some 2 to 3 m per year, natural springs
and wells have dried up and local ground subsidence has occurred, in some areas of the city.
Coal mining activities compound these problems. Abstraction depths are over 200 m in the
south of the city, but less to the north (around 120 m). Unless alternate sources of water are
developed, the City will face a declining level of urban and industrial activity, under use of
land, energy and mineral resources, increase in environmental pollution and lower living
standards.
4.    Groundwater abstraction is regulated through a renewable (five years) permit
system, instituted in 1993. All requests for new wells are subject to review and approval by
the Municipal Water Resources Commission. Wells are metered and allocated abstraction
quotas by the Water Saving Office of the Urban Construction Commission (UCC). Heavy
fines are imposed for overabstraction.  However, basic abstraction charges are low
(Y 0.11 /m3 for industry and Y 0.10 for domestic users). TMWC is exempt from these
charges. Per capita supply for domestic/municipal uses in 1993 was around 120 liters/day,
of which residential use was 70 liters/day, as estimated by TMWC. These are among the
lowest in the nation. In some areas, residential use is even less, due to local supply
constraints. Estimated uses and sources of water in the Municipality and in the TMWC
service area in 1993 are summarized in Table I below.
TABLE 1: USE AND SOURCES OF WATER IN TAIYUAN, 1993
Municipality                TMWC Service Area
'000 ma/day    %'000 m3/day 
Water Uses
Commercial/Industrial    696         58                 567          74
Domestic/Municipal       288         24                 197          26
Rural Domestic             19          1
Irrigation                186        16
Other                     11           1
Total                1,200        100                764          100
Water Sources
Groundwater              964         80                 644          84
Surface Water/Reuse      236         20                 120          16
Total                1,200        100                764          100
Sources: Municipality data from Environmental Impact Assessment, Shanxi University,
April 1995. City data from Taiyuan Water Supply Prefeasibility Study,
Southwest Municipal Engineering Design and Research Institute of China.



- 76 -                      ANNEX 1
5.    TMWC serves the main urban and industrial area and operates nine water
distribution/treatment plants and five booster pump stations. TMWC sources are dominated
by three major well fields: Lan Cun (300,000 m3/day); Xi Zhang (70,000 m3/day); and
Zaogou (70,000 m3/day). TMWC has other, minor, active sources totaling 23,000 m3/day.
Total supply by the water company in 1993 averaged around 415,000 m3/day (151 million
m 3/year), with annual sales of 140 million m 3/year, of which an estimated 53 million m3
were domestic/municipal, 10 million m3 institutional and 77 million m3 industrial/
commercial. Supply in 1994 increased to 420,000 m3/day. About 240 factories have their
own supply systems, abstracting groundwater (serving both industrial and domestic uses)
from around 558 wells. In 1993, abstractions totaled 270,000 m3/day (98 million m3/year),
rising to 290,000 m3/day in 1994. The Water Savings Office estimates that 40 percent of
water abstracted by factories is used for domestic purposes for workers living on the factory
site. Industry is reported to reuse about 30,000 m /day (11 million m3/year) after treatment,
and the power and steel industries use about 90,000 m3/day (33 million m3/year) of surface
water abstracted from the East and West irrigation canals, which pass through the city.
6.    Water Treatment Facilities, Distribution System and Supply Zones. Water
abstracted from the wells is generally of good quality. Treatment of groundwater comprises
chlorination conforming with national standards. Water quality is tested both by TMWC
and by the Public Health Bureau. Treatment for industrial self-supplies presumably reflect
end use (i.e., disinfected for domestic use; untreated for basic industrial uses). Surface
waters abstracted by the power station and steel mill are used, after settlement, as cooling
and wash water.
7.    TMWC serves three principal service areas in the City:  west, northeast and
southeast. Each of these areas have a number of subzones controlled by five booster pump
stations and seven forwarding stations at which additional disinfection is applied. The
system is designed to be pressurized by pumps, with no water towers or reservoirs. Some
enterprises have constructed their own storage facilities and many apartment blocks have
roof tanks. The shortage of water in some areas is attributable both to inadequate supply
and to deficiencies in the network. The western service area is fed from the Xi Zhang
wellfield, whereas the east bank is fed from both the Lan Cun and Zaogou wellfields.
Trunk mains from Xizhang are 700 and 900 mm dia. and from Lan Cun twin 1,000 mm dia.
pipes supplying the power station and steel mill, and an 1,100 mm dia. pipe supplying the
central city area; 600 and 700 mm dia. pipes from Zaogou connect to the Lan Cun trunk
mains. The Lan Cun source has the highest quality and is also the cheapest to operate
(lowest pumping head). It is thus generally used to provide the base load.
8.    Trunk mains are generally concrete or steel with cast iron secondary mains, now
between 10 and 30 years old. Tertiary distribution and house connections are generally
galvanized iron. Leakage, which forms almost all unaccounted-for water (UfW), is reported
to be only 6 to 7 percent, based on readings taken at "bulk" meters (there are 3,000 such
meters in the whole city and TMWC charging is based on readings at these). This does not
take account of downstream distribution losses and also reflects the low pressures within the
system. Losses per unit length of trunk main are estimated to be 44 m /km/day. The total



- 77 -                      ANNEX I
UfW is estimated at 30 percent presently. The UfW is expected to be kept lower (about 20
percent) because of complete rehabilitation of the distribution system before 2001 (see
Table 4). In addition, it is planned to have a UfW study to identify the main sources of
UfW. Distribution is controlled by manual adjustment of valves and pumps. Information
on pressures at 20 monitoring points in the network is telemetered to the head office, from
where decisions on the dispatch of water are made. In view of the water shortage, parts of
the network are shut down in turn, to ensure that water is available to all areas in rotation.
Large enterprises, such as the Steel Mill and Chemical Works, have their own distribution
systems.
9.    Resource and System Management and Water Charges. There is an underlying
problem of a lack of overall coordinated resource management; the inability of TMWC to
deliver adequate supplies has led, in the urban area, to what is effectively a "free for all,"
with industrial, domestic and agricultural users competing for a dwindling supply of
groundwater. While the Municipal Water Resources Commission is attempting to control
the situation through the abstraction permit system, a more rational approach to the
conjunctive management of surface and groundwater is being developed. In particular,
further regulatory measures will need to be taken to curb and rationally allocate individual
groundwater abstractions, as this source will be cheaper than project water. As pressures in
the system are increased, water wastage and system losses could become significant. A
comprehensive water conservation, leak detection and prevention program is required.
Business and domestic users are metered as bulk supplies, with residents in an apartment
block, for example, being required to organize their own sharing of water charges. There
are around 330,000 domestic meters. As noted earlier, a groundwater abstraction charge is
collected from enterprises with their own wells. TMWC charges for treated water (1995)
are Y 1.1/m3 for business users and Y 0.7/m3 for domestic/municipal users and for
government institutions.
10.   Economic Growth and Water Demand Forecasts. Forecasts are prepared by the
Municipal Government and indicate significant growth in a number of areas. Principal
features are summarized in Table 2. Forecasts of water demand have been made both by
TIDI for the Municipality as a whole, and by SMEDRIC for the TMWC service area.
These forecasts are difficult to compare, as they are not based on the same assumptions.
Furthermore, while TIDI has focused on industrial and agricultural forecasts, with limited
attention to domestic/municipal demands, SMEDRIC has focused on domestic/municipal
demands with industrial demand forecasts being relatively crude and agricultural/rural
demands omitted. Clearly, the forecasts are intended for different purposes; TIDI's is
intended to provide an overall view of the projected municipal water balance, whereas
SMEDRIC's is intended as a guide to capacity requirements within TMWC's service area.
As the project water will be transferred to TMWC, the SMEDRIC forecasts have been
adopted for the planning of the treatment and distribution works. Detailed forecasts were
also prepared based on economic growth parameters and taking account of price elasticity
effects (see Annex 7).



- 78 -                     ANNEX 1
11.   Water Distribution System. The SMEDRIC report compares the options of a
single water treatment plant at Huyan for all of the transferred water against one plant at
Huyan and a second nearer the city center, each treating part of the flow. The conclusion
reached is that a single plant at Huyan is the most cost-effective solution. The major part
of the city can be supplied by gravity from this location, although fringe areas require
pumping stations to boost supplies. The report reviews the use of three booster pump
stations compared to two, and concludes that two will provide the most economic
solution. Pipeline sizing has been optimized using a network analysis program provided
by Tongji University. Storage of 10 percent of daily capacity will be provided for
regulation purposes. A separate low quality supply of 56,000 m3/day for power station
No. 2 is provided by omitting some of the processes for part of the flow. The possibility
of supplying other industrial areas with low quality water has also been examined, both
for a complete dual distribution system for the whole city and also for a partial system for
the principal industrialized areas. The conclusion reached is that dual supplies, whether in
general or to targeted industrial areas, would add significantly to costs due to the
additional pipework required.

12.    It has been agreed that TMWC will be responsible for the treatment and distribution
of the whole of the project water resource delivered to Taiyuan and has signed a
memorandum of understanding with YRDPO to this effect. The new treatment works at
Huyan and associated distribution networks will present a new challenge for TMWC, both
in view of its size and the modem treatment technologies required. It will therefore be
important to ensure that technical assistance and training are provided, both for
management and systems design and O&M. The conjunctive management of the existing
and future resources and system design will depend on the locations of major centers of
demand (particularly large industry), and the policy regarding the use of groundwater.
Groundwater abstractions in the urban area are predicted to reduce from around 650,000
m3/day in 1994 to 410,000 mr3/day in 2020. A rational approach to groundwater allocations
could result in significant cost savings, in terms of treatment capacity and pipework
required. A operational analysis using a dynamic computer network model is to be carried
out to assist in planning, design and specification of new works. SMEDRIC future resource
and demand forecasts are summarized in Table 3 below. The investigation of the upstream
connecting works by Shanxi Investigation and Design Institute for Water Resources (SIDI)
suggests an appropriate interim step in transfers. The timing of utilization of project
transfers can be reviewed, as demand develops, and it may be possible to surcharge the
treatment works should deficits develop in interim years while additional capacity is being
developed.

- 80-                       ANNEX 1
13.   Project Water Delivery aind Treatment. Water from the project tunnel will be
delivered at the new TMWC water treatment plant at Huyan at the north end of the City.
The Huyan treatment works will have an initial capacity of 1.0 million m3/day and an
ultimate capacity of 1.87 million m3/day. The treatment processes will take into account the
raw water quality-a varying mix of Yellow River water and local runoff, with high
turbidity and suspended solids, especially during the flood season. The SMEDRIC studies
recommend the following process: Presedimentation using open radial flow clarifiers with
provision for the addition of polymer coagulating agents; rapid mechanical mixing tanks for
coagulant dosing and pH adjustment; mechanical flocculators; rectangular, baffled
settlement tanks; conventional rapid gravity sand filters with air/water backwashing;
disinfection tanks for liquid chlorine and ammonia dosing; and treated water storage of 15
percent of the daily flow. TMWC are proposing to build the presedimentation tanks at the
Fen-1 reservoir site. These would only be operated during the flood season. Given that
the Fen-1 reservoir acts as a silt trap, the need for these tanks remains to be established,
following  further study  and  water quality  analysis.   The  requirements  for
presedimentation will be reviewed during the detailed design phase. Sludge generated by
the treatment processes is to be discharged to river under the current Phase 1 feasibility
design, with proper treatment facilities being provided in Phase 2. This decision will be
reviewed during the detailed design phase, in view of concerns over the environmental
acceptability of this proposal. A facility for the tapping of partially treated water for the
power station will be included in the works and would optimize treatment and distribution
costs. Computerized monitoring and control systems will be included for all the processes.
The coagulation, settlement and filtration processes will be enclosed, in view of the severe
winter temperatures: heating will also be necessary.   The SMEDRIC  design
recommendations will form the basis of the final designs now under preparation in
collaboration with Japanese OECF financing.

14.   The SMEDRIC study recommends the replacement of most of its trunk main
system on the grounds of age and undersizing. Approximately 250 km of pipes will be

added or will replace existing systems (see Table 4 above). These will be cast iron
(400 mm and less), "improved" cast iron (up to 1,600 mm) or steel (for larger diameters).
Tertiary systems will also need to be expanded and renewed where appropriate. However,
final designs and programs will be based on dynamic network modeling to optimize
operations and take into account the revised Town Plan. A leakage detection and control
program  for which Terms of Reference will be agreed during the appraisal will be
implemented and will assist in detailed network planning, pipeline replacement and
conservation of water. A more strategic approach to system improvement, over a longer
time span, targeting specific supply zones and making best use of the capacity of the
existing systems may be appropriate. Network modeling will aid strategic planning and
programming and serve to optimize the design, assess operational characteristics, optimize
performance and examine the effects of conjunctive use of the project water and
groundwater supplies. Consideration also could be given to the provision of additional
storage over the 10 percent of daily supply now proposed, as a means of flow balancing,
and reducing pipe size requirements.

15.    Program, Financial Requirements and Proposals.  TMWC intends to utilize
project water as it becomes available from 2001 and preliminary cost estimates have been
made. Works that are planned to be completed by TMWC for Phase 1 and their costs,
including 8 percent physical contingencies (as estimated by SMEDRIC), are summarized in
Table 5. TMWC propose to finance these investments through a combination of national
funds and international loans (60 percent). TMWC is negotiating with Japanese financing
agencies (OECF) to secure finance for the Huyan treatment works. Local financing will be
raised from the Municipal Planning Commission, the Municipal Construction Commission,
the Provincial Government and from additional connection and supply charges, water



- 82 -                      ANNEX 1
resource fees and surcharges on current water consumption. The Japanese finance will be
provided through their OECF private sector facility, in a similar manner to that provided
for the Changchun Industrial Water Supply Project. Onlending will be through the Bank
of China. Under this arrangement, a joint venture (J/V) will be established between
private sector Japanese and Chinese companies to design, build and operate the works.
TMWC, along with 32 local investors from the state-owned and private business
community in Taiyuan, will form the Chinese company, which has applied for its
business license. Most of the mechanical and electrical components for the treatment
works and pumping stations will be procured from Japan. The Japanese Company will
not invest directly in the project, but will contribute to the design and construction of the
works, provide technical assistance with operation and maintenance for three years after
completion of construction, and then withdraw. The J/V will thus operate the plant for
three years, and then hand over to the Chinese partner to continue.  Water sales
agreements between the three parties (YRDPC, the J/V and TMWC) will be drawn up at
the same time as the financing and concession agreements.
B. WASTEWATER
16.   The Existing Water Environment. The Fen River deteriorates significantly in
quality as it passes through Taiyuan City. There are significant levels of pollution in the
river and its tributaries as a result of the numerous discharges of untreated and partially
treated domestic sewage and industrial wastes. This situation will only be improved with
the extension and upgrading of sewerage systems, and the expansion of treatment facilities
for domestic and industrial discharges. Wastewater discharges were estimated in 1993 to be
around 220 million m3, of which around half was of industrial origin. Most of this
wastewater is discharged to the Fen River, either through sewers or open ditches and
tributaries. Seventeen major enterprises contribute over 80 percent of the industrial volume,
the largest being the steel mill, the fertilizer plant, the two power stations, the chemicals
plant and the paper mill. Water quality measurements taken at seven sampling sites on the
Fen River in May 1993 (a typical low-flow situation), indicated that levels of chemical
oxygen demand (COD), phenol, ammonia, nitrates, chromium and cyanide were well above
acceptable standards. Samples taken in tributaries of the Fen River in April 1993 showed
similar problems.
17.   Drainage Areas. There are currently eight defined drainage areas in Taiyuan, as
described below. Most have separate stormwater and sanitary sewers, but the older,
downtown area has a combined system. The drainage areas are: (a) Shang Lan, a small
area (2.5 km ) to the north of the main city mainly serving the paper mill; (b) Bei Jiao in the
northeast of the city (23 km 2), which serves the Jing Yang and Xin Yang chemical works;
(c) the steel mill industrial and housing area (26 kmi2) in the northeast of the city; (d) the
main downtown area (34 kmi2) on the east bank; (e) Nan Jiao, the largely industrial area
(23 km2) in the southeast of the city; (f) He Xi North, the west bank of the city, northern
area (21 km2); (g) He Xi Central, the west bank of the city, central area (10 km2); and
(h) Nan Yan, the area (20 km2) in the southwest of the city, mainly serving the Taiyuan
Chemical Company's factories and the No. 2 Power Plant.



- 83 -                      ANNEX 1
18.   Treatment Works. There are three municipal sewage treatment works (STWs), all
on the east side of the river: Bei Jiao in the north, Yang Jia Bao serving the central down
town area, and Ying Jia Bao in the south. In addition, there are two works serving
industrial plants and their cnvirons: Zhao Zhuang, serving Taiyuan Iron and Steel
Company on the east side of the river, and Nan Yan serving the chemical complex on the
west side. Key data are summarized in Table 6.

19.   Management and Financing of Operations. There are a number of fees for
sewerage services collected by the Water Saving Office on behalf of the Municipal
Government:  Y 0.1 0/mn3 from government institutions; Y 0.12/mi3 from industry and
commerce; and no charge for domestic connections. In addition, where an effluent from an
enterprise does not reach the standards laid down for discharge to sewer, a fine is collected
by the Monitoring Station of the Urban Engineering Management Bureau, depending on the
nature of the effluent and the damage caused. These fees are supplemented by revenue
from the sale of treated sewage effluent for reuse within industry, for which the rate was
Y 0.4/mi3 in 1995. A volume charge of Y 0.05/mi3 is also collected by the Environmental
Protection Bureau (EPB) from industrial users. EPB also imposes fines for discharges to
the environment that do not comply with the standards set. The reported operating costs for
sewage collection and treatment vary from Y 0.1 6/mn3 for a simple pumping station
discharging sewage to the river without treatment, to Y 0.22/m3 for treatment at Yang Jia
Bao and Y 0.74/m3 for treatment at Bei Jiao. Total income does not cover the costs of
providing the service, which is subsidized by the Municipal Government through an annual
budget provision, with all revenues collected being returned to Government. The budget for
1994 for operation of treatment works was around Y 9.4 million, whereas that for
maintenance of the sewerage network was Y 0.9 million.



- 84 -                      ANNEX 1
20.   The operation and maintenance of STWs is carried out by the Drainage
Management Section of the Urban Management Bureau, while the maintenance of sewers is
the responsibility of the Municipal Engineering Management Section of the Bureau, which
also has responsibility for roads, bridges, streetlights, etc. The latter organization has area-
based management units, with separate roads and sewers gangs in each. All STWs and
most sewers are designed by the Taiyuan Municipal Engineering Design Institute (TMEDI).
However, this institute only takes schemes to the detailed engineering stage: completion of
tender documents and all subsequent stages of implementation are dealt with by an
investment institute under the Planning Commission. Public lavatories and the disposal of
nightsoil are managed by the Sanitation Bureau, and the Environmental Protection Bureau
is responsible for monitoring discharges to the environment and for collecting volume fees,
pollution fees and fines. It is apparent that the organizational framework for planning,
operations, investment, fee collection and financing of sewerage services is fragmented.
Ideally, the provision of sewerage services should be integrated within a single entity,
which would have full responsibility for planning, design and financing of systems and for
the collection, treatment and disposal of sewage and industrial wastes, nightsoil and sewage
sludge. The formation of such an entity could be considered as a precursor to the formation
of a Sewerage Company, which would become financially autonomous through the
collection of user charges for wastewater collection, treatment and disposal. Control of
discharges to the environment should be effected through a discharge permit system,
which would set limits for industrial and municipal discharges and ensure these are met
through enforcement proceedings. The EPB has currently issued permits for 36 major
factories accounting for 85 percent of wastewater discharged, and has plans to expand the
system to over 100 factories. At present the permitting system does not encompass
municipal treatment plants.
21.   Plans for Expansion. An outline plan for the improvement of existing sewerage
facilities has been drawn up by TMEDI, taking into account the anticipated increases in
flows due to the project. The planning horizon assumed is 2010. The planning increases
the number of drainage areas from 8 to 15, as detailed in Tables 7 and 8, and takes account
of the new city plan. Investment needs are summarized in Table 9. It is planned to have
adequate treatment facilities for about 60 percent (700,000 m3/day) of the estimated wastes
by the year 2002 and for about 75 percent (1,170,000 m3/day) by 2010. Although the
improvements necessary to overcome existing deficiencies and to accommodate the
additional loads to be generated by the project have been identified in very broad terms,
only limited study/design work has been undertaken on these improvements and funding
has not been secured.
22.   Outline designs have been prepared for the provision of sludge digestion facilities at
Yang Jia Bao and for the He Xi Central and North treatment plant (which replace earlier
proposals for two plants). These are seen to be the priorities. In both cases, the designs
relate to the preproject capacity scenario, and will need to be revised. The Institute is also
preparing construction drawings for a proposed plant at Xi Shan on behalf of the Coal
Mining Bureau for construction later this year

23.   Financing. The current rate of expenditure on sewerage is Y 30 million per year,
compared to the need for Y 3.2 billion over the next 20 years, if the plan is to be
implemented (about Y 150 million per year). The new Provincial Governor has indicated
that expenditure on sewerage should be stepped up by Y 10 million per year, each year, but
this will still not be adequate to achieve the program indicated. The Municipality has
considered foreign finance, and a deputation went abroad to seek an environmental
protection loan last year, as it was recognized that there would not be an economic rate of
return on the investment. However, a major share of long-term financing and operational
costs could be borne by wastewater generators on the "generator pays" principle. The
proposed financing plan is summarized in Table 10, although it is envisaged that this will be
reviewed under the Environmental Waste Plan.

24.   Wastewater Management Master Plan. The Municipal STWs serving Taiyuan
are well constructed and maintained although the day-to-day management of the plants
could be improved to optimize their perforrnance. The extent of the sewerage systems is
such that large volumes of domestic and industrial sewage are discharged directly to
watercourses resulting in high levels of pollution and risk to public health. The revenue for
the services supplied is inadequate to cover the costs of the existing service, resulting in
substandard performance. Outline plans for the improvement of these services have been
identified and some design work undertaken on specific items. The Urban Master Plan for
Taiyuan area has been updated to include priority sewerage and industrial wastes collection
and treatment requirements in the near term. The Taiyuan Municipality and the proposed
TWMC will also prepare a Wastewater Management Master Plan (WMMP). Terrns of
Reference for the WMMP have been prepared and will be agreed with the Shanxi
Government during negotiations.
