57.   Resettlement. Based on the analysis of local resources, environment carrying
capacity and infrastructure conditions, resettlement strategy has been worked out for each
of the affected villages. Affected persons will be resettled within the same village as
much as possible and 813 persons will be resettled this way. Where outside resettlement
is necessary, the villages will be moved as a whole. After field investigations and close
consultation with both the resettlers and host communities, the resettlement offices have
identified 21 resettlement sites for 5,669 persons. Detailed survey and design have been
completed for each resettlement site, covering residential houses, public facilities, water
supply and sewage system, power transmission lines, broadcasting lines, road and
irrigation facilities. PAPs will be provided allowances for transportation, income loss
during transportation and subsidy for medical care.
58.   The principle of rehabilitating the affected enterprises is to restore their current
productive capacity. The design institutes worked with the affected enterprises and has
drawn up different rehabilitation schemes for each of the enterprises. The cost of
rehabilitation is fully budgeted, including compensation for lost assets, relocation cost,
compensation for profit loss and etc.  Many of the enterprises plan to take this
opportunity for technical renovation.
59.   Rehabilitation schemes have been worked out for affected infrastructure.
Relevant design institutes have estimated the replacement cost and have designed the
reconstruction of the infrastructure. The reconstruction cost is fully budgeted in the
project resettlement cost.
60.   Tianjin Hydropower Design Institute and Nei Mongol Archaeology Research
Institute conducted a survey on cultural relics and have drawn up an action plan for
protection, relocation and retrieving the relics.
61.   Livelihood Development. Livelihood rehabilitation will be conducted for 6,430
people (in the year of moving), including 5,085 people in agriculture, 1,210 people in
industries, service sector and 135 people opting for self-employment. Agricultural
development has been planned in detail for each resettlement site. All plots of land have
been identified and surveyed.  Irrigation facilities have been designed for land
development. It is planned to develop and improve 13,941 mu of low-yielding land, to
develop 5,511 mu of irrigated land, 4,686 mu of dry land and 3,017 mu of grassland.
Industrial jobs will be provided to 746 PAPs (total population is 1,210, including
children) in new enterprises or existing enterprises to be expanded. These enterprises
have been identified. Feasibility studies have been conducted for each of them and
approved by county governments.  These factories will sign contracts with the
resettlement offices for employing the PAPs and they cannot unilaterally dismiss any
PAPs. Part of the power profits will be set aside as a special fund to support PAPs in
their livelihood development.
Cost and Budget
62.   The total reservoir resettlement cost is Y 510 million and the cost breakdown is
detailed in Table 9.

Resettlement Implementation Arrangements
63.      Organization.   A  multilevel institution  has been  set up  for WJZ  resettlement
implementation.   The resettlement office  in  WJZ  Reservoir Construction  and
Management (WRCM), under the guidance of the Resettlement Office of Ministry of
Water Resources, is responsible for the WJZ resettlement implementation. Both Shanxi
and Nei Mongol have established resettlement offices at provincial, prefecture, county
and township level. The resettlement office of WRCM will operate as the supervisor of
the WJZ resettlement implementation, and will monitor resettlement progress. The
county resettlement office would undertake internal monitoring on resettlement progress.
YRCCRO will be contracted as the independent external monitor for R&R monitoring
and evaluation. The Environment and Resettlement Panel will also review the progress
of WJZ  Reservoir  resettlement progress.   Detailed  monitoring  indicators, reporting
requirements and formats have been developed and attached to the RAP.
64.      Implementation  Schedule.   Resettlement schedule  is  detailed  with  reservoir
impounding schedule. Resettlement is to be completed by the end of 1998 and livelihood
development activities will continue till 2000. A detailed CPM schedule is developed
and attached to the RAP.



-160 -                     ANNEX6
Damsite Resettlement Evaluation
65.   The construction of the dam, started in 1994, affected three villages, i.e.,
Wanjiazhai in Shanxi Province on the left bank and Yaozimaoshen, Shidaoqingshen in
the Nei Mongol Region on the right bank. The census survey was completed in early
1993. A resettlement action plan was prepared respectively for the Left Bank and Right
villages and was approved by the Ministry of Water Conservancy (MWC) in December
1993. Resettlement offices have been established in Wanjiazhai Project Construction
Corporation, the affected counties and townships to coordinate and implement land
acquisition and resettlement.
66.   Land Acquisition and Resettlement. The project acquired 7,482 mu of land,
including 5,706 mu of cultivated land affecting 664 persons. Land acquisition was
completed by 1995 and compensation was fully disbursed. Twenty-four people in 6
households who lost houses to the project have resettled in new houses. The income
rehabilitation component is 80 percent completed and will be completed by the end of
1997. The total cost for land acquisition and resettlement was estimated at Y 26.82
million and has been fully paid. The budget was later adjusted to Y 34.80 million with
increased compensation rates. An additional Y 7.98 million is to be disbursed to the
affected villages.
67.   Livelihood Development. Livelihood development is planned through on-farm
and off-farm activities. For the two villages on the right bank, the planned development
of 1,640 mu of wasteland has been completed and distributed to affected villagers, raising
their per capita land holding above preproject level.
68.   For the Wanjiazhai Village on the left bank, the arrangement is (a) to develop land
for on-farm activities for 111 PAPs, including 50 mu for vegetable, 10 mu for nursery,
100 mu for orchard, 300 mu for fruit trees, melons and corn; (b) to develop animal
husbandry for 63 PAPs (pigs, sheep, cows, chickens, fisheries); (c) to provide
employment for 30 PAPs in service industry; and (d) to provide industrial employment
for 44 PAPs (food processing plants and bean product processing). Fifty-eight old people
are on pension arrangement. R&R is well in progress and 80 percent of livelihood
development activities have been finished. They are expected to be completed by the end
of 1997.
69.   Living Standard Survey. The living standard survey conducted in the three
villages indicates a significant improvement of living standards, infrastructure and public
facilities. The sample survey shows a sharp increase in household income. Wanjiazhai
Village saw an average per capita annual income increase from Y 127 in 1990 terms to
Y 1,200 in 1995. The three villages are now enjoying greatly improved infrastructure,
such as road, telecommunication facilities, water supply system, education and health
care facilities.



-161-                      ANNEX7
ANNEX 7: PROJECT ECONOMICS
A. ECONOMIC GROWTH AND WATER DEMAND
1.    Taiyuan is Shanxi Province's capital, largest city, and principal industrial
complex. The area to be served by the WWTP Phase I includes Taiyuan City, the north
and south suburbs, and the adjacent county of Gujiao. Combined, the urban population
was 1,672,000 in 1993, and the rural population, 532,000. Taiyuan had 7.2 percent of
Shanxi's population in 1993, but produced 18.3 percent of its industrial output, including
11.2 percent of coal, 14.6 percent of electricity, 31.2 percent of chemicals, 48.0 percent of
metallurgical products, and 28.8 percent of other industrial output.
2.    Table 7.1 reports current and future population, industrial output, and water
demands by consuming sector as projected by YRDP staff and the Tianjin Design
Institute. These projections have been carefully worked out using projected population
and industrial growth rates, and water consumption "norms" used in China-wide planning
exercises. Combined, they show total water consumption increasing from 437 million m
in 1993 to 784 in 2000, 1,000 in 2010, and 1,196 in 2010, with implied compound rates
of growth of 8.7 percent between 1993 and 2000, 2.5 percent between 2000 and 2010,
and 1.8 percent after 2010.
3.    There are two significant problems with these forecasts. First, they undoubtedly
understate both growth in population and industrial output in the early years of the
forecasts. We know that the Taiyuan urban population has been growing in excess of 3
percent per year, but the projections use only a 1.2 percent rate for the entire period 1993-
2000. We also know that industrial growth has been averaging 15 percent in recent years,
while the projections assume only 8 percent from 1993 to 2000. These considerations
imply that the forecasts are far too conservative. Second, the projections ignore any
impacts of price on demand. Prices of both household and industrial water supplies have
been increased recently, and are scheduled to rise much faster in the near future.
Heretofore, water prices were so low that there was no need for a distinction between
projected "consumption" and projected "demand". But with household water prices
scheduled to triple, and industrial water prices scheduled to more than quadruple, this
distinction becomes important. For these reasons, the forecasts have been recomputed,
taking into account both the recent growth in population and industrial activity, and the
influence of the projected higher prices.  The changes will affect only the main
consuming sectors industries and households which includes municipal water
consumption. At the same time, we will produce a range of forecasts-low, medium, and
high-recognizing the inherent difficulty of making such projections in a rapidly
changing area.



-162 -                      ANNEX7
4.    The assumptions about the growth in Taiyuan's population, industrial capacity,
and water consumption are shown in the top part of Table 7.2. Those assumptions
pertaining to household and municipal (H&M) water demand are taken from projections
prepared by the Taiyuan Water Company, which rely on the most recent data available,
and contain assumptions relevant to the local economy. Combined, the assumptions
produce the forecasts for H&M demand shown in the bottom left portion of Table 7.2.
The "medium" case, which we expect will have the highest probability of proving true, is
somewhat higher than the YRDP/TDI projections given in Table 7.1. The difference is
mainly due to the higher population growth rates assumed (which more closely match
recent trends), and to the inclusion of "municipal" demands (firefighting, urban landscape
requirements, etc.) which the YRDP/TDI projections omitted.
5.    The middle part of Table 7.2 gives the assumptions pertaining to the revised
industrial water demand forecasts. Whereas YRDP/TDI assumed an 8 percent growth in
industrial activity until 2000, slowing to 5 percent and ultimately 4 percent, the Mission
feels this assumption is conservative, and may be characterized as the "low" case. The
preferred "medium" case assumes a 10 percent growth after 1995 (1993-95 averaged 18
percent), then slowing to 7.5 percent and finally 5 percent after 2010. The assumptions
about growth in the productivity of industrial water approximate those of YRDP/TDI.
The higher growth rates would have produced a higher demand forecast except for the
influence of the higher prices which have been taken into account. We have assumed a
price elasticity of -0.1, which results in, for example, the 2000 water consumption
declining from 530 million m in 2000 to only 327 because prices for industrial water are
scheduled to increase from about Y 1.0/mi3 to 5.8 (YRDP/TDI projected demand in 2000
to be 382 ignoring any price effects). The low (in absolute value) elasticity assumed is
justified for several reasons. First, Taiyuan's firms have already made great strides in
reducing consumption per unit of output in reaction to physical water shortages. Second,
even the higher prices will not significantly affect profits of most industries, as water
costs will remain a small part of total costs. Third, the assumption that there will be
continuing productivity gains with or without price changes implies that there will be
ongoing, long-term adjustments to both price and probable future shortages which the
Project will be unable to eradicate in total.
6.    The bottom part of Table 7.2 show the water demands used for the economic
analysis for H&M, Industry, and the categories combined. We assume that the rural
consumption of humans and livestock will be met by local surface water and
groundwater, and that all irrigation demands will be met from these sources plus treated
wastewater from the Project, as discussed below.

B. WATER RESOURCES
7.      Taiyuan's water resources may be classified as surface and groundwater. Surface
water resources include local runoff and diversions from the Fen Reservoir, located 50
km upstream of Taiyuan City. Local runoff is primarily usable only by agriculture,
because there are no water storage facilities of consequence other than Fen reservoir. The
reservoir directly supplies, via a dedicated pipeline, about 36.5 million m3/year to Gujiao,
an industrial/mining complex north of Taiyuan. Virtually all of the remaining supplies
are diverted to the large irrigation districts bordering the Fen River. These irrigation areas
are also suffering severe water shortages: only 80 percent of irrigable land received any
water in most years, and supplies are only half those required for optimal yields. In recent
years, runoff to the Fen has been low and apparently declining, due to extensive terracing
upstream to limit erosion, and construction of small-scale dams. Compared to long term
trends, each of the of the years 1989-93 is classified "P95" (very dry) because local
runoff has averaged less than 50 million m3 compared to a long-term  average of 127.
This water source is not considered a viable alternative to WWTP as a supply source for
Taiyuan because (a) further curtailment of water supplies to the irrigation districts would
further exacerbate poverty and unemployment, (b) there are no physical diversion
structures in place at present to supply the City, and (c) the available supplies would be
far from sufficient to alleviate Taiyuan's present and projected shortages.
8.      As  shown  in  Table  7.3, Taiyuan  relies- on  groundwater for most of its
consumption. In 1991, 83 percent of consumption by households, municipal entities, and
industries came from groundwater, with the percentage rising to 87 percent in 1993. The



-165 -                          ANNEX7
Gujiao diversions from Fen Reservoir are included in the "surface" category; without
them, the percentage from groundwater in 1993 is 97 percent. Sustainable groundwater
supplies have been estimated at 307 million m3/year for the entire Project Area, of which
35 million m3 are used outside the City for irrigation and "rural life" purposes, leaving
272 as the long term groundwater resource available. In 1993, Taiyuan's consumption
from groundwater exceeded 312 million m3, implying overextraction of 15 percent. In
fact, groundwater extraction has exceeded the sustainable level in every year since 1989,
a situation which cannot continue much longer without dire environmental consequences.

C. WATER SHORTAGES
9.     In 1993, water consumption in Taiyuan totaled 437 million m3. If the measurable
shortages in the urban and industrial sectors are included, water demand was at least 538
million m', for a deficit of 101. If there had been no overdraft of groundwater, the
shortage would have been 146 million m', or 27 percent of demand. In addition, farmers
in the project areas were getting only about one-third of the fresh water they needed (part
of their deficits were made up by using untreated sewage and industrial wastewater),
implying the true deficit was over 30 percent.
10.    Combining the household, municipal, and industry water demand projections of
section A and the sustainable local groundwater supplies available to meet these demands
we can predict the future water shortages as shown in Table 7.4. The middle part of the
Table represents the volumes which the WWTP will attempt to supply.

D. ALTERNATIVES TO THE PROJECT
11.   If economic growth in the Project Area is to continue, and the urban population is
to receive adequate supplies of water, additional sources of water must be found.
Potential sources include (a) more intense use of local water, (b) importing surface water,
and (c) reallocation of existing water from other uses (irrigation).
Local Water
12.   There is strong evidence that groundwater is already overexploited, and has been
since the late 1980s. The evidence comes from both a comparison of extractions with
estimates of sustainable yields, and from observations of declining water tables and
increasing incidence of land subsidence. The question is not whether increased extraction
of groundwater can be tolerated, but whether current levels can be sustained to the year
2000 when the Project water is scheduled to become available.
13.   Taiyuan City is situated near the upper end of the Upper Fen Basin, an enclosed
hydrological entity that relies almost entirely on snowmelt and far upper reach runoff for
the river flow. There is negligible offtake above Taiyuan City and its northern suburbs,
but virtually unlimited demand for water in and around Taiyuan and downstream for
irrigation. The utilization rate has been as high as 74 percent in recent years, which is
probably the highest in the Yellow River Basin, and cannot be increased given the
seasonality of flows and high sediment content during flood periods. This level of
utilization has resulted in the river bed often running dry much of the year, and a dramatic
decline in the irrigation supplies to downstream farmers.
Importing Surface Water
14.   The alternative sources for importing surface water are either from adjacent
subbasins within Shanxi, or from the Yellow River. Conceivably, Taiyuan could receive
water diverted from the adjacent Northwest or Hotuohe basins. Two factors make these
options virtually impossible. First, water shortages are prevalent throughout northern
Shanxi, and are intensifying everywhere. Only in the west is excess surface flows
projected, and these occur mostly in flood season drain directly to the Yellow, at points
remote from the Taiyuan water demand center. Second, the division of Shanxi into
subbasins follows the pattern of intervening mountain ranges, which would need to
crossed if water were to be diverted. These ranges are as high as 3,000 m and would
involve insurmountable technical and economic difficulties.
15.   The above discussion implies that only importation of water from the Yellow by a
project such as Wanjiazhai can meet the projected water demands. Alternatives neither
can supply the volumes required, or would pose even greater difficulties and higher costs
than Wanjiazhai. Is, however, Wanjiazhai the best location on the Yellow for the
diversions? It is probably the only location that is feasible. Above Wanjiazhai the
Yellow River turns westward, requiring longer and more costly diversion route, and one
that encroaches on Nei Mongol. Below Wanjiazhai, the Loess Plateau begins with its



-167 -                     ANNEX7
associated runoff with high silt concentrations, which would engender prohibitively high
treatment costs.
Reallocation of Existing Water
16.  In 1993 irrigation accounted for 83 million m3 (19.0 percent of total consumption)
in Taiyuan. Only about half of this was fresh water. Downstream of Taiyuan the major
irrigation districts (Fen-I and 2) receive about 200 million m3/year diverted from the Fen
Reservoir. In these areas as well as the entire Province, irrigation consumption as a
percentage of total consumption has been declining as water has been diverted to
municipal and industrial uses or sold by farmers who have water rights. However, not all
of the current irrigation supplies could be diverted to M&I uses. Irrigation demands are
seasonal, peaking in the Spring months, whereas M&I demands are relatively constant
throughout the year. The larger irrigated areas are not located near the cities, so that
measurable reallocation would require investments in both surface water storage and
conveyance. Those irrigated areas located near Taiyuan supply important and valuable
produce to the City, supplies which would be difficult and costly to procure elsewhere.
But the most important reason why reallocation from irrigation should not be pursued has
to do with poverty alleviation. Most of Shanxi's poor are rural, and most rural residents
rely on agriculture for the bulk of their incomes. Without irrigation, agriculture in Shanxi
is a precarious undertaking because of low and unpredictable rainfall. Irrigated yields are
double those of rainfed land, and could be much higher were irrigation water available in
ample supplies. Agricultural incomes are probably quadrupled when crops can be
irrigated. In 1994, farm wages were only 33 percent of wages in manufacturing. Thus,
any further reallocation of water from farmers would have serious negative income
effects on this group which is already the poorest of Shanxi. Furthermore, the volumes
involved would still be insufficient to meet the projected deficits in Taiyuan.
The "Do Nothing" Alternative
17.   With current water shortages in Taiyuan amounting to 30 percent or more of
estimated demand, and growing rapidly, and both ground and surface water supplies
declining, failure to complete the project would engender economic, social, and
environmental calamity. If Taiyuan's urban and rural residents are allocated supplies
equally YRDP's projected demands, then no fresh water would be left for agriculture, and
industrial output would fall far short of its potential:
Percentage of Demands that can be Met
Irrigation    Industry  Lost Output (Y million)
2000                0%         74%                8,166
2010                0%         43%               29,415
2020                0%         23%               58,384
The impact would be felt throughout China given the heavy reliance on northern Shanxi's
coal, heavy industry, and chemical output. Hundreds of thousands of skilled workers



-168 -                      ANNEX7
would need to be relocated, and vast quantities of mineral and fossil resources would go
unexploited. It is not surprising that Chinese planners have mentioned the possibility that
Taiyuan (and Datong as well) would need to relocated (at incalculable cost) if project
water is not forthcoming.
E. PROJECT BENEFITS
18.   Benefits arise primarily from water shortage alleviation in the industrial and urban
consumption sectors. There may be secondary health benefits, but these have not been
quantified. Farmers near the project areas would benefit from additional supplies of
treated sewage and wastewater. There would also be environmental benefits because the
additional water would halt the overexploitation of groundwater. In the following
sections we describe how the economic benefits of YRDP water are estimated for its
intended uses for urban consumption, industry, and possibly, irrigation. In general, we
will seek to obtain marginal economic values based on sound economic principles
applied to the situation pertaining in the project areas. Furthermore, we will take a
conservative approach where a choice is obvious, preferring to err on the low side of
benefit estimation.
Value of Water in Urban Consumption
19.   The use of water receiving the highest priority from local policymakers is for
3
human consumption. In 1994, urban households in Taiyuan consumed 59.24 million m3,
and rural households, 14.6, in the project area. In terms of liters per day, the urban figure
is 91.5 per capita, which is less than half the China norm of 200 for the year 2000. Even
so, the consumption figure includes items that are not strictly household consumption,
such as household-based commerce. Recent survey work shows that about half of
Taiyuan households consumed less than 40 liters/day. The per capita consumption by
rural residents amounts to 18 liters per day, while the norm for 2000 is 70. Based on
departure from China-wide norms, it is clear that both groups suffer severe shortages that
can only worsen in the absence of the YRDP.
20.   The analysis below will derive the value of water to urban consumers, and assume
that this value will also apply to the much smaller group of rural households in the project
area.
21.   A commonly used measure of the benefits from household consumption is
willingness-to-pay (WTP). If consumers state, directly or indirectly that they are willing
to pay so much for a unit of water, then that value is commonly taken as the benefit for
project analysis. Sometimes consumer surveys provide estimates of WTP, sometimes a
lower bound on WTP can be inferred from what other consumers in similar situations are
paying. In China, urban water consumers are paying Y 1.8/mi3 in some coastal cities of
size similar to Taiyuan.
22.   Alternatively, WTP can be estimated from consumer demand functions if the
required data are available. The method is illustrated in Figure 7.1.



-169 -                        ANNEX7
FIGURE 7.1: WILLINGNESS-TO-PAY
price
WTP ------
Po  ' ' '
Q0  Qd           volume/year
The point Po, Qo is observed. For Taiyuan, total "urban life" consumption in 1994 was
110.8 million m3, adjusted to 77.56 to allow for 30 percent losses in distribution. Given
the existence of shortages and intermittent supplies, we know that (Po,Qo) is a point
interior to the urban demand function, DD'. We first seek a point on DD' for the current
price of 0.7. The China norm of 200 for cities of Taiyuan's size gives an approximation,
implying that (0.7,129.5) is a point on DD' pertaining to 1994. In the figure, this is
represented as Po,Qd. Given an estimate of the price elasticity of demand, i, we can
trace out the complete demand function. For a constant elasticity function, the formula is
Q=AP'T, where A is a constant. A typical short-run value for ii is 0.2.7 Solving for A, we
can complete the demand function (A=120.6). WTP, the point on the demand function
corresponding to the current supply, is Y 9.09/m3. This high value, however, depends in
large part on the highly inelastic short-run elasticity assumed. Furthermore, WTP is valid
for only small changes in supply, not the very large changes envisaged by the Project.
23.   When changes in supply are large, the appropriate measure of benefits is
consumers plus (plus the underlying price). In the short run conditions applying to 1994,
CS is Y 4.19/m3, which, when added to the price of 0.7, yields a value Y 4.89/m3. In the
longer run, it is widely recognized that price elasticities are less inelastic because
consumers have time to adjust habits and replace water-using appliances. In general,
long run elasticities are taken to be twice (in absolute value) of short run, which, other
things equal, results in lower values for both WTP and CS.

population in the Taiyuan Project Area, which is assumed to continue to grow at 3

nonprice factors: 0.2 is within the range of many estimates from developed countries.

percent until 2000, 2 percent for the next decade, and 1 percent thereafter. Column (3) is

25.    In 2001, the year in which benefits are to begin accruing, the value in terms of
WTP is Y 4.13/m3, while by the CS measure, it is Y 2.41/m3.  Again, WTP would be
valid for a small increase in supply. But the Project will result in a doubling of
consumption over what would be possible without it, so we must take the more
conservative measure of CS. In other words, WTP effectively assumes a perfectly elastic

demand function, which does not introduce a large error for small changes in Q, but
certainly does so when the changes are large.
26.   The consumers' surplus values are adopted for the measure of benefits from
increased urban consumption brought about by YRDP, and will be applied to the rural
consumers as well. From Y 2.41/m3 in 2001, they rise continuously over time as
population growth causes a rightward shift in the demand function.

of the cost of Project water. The above analysis shows that this price is between the WTP
pertaining in 2001 (4.13) and the CS for that year (2.41). CS will reach the administered
price about the year 2010.

The Value of Industrial Consumption
28.   Of all water-consuming sectors, only industry has the potential to generate
sufficiently high economic returns to justify the Project. A rudimentary procedure,
summarized in Table 7.6, indicates that the returns might be in the neighborhood of
Y 34/m3. In this table, we simply take the average, weighted by projected water use by
industrial sector, of value added per unit of water. (The average return will be equal to
marginal return up to some point because of the existence of present and future water
shortages leading to underutilized capacity.)

29.    Apart from  shadow-pricing, to which we will return, the main reason why this
method is likely to be erroneous has to do with the interconnectedness of industries.
More than half of industrial output in Taiyuan supplies intermediate demand, i.e., is an
input to other firns' production. Consider the coal industry. For every yuan of output,
Taiyuan's coal firms buy Y 0.53 of inputs from the other sectors, which must use water in
their production of these inputs. The true marginal return to water in the coal sector
cannot be as high as Y 47, because all other sectors supplying inputs have lower returns,
and it is impossible to increase the output of coal without increasing the output of the
others.
30.    A  convenient method of analyzing the industrial sector is through the use of
Input-Output (1-0) models for the following reasons:
*  1-0 models provide a consistent accounting framework
*  1-0 models explicitly recognize the interconnectedness of most economic
activity
*  1-0 models can easily be linked with more rigorous analytical techniques such
as linear programming
Input-Output modeling has a rich background in China. It proved highly useful in central
planning, and continues to be used to produce economic forecasts.8 A significant boon to
this study is the existence of both a Shanxi I-0 model and models for all of China which
have recently been projected up to 2020. The Shanxi model was originally developed for
1987 conditions, then updated to 1990. It consists of 28 sectors, and includes important
8 Described in Chinese Economic Planning and Input-Output Analysis, Karen R. Polenske and Chen
Xikang, eds., Oxford, 1991.



-173 -                       ANNEX7
detail on water demands.9 The China-wide models were developed for a World Bank-
sponsored study on greenhouse gas emissions, contain 18 sectors, and are available for
1990 plus projected versions to 2000, 2010, and 2020. Together, these models permit us
to develop a projected Shanxi model aggregated to the level of detail appropriate for the
analysis of the project.
31.   In its basic form, an 1-0 table of n sectors consists of a transactions matrix T of n
rows and n columns, where each element tij is the purchases of sector i from sector j in the
measured year. The sum of any column of T is the total purchased inputs of that sector,
and the sum of any row is the total intermediate products of the sector represented in that
row. The basic table is completed by noting that the total output of each sector may be
divided into that part which meets other industries' inputs, and that component which is
final demand. In matrix notation,
£jTjj + Yj = Xi
where X is total output and Y is final demand. In a regional 1-0 table, Y is typically
expressed as Y* + E - M where Y* is local final demand, E are exports from the region,
and M are imports to the region. In this analysis we need not be concerned with regional
trade flows, so the collapsed form will be used. Thus a negative Y1 implies that M i >E j,
(the region is a net importer of the sector's product). Another most useful identity is that
the sum of a given sectors' purchases, plus the payments to its factors of production, is
also equal to total output:
Xj T1J + Vj = Xj
where Vj is the sum of factor payments, or value added.
32.   The most common use of an 1-0 table is to predict the changes in the total output
and value added of an economy's sectors when an exogenous change such as an increase
in final demand occurs. Therefore, Tii is normalized by dividing each transaction of each
firm by the sum of transactions of that firm:
Aii = Tii /Ej Tii
Then, AX + Y = X, X(I-A) = Y, and X = (I-A)-'Y so that total output may be expressed
as a function of final demand. Note that the system of equations is closed and fully
interdependent. Any change in any parameter of one sector will affect all other sectors.
In particular, a change in the final demand of say, the agricultural sector, will influence
all other sectors from which agriculture purchases inputs and/or services.
9 The model was prepared by Chen Xikang for the 1994 Project Identification Mission. His reports are
available in project files.



-174 -                               ANNEX7
33.    The full 28-sector Shanxi model contains far more detail than can be used by this
study: sectoral output and water demand has only been projected up to 2020 for six
sectors: agriculture, coal, electricity, chemicals, metallurgy, and "other" for the Project
Area; Shanxi-wide projections output has only been projected to 2000, and for these six
sectors as well. Thus the 28-sector model was first aggregated to these six sectors. Table
7.7 shows the aggregated 1990 transaction table.
34.    The Tij elements appear in the top six rows of numbers and reflect the purchases
of the sectors heading the columns from the sectors listed in the rows. For example, the
agriculture sector purchased Y 2,137 million from "itself," 41 million from the coal
sector, etc. The relative size of these elements is an indication of the interconnectedness
of the industrial system. For example, agriculture is highly dependent on the chemical
sector; the coal sector on power, chemicals, and metallurgy; and power on coal.
35.    The column "Inter(mediate) Demand" is the sum  of the row elements and is total
sales to other firms for use as inputs. Final Demand is the difference between Total
Output and Intermediate Demand, and comprises final demand by Shanxi consumers,
(plus) exports from the Province, (less) imports to the Province, and changes in stocks
over the year. From these data, it can be seen that only in the metallurgy sector is Shanxi
a net importer.

36.    The next few  rows report the components of value added-payments to the

inputs equals sectoral output according to the input-output identity.



-175 -                       ANNEX7
37.   Several modifications to the 1-0 table are required before it can be used for
analysis of the project. First, it must be extrapolated to the future. Second, it must be
shadow-priced. And third, it must be converted to the level of Taiyuan. Extrapolation is
necessary because the underlying coefficients change with technical progress and changes
in the orientation of industrial activity. Fortunately, this work has been done for China-
wide 1-0 tables, and we can adopt those changes to the Shanxi model. Table 7.8 tracks
the sequence of calculations involved.
38.   The procedure for obtaining the Table for 2000 is as follows: (1) compute the Ajj
matrices for the China-wide models for the years 1990 and 2000; (b) calculate the rates of
change of the coefficients as ratios; and (c) apply the ratios to the 1990 Shanxi table. The
calculations are shown in top parts of Table 7.8. The top block is the 1990 aggregated Aii
matrix described above. The second block is the ratio of 2000 coefficients to 1990
coefficients from the China-wide matrices. A departure from unity implies that changes
in interindustry relations is projected. For example, most sectors are projected to have
lower requirements for agricultural and coal inputs, and most will place a greater reliance
on "other" sectors which includes electronics, transport and communications. Projected
technical progress can be noted from the sum of the input coefficients: if these fall (as
they do in all but agriculture and coal), then the same output can be obtained in the future
with less total inputs.
39.   The projected changes we have introduced to the Shanxi Aii 1-0 matrix are
expected to have two types of effects: (1) changes in relative intersectoral demands for
inputs, and (2) evidence of an overall decline in intermediate demands relative to total
demands, i.e., technical progress in production. We now check for these effects by
applying projections in total output to the projected A1j matrices described above.
40.   The fourth block of Table 7.8 corrects for the most significant price distortions
which occur in the electricity sector. The shadow price of electricity is 35 fen/kWh
compared to an average financial price of 13.33 in the 1990 data. Therefore, the output of
the sector ELECT was increased by 35/13.33, and the value added and final demand
figures adjusted as well to maintain the identities. In addition, the ELECT row of the A1i
was adjusted upward to reflect the higher costs, and the value added of all firms
purchasing electricity reduced by a corresponding amount. The final block of Table 7.8
applies the total output projections for Taiyuan in 2000 to obtain a 2000, shadow-priced
transactions table for Taiyuan.

Input-Output and Linear Programming
41.   Despite its statistical and accounting merits, an 1-0 table cannot tell us what the
marginal value of water is to any given sector. But a simple transformation of the basic
1-0 structure into a linear programming model can.
42.   Recall that the general form of a linear programming (LP) model is
maximize Ej cjx;
subject to X- A 1j xj = bi for all j, and xj 2 0
where the xi are activity (variable) levels, cj are objective function coefficients, A is a
matrix of technical coefficients, and bi are resources available. The equality constraints
may be changed to inequalities of either direction through the implicit introduction of
slack activities.
43.   An equivalent LP model of an I-0 model is
maximize L:j vjO;
subject to Zj (I-A)1jOj = Dj where Oj are levels of sectoral output, vj are ratios of value
added to output, I is an identity matrix, Ai3 is the normalized 1-0 transactions matrix
described earlier, and Dj are final demands. Because an 1-0 model is fully closed, one
has a choice among which variables to constrain, and which to allow to vary. Either OQ or
Dj may be allowed to vary, and the model solved for the remaining set. In this example,
we first assume that the system is demand constrained, and set upper bounds on the Dj
equal to the 1990 observed levels of net final demand, and permit to Oj to vary. In
addition, we also add a set of water constraints
ij w Oi < W
where wj is the amount of fresh water required per unit of output, and Wj is amount of the
water allocated to sector j. All data required for the LP model are available from Tables
7.7 and 7.8 above. A solution to the LP yields shadow prices on the W constraints which
are the derivatives of the objective function (total economic value added) with respect to
a small increase in the volume of water supplied-indeed the definition of marginal value
added.
44.   The results of these experiments, performed for each sector (except agriculture)
produce the following values:
COAL               78.9 (yuan/m3)
ELECT              43.7
CHEM               29.6
METAL              45.8
OTHER              34.5
weighted average    41.9



-178 -                       ANNEX7
Note that the weighted average value is substantially less than the 67 obtained by the
simple method earlier. Again, these values represent the impact, on value added, of an
additional unit of water focused on the listed sectors. We say focused, because all of the
increase in final output permitted by the experiment may come only from the sector
selected. Some of the additional water allocated must go to each of the other sectors, but
only to the extent that it is required to permit the increase in final demand for the selected
sector. The numbers reflect 1995 price levels with the exception of the shadow pricing
of electricity and fertilizer described earlier.
45.   First, benefits in the early years are much higher than in later years due to
discounting. Second, values for years later than 2000 become progressively lower as will
be seen in the next section.
46.   An implicit assumption in using the LP model to generate the marginal values of
water is that water is the constraint (apart from the technological relationships built into
the input-output matrix). That is, if water is available, then output could increase and
generate the values described above. In other words, there are no other constraints such
as labor or industrial capacity which could prevent these results from occurring. Clearly,
labor is not, and is not likely to become a constraint in Shanxi. Industrial capacity is not
now a constraint, as evidenced by unutilized capacity, and the projections of capacity to
2000 and beyond suggests that it will be even greater in the future. Once the existing
capacity is fully utilized with YRDP water and new capacity comes on stream, the above
values will overstate the true benefits. This is because economic theory tells us that firms
will select technology so as to equate the marginal return of an input to its expected long-
term price. If labor is expected to be very expensive in the long term, then firms will
choose capital-intensive technologies to reduce the labor cost. Likewise, firms will
choose water-using technologies based on their perception of the long-term cost of water.
3
We assume the long-term cost of water to be Y 5.8/mi. This means that, given a
sufficiently long period of time, the marginal returns of water will tend toward this value.
We further assume that plant and equipment life is 30 years, so that new plant coming
onstream in 2000 will be based on the Y 5.8 assumption.
47.   T   calculation of "old" industrial capacity, that subject to the high marginal
returns as we have estimated, is shown in Table 7.9 based on the assumptions outlined
above. The phased marginal values, based on this projection of "old" capacity, is given
in Table 7.11 for "new" (the long run water price) values of 5 and half that level. The
table shows that the average value will steadily decline and reach 5 by the year 2030
when all existing plant and equipment have been retired.

48.   Note that the Y 5 figure is not crucial to the final economic appraisal: in the early
years after 2000, most plant is of the existing variety and still subject to the high returns.
In the later years when the average marginal return declines, the benefits are heavily
discounted in the rate-of-return calculations. Furthermore, even halving the assumed
value of 5 produces an average return, for example, for Taiyuan, only 14 percent lower in
2010.
Irrigation
49.   Agricultural activity in the Project Area may be divided into (a) urban "truck
gardens" producing mainly fresh vegetables for the city, and (b) field crops produced in
the surrounding areas. In addition, there is a large irrigable area (c) south of Taiyuan
stretching all the way to the Yellow River.
50.   With the possible exception of (a), project water will be too expensive to be used
directly for irrigation. YRDP has included the demand of truck gardens in their water
demand projections, but we have separated them. They amount to 108 million m3 in



-181-                      ANNEX7
2000, 120 million m3 in 2010 and 132 in 2020. Because of the proximity to Taiyuan of
this production, and the fact that high-valued crops predominate, we assume that this
demand will receive precedenze in allocations of any available after M&I demands are
met. Such water may come from excess supplies during "wet" years, or from treated and
reused wastewater. Marginal economic returns are assumed to be Y 4/m3. Under the
category (b) suburban irrigation, there are 300,000 mu within the Project Area. YRDP
demands assume an irrigation norm of 550 m3/mu and distribution losses of 40 percent.
The demands decline over time under the assumption that losses will be reduced and
efficiency of application will increase.
51.   Downstream of the Project Area lie 3.8 million mu of irrigable in the Fen Upper-
Middle Region, and 2.9 million mu in the Lower Fen Region. Projections using the
Provincial Planning Model indicate that water shortages under P75 conditions will be 43
percent in the Upper Fen and 34 percent in the Lower Pen by 2000, and growing
thereafter. Effectively, then, water demands in these areas are infinite with respect to
potential water deliveries from the Project.
52.   The Provincial Agricultural Bureau has projected cropping patterns for these areas
shown in Table 7.11, and provided farm survey materials used to produce crop budgets
for the main irrigated crops (crop budgets appended to this Annex). These patterns and
budgets were used to estimate the marginal returns to water shown in the right-hand
column of Table 7.10. Returns are higher in (b) than in (c) because the mix of crops is
more heavily weighted toward fruits and vegetables, and because distribution losses are
lower.
F. THE PROJECT
Water Deliveries and Allocations
53.   The combined economic benefits from the WWTP will ultimately depend on how
much water is diverted from Wanjiazhai to the Project Area, how much is left after
transmission losses, and how much is allocated to each sector. A variety of factors
determines how much water will be diverted: pipeline capacity, water allocations,
demand, and capacity of treating and distributing the Project water. The complete
WWTP has been allocated 1,200 million m3 of Yellow River water, a volume which
holds Shanxi province to the limits of consumption established by the SPC. Of this total,
640 million m3 has been allocated to the Taiyuan Project Area by Provincial Authorities,
with the remainder reserved for Phase II. Diversion works have been designed to handle
this volume. Diverted water must be treated for sediment and bacteria, and this will be
done at Huyon Treatment Plant to be constructed for that purpose. Huyon's capacity will
be designed to accommodate the expected volume of diversions, although  the
"lumpiness" of the works implies that there may be occasional bottlenecks in the early
years of construction and operation.
54.   Table 7.12 summarizes expected diversions, treatment, and distribution of Project
to the primary beneficiaries. Column (1) is demand net of local groundwater in the



-182 -                                ANNEX7
medium case as described in Section (A). Column (2) is the volume of diversions at
Wanjiazhai required to meet that net demand, taking into account losses in distribution
and treatment, the 640 maximum diversion limit, and the scheduled capacity of Huyon
[column (3)]. Losses in distribution are estimated at 7 percent, and Huyon is expected to
lose 9 percent, mainly from evaporation from the sedimentation tanks. The 640 limit is
not reached until 2010. Huyon capacity will be a constraint on the system in 2002 and
2006 if the demand projections and other assumption hold, but only by small margins.
Column (4) gives the feasible inflow to Huyon, and column (5) the outflow after losses in
treatment. Columns (6) and (7) allocate treated water to industries, households, and
municipal demands in the same proportions as the projected demands. These are the
volumes upon which the primary benefits from the Project are obtained. Column (7) is
the total supply from local groundwater and Project water net of all losses, and column
(8) is the remaining water deficit, if any, again according to the medium demand
projections.

Water Treatment Program
57.    At present, only 19 percent of wastewater in Taiyuan receives primary treatment.
The Project will ensure that this proportion eventually reaches 100 percent, and that the
treated water is recycled to industry and agriculture. Table 7.13 shows the planned
volumes by type of treatment. Part of the wastewater receiving secondary treatment may
be resold to industry for reuse. Plans for doing so have yet to be completed; therefore we
assume that all treated water will be made available to irrigation, first serving the
"suburban" areas, and then the downstream areas. The right part of Table 7.13 shows the
volumes which may be diverted to these irrigation uses, deriving the benefits described in
the previous sections.

Environment Comp.           -       -       -       -     1.5     4.0    4.8      0.2     0.1    10.6
Total Baseline Costs   35.1    49.0    59.4    58.0   109.6   211.4   193.1   158.8   107.5   981.9
Physical Contingencies    5.3     7.2     8.7    8.5    14.0    28.7    26.1    22.7    15.4   136.5

Total Project Costs    58.1    53.6    70.2    73.6   138.9   273.0   254.7   215.9   149.9  1,287.8
60.    In addition, there are several types of variable economic costs associated with
diverting and supplying water, shown in the bottom part of Table 7.14. First, there are
operating costs which are applied to all water diverted and include power costs of
pumping, system management costs, and maintenance. Operating costs are estimated to
be Y 1.22/mr3 in 2002, declining to a long-range value of 1.04 due to economies of scale.
Second, Yellow River water will require desiltation and additional treatment at the Huyon
plant to be constructed as part of the project.  These treatment costs begin at Y 0.83/mr3
in 2002 and decline to Y 0.46/m3, and are applied to that part of water not lost in
transmission. The electricity component of the operating and treatment costs are shadow-
priced at Y 0.35/kWh. Finally, there are opportunity costs of Yellow River water which
reflect benefits forgone by downstream hydropower installations and irrigators. These
were estimated using the Yellow River Basin Model, and are Y 0.6/mr3 in 1996 prices.
G. ECONOMIC PERFORMANCE OF THE PROJECT
61.    The economic performance of the Project is estimated with an economic cash
flow model based on the construction cost schedule given above, the benefits described in
section E, and the following assumptions about allocation policies:



-185 -                      ANNEX7
(a)   WWTP water will be directly supplied to households, municipal and
industrial demands only, in proportion to the demands of these sectors
(approximately 30 percent to H&M, and 70 percent to industries).
(b)   Eighty percent of water supplies will be returned for treatment and reuse
by irrigators. Treatment by type will follow the schedule given above in
Section F, and the water first delivered to irrigation demands within the
project area (so-called truck gardens) up to the limit of estimated annual
demand of 108 million m3. The remainder of treated wastewater will be
released into the Fen River for use by downstream farmers.
62.   The basic cash flow model is shown in Table 7.15 up to the year 2020, after which
most of the entries are constant throughout the life of the project.
63.   Based on the assumptions contained in these cost and benefit streams, the
"medium" growth and demand projections, and a 50-year project life, the EIRR of the
project, including wastewater treatment and reuse, is estimated to be 22 percent. It's net
present value discounted at 12 percent (to 1996) is Y 10,221 million. Increased H&M
consumption generates 7 percent of these benefits, industrial consumption, 83 percent,
and reuse in irrigation, 10 percent. These returns exclude any difficult-to-measure
environmental benefits from the elimination of groundwater mining, and from the
removal of health risks associated with inadequate urban water supply.
64.   When the costs of constructing and operating the treatment plants and water
retention ponds are removed, and the benefits from the use of the treated water in
irrigation are excluded, the rate of return is 20.4 percent (Table 7.16). This option is
clearly not preferable because treated wastewater benefits are distributed to a much wider
group of people, including the poorest farmers.
H. PROJECT RtSKS
65.   The major physical risks involve construction mishaps and delays, and seismic
events which could destroy the tunnels and connecting works. Construction risks are
deemed to be within manageable limits given close supervision by foreign and Chinese
experts. Nevertheless, a 1O percent cost increase has been included in the base case cash
flow model. The probability of an earthquake of a sufficient magnitude (7.0 Richter
scale) to damage the works is estimated to be less than 0.01 percent over the construction
period. As shown in Table 7.16, a 50 percent increase in construction costs (after 1996)
would still yield a rate of return of 17.4 percent, and a two-year delay in obtaining any
benefits would still produce a 17.3 percent return.

66.    The Project is also  robust with  respect to  the estimates of losses in  the
transmission system  and costs of operating it. Doubling the losses reduces the rate of
return from  22.0 to 20.7 percent, while doubling the operating costs reduces the rate of
return from 22.0 to 20.4 percent.
67.    Because the benefits from  increased water consumption by industries constitute
such a large proportion of total benefits, the estimates of these returns are critical to the
Project's performance. The analysis used a combination of input-output analysis and a
linear programming model to arrive at a more conservative value for industrial water than
more straightforward methods. The value was estimated to be about Y 31/in3 in 2002,
declining to a long-run value of 5.8-the eventual price of water charged to industries. If
these returns are reduced by 25 percent, the project still yields a rate of return of 17.5
percent.
68.    An additional concern is that future flows in the Yellow River may be insufficient
to the supply the Project's diversions. A simulation over the 56 years of available
hydrologic data showed that sufficient water will be available in all months and all years
if upstream diversions are held to currently agreed provincial allocation limits. However,
demands for Yellow River are insatiable, and failure to abide by the limits on the part of
provinces upstream from Wanjiazhai could jeopardize the project if flows are sharply
curtailed.
69.    The Project will ensure that all wastewater will be treated and most of it will be
made available to farmers. Without this component, the rate of return falls marginally to
20.6 percent, implying that this strategy is economically attractive, as well as providing
significant environmental and income distributional impacts.



-188 -                        ANNEX7
H. IMPACTS ON BENEFICIARIES
70.   The Project's direct beneficiaries are urban households, and workers in factories
and mines.  Farmers will benefit indirectly.  Urban (household plus municipal)
consumption will increase from 90 million m3 (147 l/c/d) to 165 million m3 (225 l/c/d) in
2002, bringing it in line with Chinese norms. After 2010, when the Project Area again
begins to experience water shortages, per capita consumption will begin to decline
moderately, but still remain at acceptable levels throughout the Project's time horizon.
71.   The most dramatic impacts affect industrial workers.  Without the additional
water from the Project, employment and incomes in the area will almost certainly begin
to decline in the near future as population growth continues to demand more household
water (given precedence by policymakers), and local water supplies continue to diminish.
In Table 7.17, the "without" project projections assume a 3 percent annual growth in the
productivity of labor, and a 2 percent annual growth in real labor income. By 2002,
120,000 jobs will be lost to the water shortage, with the number increasing thereafter.
With the Project water, employment and income growth can continue. In 2002, this
means an increase of 98 percent in both employment and incomes over the without
Project scenario. By 2010, the differences become very large.
TABLE 7.17 INDUSTRIAL EMPLOYMENT AND INCOME EFFECTS
Employment (000)   I   Labor Income (Y million)
Without       With         Without        With       % Increase
1994         657            -          2,869            -            -
2002          536         1,062         2,742        5,433           98
2010          452         1,495         2,708        8,962          231
2020         305          1,812         2,227       13,241          495
72.   In 1995, there were 41,930 farm households in the Project Area, holding 154,770
people with a per capita income of Y 1,443 ($175). These people will benefit in two
ways which are difficult to quantify. First, they will have full use of their local surface
and groundwater resources, part of which has been diverted to urban and industrial uses
in recent years. Second, those within the City who operate truck gardens will have first
call on the treated wastewater. The volumes they require are estimated to be 108 million
m3/year. Heretofore, they have had to rely on sporadic rainfall and untreated wastewater.
73.   Farm families in the counties downstream of Taiyuan will get the remainder of the
treated wastewater, projected to be 74 million m3 in 2002, rising to 326 million m3 after
2020. There about 66,700 farm households in the counties adjacent to the Fen, with a
population of 239,000. Their per capita income in 1995 was only Y 1,317 ($158). If we
assume that all of the increased production translates directly into increased income (the
primary effect of additional water is additional yield, requiring little more expenditure
than farm labor), then the Project will increase the incomes of these people by 23 percent
in 2002, 45 percent in 2005, and ultimately will almost double it.

ANNEX 8: ASSUMPTIONS TO FINANCIAL PROJECTIONS
A. YRDPB
Pricing Methodology
1.     Water tariffs delivered by YRDPC to TMWC are projected by following steps:
a) obtaining annual capital requirements;
b) determining financing plans;
c) calculating total project cost by appropriate cost allocations (cost sharing);
d) forming necessary operational and financial assumptions;
e) setting up water tariffs based on a minimum  debt coverage ratio of 1.2 or a
minimum rate of return on fixed assets in service of 2 percent, whichever
gives a greater tariff, to make YRDPC financially sustainable and to provide
assurance of cost recovery and debt repayment.
2.     A summary table for above (a) to (c) is listed below.
Items                   Phase I (1993-2001)             Phase 11 (2002-2010)
Project Scope           Completion of GMT, SMT, and
connection work
Nonproject Scope        Wanjiazhai Dam                  Completion of NMT, Haoshuigou
Reservoir, more units for SMT power
stations
Capital Needs prior to IDC Y 13.55 billion              Y 10.8 billion
Total Capital Requirements Y 14.07 billion              Y 10.8 billion
Financing Plan          WB loan, Cofinancing Loan, Invest- YRDPC Internal Cash Generation, GSP
ment Income, GSP Surcharge,    Surcharges
Incremental Water Revenue from
Tariff Adjustments or Additional
Surcharges
